sub-bifield if S = S1 ∪ S2 and S1 is a subfield of F1 and S2 is a subfield of F2. If the 
bifield has no proper sub-bifield then we call F a prime bifield. 
 
Example 1.6.5: Let R = Q ∪ Z7, clearly R is a prime bifield.

Now having seen the definition of bifield we now define fuzzy bifield. 
 

 
83
DEFINITION 1.6.5: Let (F = F1 ∪ F2 , +, y) be a bifield. µ : F → [0, 1] is said to be a 
fuzzy sub-bifield of the bifield F if there exists two fuzzy subsets µ1 (of F1 ) and µ2 (of 
F2 ) such that  
 
i. 
(µ1 , +, y) is a fuzzy sub-field of (F1 , +, y). 
ii. 
(µ2 , +, y) is a fuzzy sub-field of (F2 , +, y).  
iii. 
µ = µ1 ∪ µ2. 
 
DEFINITION 1.6.6: Let (F = F1 ∪ F2 , +, y) be a bifield and (S = S1 ∪ S2 , +, y) be a 
sub-bifield of F. Let B (F/S) denote the set of all fuzzy sub-bifields; A = A1 ∪ A2 of F, 
such that A1≠ ⊇ S1 and A2≠ ⊇ S2. We say A ∈ B (F/S) is finite bivalued if the image of 
A1 is finite and the image of A2 is finite where A = A1 ∪ A2 is a fuzzy sub-bifield. 
 
THEOREM 1.6.17: Let (F = F1 ∪ F2 , +, y) be a finite bifield; then every fuzzy sub-
bifield of F is finite bivalued. 
 
Proof: Follows from the definition.  
 
In view of this we have the following results. 
 
THEOREM 1.6.18: Suppose that (F = F1 ∪ F2 , +, y) has finite characteristic bifield 
(i.e. characteristic of the field F1 is a prime p1 > 0 and the characteristic of the field 
F2 is a prime p2 > 0). Then F is finite if and only if every fuzzy sub-bifield A of F is 
finite valued. 
 
Proof: Left for the reader to prove, following the steps of finite fuzzy subfield. 
 
THEOREM 1.6.19:  
 
i. Let A be a fuzzy subset of the bifield F and s, t ∈ Im (A) the image of A 
where A = A1 ∪ A2, then s ≤ t if and only if As ⊇ At and s = t if and only if 
As = At . (that is  if (A1 ) s ⊃  (A1 ) t  and (A2 ) s  ⊇ (A2 ) t  then only we say As 
⊇ At , similarly for As = At ). 
 
ii. If A is a fuzzy sub-bifield of F (A = A1 ∪ A2 and F = F1 ∪ F2) then for all x 
∈ F = F1 ∪ F2, x ≠ 0 we have Ai(x) ≥ Ai(1) ≥ Ai(x) = Ai (–x) = Ai(x–1) for i 
= 1, 2.  
 
Proof: Direct as in case of fuzzy fields. 
 
THEOREM 1.6.20: Let A = A1 ∪ A2 be a fuzzy subset of F = F1 ∪ F2 . If At = (A1)t ∪ 
(A2)t is a sub-bifield of F for all t ∈ Im(A)I then A is a fuzzy sub-bifield of the bifield F.  
 
Conversely if A = A1 ∪ A2 is a fuzzy sub-bifield of F = F1 ∪ F2 then for all t such that 
0 ≤ t ≤ Ai (1), i = 1, 2; At is a sub-bifield of F. If A is a fuzzy sub-bifield of F, then At is 
called a level sub-bifield of F where 0 < t < A(1).

and A(x) = m for all x ∈ F\K; where 0 ≤ m ≤ r. Then A is a fuzzy sub-bifield of F. 
 
Proof: Follows as in case of subfield.  
 
Thus all results regarding fuzzy sub-bifields can be defined and obtained in an 
analogous way as in case of fuzzy subfields. These concepts will be once again used 
in case of fuzzy bivector spaces. 
 
 
1.7 Fuzzy Semirings and their generalizations 
 
In this section we introduce the notion of fuzzy semirings and fuzzy sub-birings. The 
study of fuzzy k-ideals in semirings started in 1985 [31] followed by several authors. 
Even [19] has studied about it. But the notion of fuzzy bisemirings in literature is 
totally absent. This concept is defined only in this book. Just for the sake of 
completeness we start to give the definition of fuzzy semirings and proceed on. 
 
An algebra (S, +, y) is said to be a semiring if (S, +) is a semigroup with identity 0 and 
(S, y) is a semigroup, satisfying the following conditions 
 
i. a y (b + c) = a y b + a y c 
ii. (b + c) y a = b y a + b y a 
 
for all a, b, c ∈ S. 

 
85
 
A semiring S may have the identity 1 defined by 1 y a = a y 1 = a and a zero 0 defined 
by 0 + a = a + 0 = a for all a ∈ S and 0 y a = a y 0 = 0. We say the semiring (S, +, y) is 
abelian if a y b = b y a for all a, b ∈ S. We say the semiring S is strict if a + b = 0 
forces a = 0 and b = 0. Let (S, +, y) be a semiring if a y b = 0 in S implies a = 0 or b = 
0 for a, b ∈ S \ {0} then we say the semiring (S, +, y) is a semidivision ring.  
 
If (S, +, y) is commutative semiring with no zero divisions which is strict then we say 
(S, +, y) is a semifield. 
 
Example 1.7.1: Let Z+ ∪ {0} = S. (S, +, y) is a semifield (Z+, set of positive integers). 
 
Example 1.7.2: Let

Example 1.7.4: Let C11 be the chain lattice with 11 elements, C11 is a finite semiring 
in fact a finite semifield.  
 
We denote the order of the semiring S by |S| or o(S), if |S| = ∞ we say the semiring is 
of infinite order otherwise S is of finite order if |S| < ∞. 
 
Now we proceed on to define left (right) ideal in a semiring. Finally we define left k-
ideal of the semiring S. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.1: Let R be a semiring. A non-empty subset I of R is said to be left 
(resp, right) ideal if x, y ∈ I and r ∈ I imply x + y ∈ I and r x ∈ I (resp. x r ∈ I). If I is 
both left and right ideal if R, we say I is a two sided ideal or simply ideal of R. A left 
ideal I of a semiring R is said to be a left k-ideal if a ∈ I and x ∈ R and if a + x ∈ I or 
x + a ∈ I then x ∈ I. Right k-ideal is defined dually and two-sided k-ideal or simply a 
k-ideal is both a left and a right k-ideal. A mapping f : R → S is said to be a 
homomorphism if f (x + y) = f(x) + f(y) and f(xy) = f (x) f (y) for all x, y ∈ R. We note 
that if f : R → S is an onto homomorphism and I is left (resp. right) ideal of R, then 
f(I) is a left (resp. right) ideal of S. 
 
Now we proceed on to recall the definition of fuzzy ideal of a semiring. 
 

 
86
DEFINITION 1.7.2: A fuzzy subset µ of a semiring R is said to be a fuzzy left (resp. 
right) ideal of R if µ(x + y) ≥ min {µ(x), µ(y)} and µ(xy) ≥ µ(y) (resp. µ(xy) ≥ µ(x)) for 
all x, y ∈ R. µ is a fuzzy ideal of R if it is both a fuzzy left and a fuzzy right ideal of R. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.3: A fuzzy ideal µ of a semiring R is said to be a fuzzy k-ideal of R if 
µ(x) ≥ min {max {µ (x + y), µ (y + x)}, µ (y)}}  for all x, y ∈ R. 
 
It is left for the reader to prove the following theorem: 
 
THEOREM 1.7.1: Every fuzzy ideal of a semiring is a fuzzy k-ideal. 
 
THEOREM 1.7.2: Let I be a non-empty subset of a semiring R and λI the characteristic 
function of I. Then I is a k-ideal of R if and only if λI is a fuzzy k-ideal of R. 
 
Proof: The reader is advised to give the proof as an exercise. 
 
THEOREM 1.7.3: A fuzzy subset µ of R is a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal of R if and 
only if for any t ∈ [0, 1] such that µ t  ≠  φ, µ t  is a left (resp. right) k-ideal of R, where 
µ t = {x ∈ R µ(x) ≥ t}, which is called a level subset of µ. 
 
Proof: For proof please refer [52]. 
 
Note that if µ is a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal of R then the set Rµ = {x ∈ R | µ(x) ≥ 
µ(0)} is a left (resp. right) k-ideal of R. 
 
THEOREM 1.7.4: Let I be any left (resp. right) k-ideal of R. Then there exists a fuzzy 
left (resp. right) k-ideal µ of R such that µ t = I for some t ∈ [0, 1]. 
 
Proof: If we define a fuzzy subset of R by

Since I and R itself are left (resp. right) k-ideals of R, it follows that every non-empty

Let µ and δ be fuzzy subsets of the semiring R. We denote that µ ⊆ δ if and only if 
µ(x) ≤ δ(x) for all x ∈ R and µ ⊂ δ if and only if µ ⊆ δ and µ ≠ δ. 
 

 
87
THEOREM 1.7.5: Let µ be a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal of R. Then two level left 
(resp. right) k-ideals µs , µt (with s < t in [0, 1] ) of µ are equal if and only if there is 
no x ∈ R such that s ≤ µ(x) < t.  
 
Proof: Suppose s < t in [0, 1] and µ s = µ t. If there exists an x ∈ R such that s ≤ µ(x) < 
t, then µ t is a proper subset of µ s, a contradiction. Conversely, suppose that there is 
no x ∈ R such that s ≤ µ(x) < t. Note that s < t implies µ t ⊆ µ s. If x ∈ µs, then µ(x) ≥ s 
and so µ(x) ≥ t because µ(x) </  t. Hence x ∈ µ t and µ s = µ t. This completes the proof. 
 
For more about these concepts please refer [52]. Now for a given fuzzy k-ideal µ of a 
semiring R we denote by Im (µ) the image set of µ. 
 
THEOREM 1.7.6: Let µ be a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal of R. If Im(µ) = {t1 , t2 , …,

THEOREM 1.7.7: An onto homomorphic pre image of a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal 
is a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal. 
 
Proof: Let f : R → S be an onto homomorphism. Let δ be a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-
ideal on S and let µ be the pre image of δ under f. Then it was proved that µ is a fuzzy 
left (resp. right) ideal of R. 
 
For any x, y ∈ δ we have  
 
µ(x)  =

proving that µ is a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal of R.

THEOREM 1.7.9: Let f : R → S be an onto homomorphism and let µ be a fuzzy left 
(resp. right) k-ideal of R. Then the homomorphic image f(µ) of µ under f is a fuzzy left 
(resp. right) k-ideal of S. 
 
Proof: The proof can be got as a matter of routine; hence left for the reader to prove. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.4: A left (resp.right) k-ideal I of R is said to be characteristic, if f(I) = 
I for all f ∈ Aut (R), where Aut (R) is the set of all automorphisms of R. A fuzzy left 
(resp. right) k-ideal µ of R is said to be  fuzzy characteristic if µ(f(x)) = µ(x) for all     
x ∈ R and f ∈ Aut ( R). 
 
THEOREM 1.7.10: Let µ be a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal of R and let f : R → R be 
an onto homomorphism. Then the mapping µ f: R → [0, 1] defined by µ f(x) = µ(f(x)) 
for all x ∈ R is a fuzzy left (resp. right ) k-ideal of R. 
 
Proof: It is a matter of routine once we write µf(x) = µ (f(x)) ≥ min {max {µ (f(x) + 
f(y)), µ (f(y) + f(x))}, µ(f(y))}. 
 
The simplifications are direct and simple and hence left for the reader to prove. 
 
The following theorems are also straightforward hence stated without proof; so that 
the interested reader can do them. 
 
THEOREM 1.7.11: If µ is fuzzy characteristic left (resp. right) k-ideal of R , then each 
level left (resp.right) k-ideal of µ is characteristic. 
 
THEOREM 1.7.12: Let µ be a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal of R and let x ∈ R. Then 
µ(x) = t if and only if x ∈ µ t  and x ∉ µ s   for all s > t. 
 
We recall the proof of the following theorem. 
 
THEOREM 1.7.13: Let µ be a fuzzy left (resp. right) k-ideal of R. If each level left 
(resp right) k-ideal of µ is a characteristic then µ is fuzzy characteristic. 
 
Proof: Let x ∈ R and f ∈ Aut (R ). If µ (x) = t ∈ [0, 1], then by the just above theorem 
we have x ∈ µt and x ∉ µs for all s > t. 
 
Since each level left (resp right) k-ideal of µ is characteristic, f (x) ∈ f (µ t) = µ t. 
Assume µ (f(x)) = s > t. Then f(x) ∈ µs = f(µs). Since f is one to one it follows that x 
∈µs, a contradiction. Hence µ (f(x)) = t = µ (x) showing that µ is fuzzy characteristic . 
 
For more about fuzzy semiring properties refer [50, 51, 52, 54]. 
 

 
89
Now we proceed on to define fuzzy notions on bisemirings. As bisemirings 
themselves are very new much so is the concept of fuzzy bisemiring. Just we recall 
the definitions of them. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.5: Let (S, +, y) be a non-empty set with two binary operations ‘+’ 
and ‘y’. We call (S, +, y) a bisemiring if  
 
i. 
S = S1 ∪ S2 where both S1 and S2 are distinct subsets of S,  S1 ⊄ S2 
and S2 ⊄ S1. 
ii. 
(S1, +, y) is a semiring. 
iii. 
(S2, +, y) is a semiring. 
 
Example 1.7.5: S = Z0 ∪ C2 where C2 is a chain lattice of order 2 and Z0 is a 
semiring, thus S is a bisemiring. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.6: Let (S, +, y) be a bisemiring. If the number of elements in S is 
finite we call S a finite bisemiring. If the number of elements in S is infinite we say S is 
an infinite bisemiring. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.7: Let (S, +, y) be a bisemiring. We say S is a commutative 
bisemiring if both the semirings S1 and S2 are commutative otherwise we say the 
bisemiring S is a non-commutative bisemiring. Let (S, +, y) be a bisemiring. We say S 
is a strict bisemiring if both S1 and S2 are strict bisemirings where S = S1 ∪ S2. Let (S, 
+, y) be a bisemiring if 0 ≠ x ∈ S be a zero divisor if there exists a y ≠ 0 ∈ S such that 
x y y = 0. We say a bisemiring S has a unit 1 in S if a y 1 = 1 y a = a for all a ∈ S. Let 
(S, +, y) is a bisemiring we say S is a bisemifield if S1 is a semifield and S2 is a 
semifield where S = S1 ∪ S2. If both S1 and S2 are non-commutative semirings with no 
zero divisors then we call S = S1 ∪ S2 to be a bisemidivision ring. It is to be noted that 
even if one of S1 or S2 is non-commutative with no zero divisors but other is a 
semifield still S is a bisemidivision ring. 
 
Example 1.7.6: (S, +, y) is a bisemiring. S = S1 ∪ S2 where S1 = C7 and S2 is the 
lattice given by  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
then S is only a bisemiring which is not a semifield. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.8: Let (S = S1 ∪ S2, +, y) be a bisemiring which is commutative and 
has unit element. The polynomial bisemiring is denoted by S[x] = S1[x] ∪ S2[x] where 
S1[x] and S2[x] are polynomial semirings.

with si ∈ Si, pi si and si pi ∈ Pi ; i = 1, 2.

DEFINITION 1.7.11: (S = S1 ∪ S2 , +, y) is a bisemiring. We say µ : S → [0, 1] is a 
fuzzy bisemiring, µ = µ1 ∪ µ2 where both µ1 : S1 → [0, 1] and µ2 : S2 → [0, 1] are 
fuzzy semirings. 
 
Now we proceed on to define fuzzy bi-ideals in a bisemiring. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.12: A fuzzy subset µ of a bisemiring (S = S1 ∪ S2 , +, y) is said to be a 
fuzzy left (resp. right) bi-ideal of S if µ : S→ [0, 1] such that µ(x + y) ≥ min {µ(x) + 
µ(y)}, and µ (xy) > µ (y) (resp. µ (xy) > µ (x)) for all x, y ∈ S. µ is a fuzzy bi-ideal of S 
if it is both a fuzzy left and fuzzy right bi-ideal of S = S1 ∪ S2. 
 
µ1 : S1→ [0, 1] and 
µ2 : S2→ [0, 1] where µ1 and µ2 are fuzzy ideals of the semirings S1 and S2. 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.13: A fuzzy ideal µ of a bisemiring (S, +, y) is said to be a fuzzy k-
ideal of S if µ (x) ≥ min {{max µ(x + y), µ(y + x)}, µ(y)} for all x, y ∈ S. i.e. µ (x) ≥ 
min {max µ1 (x + y), µ1(y + x)}, µ1(y)} ∪ min{max µ2(x + y), µ2(y + x)}, µ2(y)} for all 
x, y ∈ S1 (x, y ∈ S2 ) where S = S1 ∪ S2. If S is additively commutative then the 
condition reduces to µ(x) ≥ min{µ (x + y), µ(y)} for all x, y ∈ S; where µ(x) = µ1(x) ∪ 
µ2(x) ≥ min {µ1(y + x)}, µ1(y)} ∪ min {µ2(x + y), µ2(y)} for all x, y ∈ S1 (x, y ∈ S2 ). 
 
DEFINITION 1.7.14: Let I = I1 ∪ I2 be a non-empty subset of a bisemiring S = S1 ∪ S2

Any matrix A in Mn (F) will be called a fuzzy matrix we may call Mn (F) a fuzzy 
matrix semiring. Several results can be had in this direction.

Further the fuzzy semirings can be utilized in constraint handling rules. For more

other research papers. 
 
 
1.8 Fuzzy near-rings and their properties 
 
In this section we recall the definition of fuzzy near-ring, give some new types of 
fuzzy near-rings and introduce the notion of fuzzy bi near-rings. Also notions like 
Fuzzy near-ring module and fuzzy congruence of a near-ring module are recalled in 
this section. For more about fuzzy near-ring literature please refer [28, 38, 56, 57, 71, 
122, 130].  
 
DEFINITION [71]: Let R be a near-ring and N a fuzzy set in R. Then N is called a fuzzy 
near-ring in R if 
 
i. 
N (x + y) ≥ min {N(x), N(y)}. 
ii. 
N (–x) ≥ N (x). 
iii. 
N (xy) ≥ min {N(x), N(y)} for all x, y in R. 
 
DEFINITION [71]: Let R be a near-ring and N a fuzzy near-ring in R. Let Y be a near-
ring module over R and M a fuzzy set in Y. Then M is called a fuzzy near-ring module 
in Y if  
 
i. 
M (x + y) ≥ min {M(x), M(y)}. 
ii. 
M (λx) ≥ min { N ( λ), M(x)} for all x, y ∈ Y and λ ∈ R. 
iii. 
M(0) = 1. 
 
If N is an ordinary near-ring then condition (ii) in the above definition is replaced by  
 
ii(a).    M(λx) > M(x) for all λ ∈ N and for all x ∈ Y. 
 
THEOREM [71]: Let Y be a near-ring module over a fuzzy near-ring N in R. Then M is 
a fuzzy near-ring module in Y if and only if M ( λ(x) + µ(x)) ≥ min {min {N (λ), M(x)}, 
min {N(µ), M(y)}} for all λ, µ ∈ N and for all x, y ∈ Y. 
 
If N is an ordinary near-ring then the above condition is replaced by M (λx + µ y) ≥ 
min{{M (x), N(y)} for all x, y ∈ Y. 
 
Proof: Left for the reader as an exercise as it can be got directly by the definitions. 
 
THEOREM [71]: Let Y be a near-ring module over a near-ring R with identity. If M is 
a fuzzy near-ring module in Y and if λ ∈ R is invertible then M(λx) = M(x) for all       
x ∈ Y. 
 
Proof: If λ ∈ M is invertible then we have for all x ∈ Y. M(x) = M (λ–1 λx) ≥ M(λx) 
≥ M(x) and so M(λx) = M(x). 
 

 
95

We say that a fuzzy set A in M has the sup property if for any subset T of M there 
exists to ∈ T such that

THEOREM [71]: Let Y and W be near-ring modules over a fuzzy near-ring N in a

Throughout the discussion from now on by a near-ring R we mean a system with two 
binary operations addition and multiplication such that 
 
i. 
The elements of R form a group under ‘+’. 
ii. 
The elements of R form a semigroup under multiplication 'y'. 
iii. 
x y (y + z) = x y y + x y z for all x, y, z ∈ R. 
 
An R-module that is near-ring module is a system consisting of an additive group M, 
a near-ring R, and a mapping (m, r) → mr of M × R into M such that  
 
i. 
m (x + y) = mx + my for all m ∈ M and for all x, y ∈ R. 
ii. 
m(xy) = (mx)y for all m ∈ M and for all x, y ∈ R. 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.1: An R-homomorphism f of an R-module M into an R-module M' is a 
mapping from M to M' such that (m1 + m2 ) f = m1 f + m2 f and ( mf ) r = (mr) f for all 
m, m1 , m2 ∈ M and for all r ∈ R . 
 
The submodules of an R-module M are defined to be kernels of R-homomorphism. 
 
THEOREM [22]: An additive normal subgroup B of an R-module M is a submodule if 
and only if, (m + b)r – mr ∈ B for all m ∈ M, b ∈ B and r ∈ R . 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.2: A relation ρ on an R-module M is called a congruence on M if it is 
an equivalence relation on M such that (a, b) ∈ ρ and (c, d) ∈ ρ imply that (a + c, b + 
d) ∈ ρ and (ar, br) ∈ ρ for all a, b, c, d in M and for all r in R . 
 
DEFINITION [98]: A non empty fuzzy subset µ of an additive group G is called a fuzzy 
normal subgroup of G if 
 
i. 
µ (x + y) ≥ min {µ (x) , µ(y)} for all x, y in G. 
ii. 
µ (–x) = µ(x) for all x ∈ G. 
iii. 
µ (y + x – y) = µ(x) for all x, y in G. 

 
97
 
Let µ be a fuzzy normal subgroup of an additive group G and x ∈ G. Then the fuzzy 
subset x + µ of G defined by (x + µ) (x) = µ (y – x) for all y in G is called the fuzzy 
coset of µ . 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.3: Let µ be a non-empty fuzzy subset of an R-module M. Then µ is 
said to be a fuzzy submodule of M if 
 
i. µ is a fuzzy normal subgroup of M and 
ii. µ {(x + y) r – x r} ≥ µ (y) for all x, y in M and for all r in R . 
 
The proof of the following two theorems are omitted as they can be easily obtained by 
the reader using direct methods. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.1: Let B be a non-empty subset of an R-module M. Then the 
characteristic function χg is a fuzzy submodule of M if and only if B is a submodule of 
M. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.2: Let µ be a fuzzy submodule of an R-module M. Then the level subset 
µt = {x ∈ M | µ (x) ≥ t}, t ∈ Im µ is a submodule of M. 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.4: Let µ be a fuzzy submodule of an R-module M. Then the 
submodules µI’s are called level submodules of M. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.3: For a non-empty fuzzy subset µ of an R-module M, the following 
assertions are equivalent 
 
i. 
µ is a fuzzy submodule of M. 
ii. 
The level subsets µt, t ∈ Im µ are submodules of M. 
 
Proof: It is a simple matter of routine. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.4: If µ is a fuzzy normal subgroup of an additive group G. Then x + µ 
= y + µ if and only if µ(x – y) = µ(0) for all x, y in G. 
 
Proof: Left for the reader as an exercise. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.5: Let µ be a fuzzy submodule of an R-module M. Then the set M / µ of 
all fuzzy cosets of µ is an R-module with respect to the operations defined by (x + µ) 
+ (y + µ) = (x + y) + µ and (x + µ) r = xr + µr for all x, y in M and for all r in R. If f 
is a mapping from M to M/ µ, defined by xf = x + µ for all x ∈ M then f is an R-
epimorphism with ker f = { x ∈ Mµ(x) = µ (0)}. 
 
Proof: Please refer [28, 39]. 
 
DEFINITION [28]: The R-module M / µ is called the quotient R-module of M over its 
fuzzy submodule µ.

THEOREM 1.8.9: Let α be a fuzzy congruences on an R-module M and µα be the fuzzy 
submodule induced by α. Let t ∈ Im α. Then (µ α ) t = {x ∈ M x ≡ 0 (α t ) } is the 
submodule induced by the congruence α t . 
 

 
100
Proof: Let a ∈ M. Now a ∈(µα)t if and only if (µα) (a) ≥ t if and only if α(a, 0) ≥ if 
and only if (a, 0) ∈ αt if and only if α ∈ 0(αt) if and only if α ∈ {x ∈ M x ≡ 0(αt)}. 
Hence the theorem. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.10: Let µ be a fuzzy submodule of an R-module M and αµ be the fuzzy 
congruence induced by µ . Let t ∈ Im µ . Then (αµ )t is the congruence on M induced 
by µt. 
 
Proof: Let β be the congruence on the R-module M induced by µt . (x, y) ∈ β if and 
only if x – y ∈ µ t.  Let  (x, y)  ∈  (α µ ) t. Therefore (α µ ) (x, y) ≥ t ⇒ µ (x – y) ≥ t ⇒ 
(x – y) ∈ µ t ⇒ (x, y) ∈ β. Thus (α µ) t ⊆ β. By reversing the above argument we get β 
⊆ (α µ ). Hence (α µ) t = β. 
 
DEFINITION [28]: Let M be an R-module and α be a fuzzy congruence on M. A fuzzy 
congruence β on M is said to be α-invariant if α (x, y) = α (u,ν) implies that β (x, y) 
= β (u,ν) for all (x, y),(u, ν) ∈ M × M.

THEOREM 1.8.11: Let M be an R-module and µ be a fuzzy submodule of M. Let α be 
the fuzzy congruence on M induced by µ . Let t = sup Im α then M/ µ ≅ 
t
/
M
α  . 
 
Proof: We define a mapping θ: M/µ→M/αt by (x +y) θ = x αt where x αt denotes the 
congruence class containing x of the congruence αt, x + µ = y + µ ⇒ µ(x – y) = µ(0) 
= α(x, y) = sup Im α = t ⇒ (x, y) ∈ αt ⇒ xαt = yαt ⇒ (x + µ) θ = (y + µ)θ. So θ is 
well defined.  
 
Now (x + µ + y + µ) θ = (x + y + µ) θ = (x + y) αt = x αt + y αt = (x + µ)θ + (y + µ) θ 
and ((x + µ) r) θ = (x r + µ) θ = (x r) αt = (x αt )r = ((x + µ) θ) r. 
 
Therefore θ is an R-homomorphism. Again (x + µ) θ = (y + µ) θ ⇒ xαt = y αt = (x, y) 
∈ αt ⇒ α (x, y) = t ⇒ µ (x – y) = t ⇒ µ (x – y) = α(0, 0) = µ(0) which implies that    
x + µ = y + µ. So θ is injective. Obviously θ is surjective. Hence the result. 
 
DEFINITION [56]: Let R be a near-ring and let µ be a fuzzy set in R. We say that µ is a 
fuzzy subnear-ring of R if for all x, y ∈ R. 
 
i. µ (x –y) ≥ min {µ(x), µ(y)}. 
ii. µ(xy) ≥ min {µ(x), µ(y)}. 
 
If a fuzzy set µ in a near-ring R satisfies the property µ(x – y) ≥ min {µ(x), µ(y)} then 
letting x = y; µ(0) > µ (x) for all x ∈ R. 
 
DEFINITION [1]: Let (R, +,y) be a near-ring. A fuzzy set µ in R is called a fuzzy right 
(resp. left) R-subgroup of R if 
 
i. µ is a fuzzy subgroup of (R ,+). 
ii. µ(xr) ≥ µ (x) (resp. µ(rx) ≥ µ (x)) for all r, x ∈ R .

THEOREM [56]: If µ is a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of a near-ring R, then the 
set Rµ = {x ∈ Rµ (x) = µ (θ)} is a right (resp. left) R-subgroup of R. 
 
Proof: Let x, y ∈ Rµ . Then µ(x) = µ(y) = µ(0). Since µ is a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-
subgroup, it follows that  
 
µ(x – y)  
≥  
min{µ(x) , µ(y)} 
=  
min{µ(0), µ(0)} 
  
 
 
 
=  
µ(0). 
 
On the other hand µ(x – y) ≤ µ(0). Hence we have µ(x – y) = µ(0) so x – y ∈ Rµ Also 
for any x ∈ Rµ and r ∈ R, we get µ (xr) ≥ µ(x) = µ(0) (resp. µ(rx) ≥ µ(x) = µ(0)). 
 
On the other hand µ(xr) ≤ µ(0) (resp. µ(rx) ≤ µ(0). Hence we obtain µ(xr) = µ(0) (resp 
µ(rx) = µ(0)), which shows that x r ∈ Rµ (resp. r x ∈ Rµ) Consequently the set Rµ is a 
right (resp.left) R-subgroup of R. 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.6: A fuzzy right (resp.left) R-subgroup µ of a near-ring R is said to be 
normal if there exists x ∈ R such that µ(x) = 1. 
 
Note that if a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup µ of a near-ring R is normal then 
µ(0) = 1; hence µ is a normal fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of a near-ring R if 
and only if µ(0) = 1. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.12: Let µ be a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of a near-ring R and 
let µ+ be a fuzzy set in R defined by µ+(x) = µ(x) + 1 – µ(0) for all x ∈ R. Then µ+ is a 
normal fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of R containing µ.

THEOREM 1.8.13: If µ is a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of R satisfying µ+(x) = 0

THEOREM [1]: Let (R, +, y) be a near-ring and χH be the characteristic function of a 
subset H ⊂ R. Then H is a right (resp. left) R-subgroup of R if and only if χH is a fuzzy 
right (resp. left) R-subgroup of R. 
 
Proof: Refer [1]. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.14: For any right (resp. left) R-subgroup H of a near-ring R, the 
characteristic function χH of H is a normal fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of R 
and 
H
Rχ = H. 
 
Proof: From the above theorem the proof of this theorem can be easily obtained. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.15: Let µ and ν be fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroups of a near-ring R. 
If µ ⊂ ν and µ(0) = ν(0) then Rµ ⊂ Rν . 
 
Proof: Assume that µ ⊂ ν and µ(0) = ν(0). If x ∈ Rµ then ν(x) ≥ µ(x) = µ(0) = ν(0). 
Noting that ν(x) ≤ ν(0) for all x ∈ R we have ν(x) = ν(0) that is x ∈ R. Hence the 
proof. 
 
The following result is a direct consequence of the above theorem; hence left for the 
reader. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.16: If µ and ν are normal fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroups of a 
near-ring R satisfying µ ⊂ ν then Rµ ⊂ Rν . 
 
THEOREM 1.8.17: A fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup µ of a near-ring R is normal if 
and only if µ+ = µ . 
 
Proof: Sufficiency is direct. To prove the necessary condition assume µ is a normal 
fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of R and let x ∈ R. Then µ+(x) = µ(x) + 1 – µ(0) = 
µ(x) and hence µ+ = µ. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.18: If µ is a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of a near-ring then 
(µ+)+ = µ+. 
 
Proof: For any x ∈ R, we have (µ+)+(x) = µ+(x) + 1 – µ+(0) = µ+(x) completing the 
proof.  

 
104
 
The following result is direct by the above definition. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.19: If µ is a normal fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of a near-ring 
R, then (µ+)+ = µ. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.20: Let µ be a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of near-ring R. If 
there exists a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup ν of R satisfying ν+ ⊂ µ then µ is 
normal. 
 
Proof: Suppose there exists a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup ν of R such that ν+ ⊂ 
µ. Then 1 = ν+ (0) ≤ µ (0) whence µ(0) = 1. Hence the proof. 
 
Using the above theorem the following result is straightforward. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.21: Let µ be a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of a near-ring R. If 
there exists a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup ν of R satisfying ν+ ⊂ µ then µ+ = µ. 
 
THEOREM 1.8.22: Let µ be a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of a near-ring R and 
let f: [0, µ (0)] → [0,1] be an increasing function. Define a fuzzy set µf: R → [0,1] by 
µf (x) = f (µ(x)) for all x ∈ R. Then µf is a fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroup of R. In 
particular if f(µ(0)) = 1 then µf is normal and if f(t) ≥ t for all t ∈ [0, µ(0)] then µ ⊆ 
µf. 
 
Proof: Let x, y ∈ R. Then µf (x – y) = f(µ(x – y)) ≥ f(min µ(x), µ(y)) = min {f(µ(x)), 
f(µ(y))} = min{µf (x), µf(y)} and for all x, r ∈ R, we have µf (xr) = f (µ (xr)) ≥ f (µ(x)) 
= µf (x). Similarly µf (rx) = f(µ(rx)) ≥ f (µ(x)) = µf (x). Hence µf is a fuzzy right (resp. 
left) R-subgroup of R. If f (µ(0)) = 1 then clearly µf is normal. Assume that f(t) ≥ t for 
all t ∈ [0, µ(0)]. Then µf (x) = f (µ(x)) ≥ µ(x) for all x∈R, which proves that µ ⊆ µf.

of R , which is maximal in the poset of normal fuzzy right (resp. left) R-subgroups 
under set inclusion. Then µ takes only the values 0 and 1. 
 
Proof: Please refer [56].

Now we proceed on to define the notion of fuzzy near-rings.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.7: Let µ be a non-empty fuzzy subset of a near-ring N (that is µ(x) ≠ 0 
for some x ∈ N) then µ is said to be a fuzzy ideal of N if it satisfies the following 
conditions:  
 
i. µ (x + y) ≥ min {µ (x), µ (y)}.  
ii. µ (–x) = µ (x). 
iii. µ (x) = µ (y + x – y). 
iv. µ (xy) ≥ µ (x) and  
v. µ {x (y + i) – xy } ≥ µ (i) for all x, y, i ∈ N.  
 
1. If µ is a fuzzy ideal of N then µ (x + y) = µ (y + x).  
2. If µ is a fuzzy ideal of N then µ (0) ≥ µ (x) for all x ∈ N. The above two statements 
can be easily verified for if we put z = x + y, then µ (x + y) = µ (z) = µ (– x + z + 
x) since µ is a fuzzy ideal µ (– x + x + y + x) = µ (y + x) (since z = x + y).  
 
Likewise for the second statement µ (0) = µ (x – x) ≥ min {µ (x), µ (-x)} = µ(x)  since 
µ is a fuzzy ideal (since µ (–x) = µ (x) by the very definition of fuzzy ideal).

THEOREM [130]: Let N be a near-ring and λI, the characteristic function on a subset I 
of N. Then λI is a fuzzy ideal of N if and only if I is an ideal of N.  
 
DEFINITION [130]: Let µ be a fuzzy subset of X. Then the set µt of all t ∈ [0, 1] is 
defined by µt = {x ∈ N / µ (x) ≥ t} is called the level subset of t for the near-ring N.  
 
DEFINITION [130]: Let N be a near-ring and µ be a fuzzy ideal of N. Then the level 
subset µt of N for all t ∈ [0, t], t ≤ µ(0) is an ideal of N if and only if µ is a fuzzy ideal 
of N.  
 
DEFINITION [130]: A fuzzy ideal µ of N is called fuzzy prime if for any two fuzzy 
ideals σ and θ of N σ oθ ⊆ µ implies σ ⊆ µ or θ ⊆ µ.  
 
Now we use the concept of fuzziness in Γ-near-rings as given by [1, 130].  
 
THEOREM [130]: If µ is a fuzzy ideal of a near-ring N and a ∈ N then µ (x) ≥ µ(a) for 
all x ∈ 〈a〉.  

 
107
 
Proof: Please refer [1, 130]. 
 
DEFINITION [130]: Let µ and σ be two fuzzy subsets of M. Then the product of fuzzy 
subset ( σ o τ)(x) = sup {min (σ (y), τ (z))} if x is expressible as a product x = yz 
where y, z ∈ M and (σ o τ) (x) = 0 otherwise.  
 
DEFINITION [130]: A fuzzy ideal µ of N is said to have fuzzy IFP if µ (a n b) ≥ µ(ab) 
for all a, b, n ∈ N.  
 
DEFINITION [130]: Let µ be a fuzzy ideal of N, µ has fuzzy IFP if and only if µk is a 
IFP ideal of N for all 0 ≤ k ≤ 1.  
 
DEFINITION [130]: N has strong IFP if and only if every fuzzy ideal of N has fuzzy 
IFP.  
 
THEOREM [130]: If µ is a fuzzy IFP-ideal of N then Nµ = {x ∈ N / µ (x) = µ(0)} is an 
IFP ideal of N.  
 
Proof: We have µ (0) ≥ µ (x) for all x ∈ N; write t = µ (0). Now Nµ = µt and by 
definitions µt has IFP. Therefore Nµ is an IFP ideal of N.

108

THEOREM 1.8.28: Let µ be a fuzzy subset of M. Then the level subsets µt = {x ∈ M / 
µ(x) ≥ t}, t ∈ Im µ, are ideals of M if and only if µ is a fuzzy ideal of M.  
 
All results true in case of fuzzy ideals of N are true in case of fuzzy ideals of M with 
some minor modifications.  
 
The following result can be proved by routine application of definitions.  
 
THEOREM 1.8.29: Let M and M' be two Γ-near-rings, h : M → M' be an Γ-
epimorphism and µ, σ be fuzzy ideals of M and M' respectively then  
 
i. h (h –1(σ)) = σ. 
ii. h–1(h (µ)) ⊇ µ and  
iii. h–1(h (µ)) = µ if µ is constant on ker h.  
 
DEFINITION [130]: A fuzzy ideal µ of M is said to be a fuzzy prime ideal of M if µ is 
not a constant function; and for any two fuzzy ideals σ and Γ of M, σ o Γ ⊆ µ,  
implies either σ ⊂ µ or Γ ⊂ µ.  
 
Using these definitions it can be proved.  
 
THEOREM [130]: If µ is a fuzzy prime ideal of M then Mµ = {x ∈ M / µ (x) = µ(0)} is 
a prime ideal of M.

where aij.bij = aij for all aij ∈ A and bij ∈ B. Clearly (Pn×n, ☼) is a semigroup. Thus (A

THEOREM 1.8.30: The fuzzy near- matrix ring is a commutative near-ring.  
 
Proof: Straightforward.  
 
THEOREM 1.8.31: The fuzzy matrix near-ring is not an abelian near-ring.  
 
Proof: For A, B ∈ Pn×n we have A ☼ B ≠ B ☼ A in general.  
 
THEOREM 1.8.32: In {Pn×n, ⊕, ☼} we have In×n ≠ A where In×n is the matrix with 
diagonal elements 1 and rest 0.  
 
Proof: Left for the reader to prove.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.10: Let {Pn×n, ⊕, ☼} be a fuzzy near matrix ring we say a subset I of 
Pnxn is a fuzzy left ideal of Pnxn if  
 
i. 
(I, +) is a normal subgroup of Pnxn.  
ii. 
n (n' + i) + nr n' ∈ I for each i ∈ I and nr, n, n' ∈ N where nr 
denotes the unique right inverse of n.  
 
All properties enjoyed by near-rings can be defined and will be true with appropriate 
modifications.  
 
Next we proceed on to define the concept of fuzzy complex near-rings.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.11: Let V = {a + ib / a, b ∈ [0,1]} define on V the operation called 
addition denoted by ⊕ as follows:  
 
For a + ib, a1 + ib1 ∈ V, a + ib ⊕ a1 + ib1 = a + a1 + i (b + b1) where a ⊕ a1 = a + 
a1 if a + a1 < 1 and a + a1 = a + a1 – 1 if a + a1 ≥ 1 where '+' is the usual addition of 
numbers. Clearly (V, ⊕) is a group. Define ☼ on V by (a + ib) ☼ (a1 + ib1) = a + ib 
for all a + ib, a1 + ib1 ∈ V. (V, ☼) is a semigroup. It is easily verified. (V, ⊕, ☼) is a 
near-ring, which we call as the fuzzy complex near-ring.  

 
111
 
Further P = {a / a ∈ [0,1]} and C = {ib / b ∈ [0 1]} are fuzzy complex subnear-rings 
of (V, ⊕, ☼).  
 
PROPOSITION 1.8.1: V has non-trivial idempotent.  
 
Proof: Left for the reader to prove.  
 
THEOREM 1.8.33: Let {V, ⊕, ☼} be a fuzzy complex near-ring. Every nontrivial fuzzy 
subgroup of N is a fuzzy right ideal of V.  
 
Proof: Obvious by the fact that if N is a fuzzy subgroup of V then NV ⊆ N.  
 
It is an open question. Does V have nontrivial fuzzy left ideals and ideals? The reader 
is requested to develop new and analogous notions and definitions about these 
concepts.  
 
Now a natural question would be can we have the concept of fuzzy non-associative 
complex near-ring; to this end we define a fuzzy non-associative complex near-ring.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.12: Let W = {a + ib / a, b ∈ [0, 1] called the set of fuzzy complex 
numbers}. Define on W two binary operations ⊕ and ☼ as follows: 
  
(W, ⊕) is a commutative loop where for a + ib,  c + id ∈ W define a + ib ⊕  c + id = 
a ~ c + i ( b ~ d) where ‘~’ is the difference between a and b. Clearly (W, ⊕) is a 
commutative loop.  
 
Define ☼ on W by (a + ib) ☼ (c + id) = a + ib for all a + ib, c + id ∈ W. (W, ⊕, ☼) 
is called the fuzzy complex non-associative near-ring. ([0, 1], ⊕, ☼) ⊆ (W, ⊕, ☼) is a 
fuzzy non-associative subnear-ring.  
 
Obtain interesting properties about these non-associative fuzzy complex near-rings.

DEFINITION 1.8.20: Let {W, ⊕, ☼} be the fuzzy non-associative complex near-ring. 
Let x be an indeterminate. We define the fuzzy non-associative polynomial near-ring

W [x] = {Σ pi xi / pi ∈ W}; we say p(x), q(x) ∈ W[x] are equal if and only if every 
coefficient of same power of x is equal i.e. if p(x) = p0 + p1x + … + pnxn and q(x) = q0 
+ q1x + … + qnxn. p(x) = q(x) if and only if pi = qi. for i = 1, 2, …, n. Addition is 
performed as follows p(x) ⊕ q(x) = p0 ⊕ q0 + … + (pn ⊕ qn) xn where ⊕ is the 
operation on W. For p(x), q(x) in W[x] define p(x) ☼ q(x) = p(x). Clearly {W(x), ⊕, 
☼} is a fuzzy non-associative complex polynomial near-ring.

Now we proceed on to define a special class of fuzzy near-ring.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.21: Let P = [0, 1] the interval from 0 to 1. Define ⊕ and ☼ on P as 
follows. For a, b ∈ P define a ⊕ b = a + b if a + b < 1, a ⊕ b = 0 if a + b = 1 and a 
⊕ b = a + b –1 if a + b > 1. Thus ⊕ acts as modulo 1. Define ☼ on a, b ∈ P = [0, 1] 
by a ☼ b = a; clearly (a ⊕ b) ☼ c = a☼ c + b☼ c = a ⊕ b. Clearly (P, ⊕) is a group 
and (P, ☼) is a semigroup. Hence (P, ⊕, ☼) is a right near-ring. We call {P, ⊕, ☼} 
the special fuzzy right near-ring.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.22: Let (P, ⊕, ☼) be a fuzzy near-ring. P0 = {p ∈ P / p.0 = 0} is 
called the fuzzy zero symmetric part and Pc = {n ∈ P / n.0 = n} is called the fuzzy 
constant part.   
 
THEOREM 1.8.34: The special fuzzy right near-ring {P, ⊕, ☼} has no fuzzy invertible 
elements.  
 
Proof: Left for the reader to prove.  
 
Let S = {r/p, 0 / 1 < r < p} is a fuzzy subnear-ring or to be more specific if S = {0, ¼, 
½, ¾}; S is a fuzzy subnear-ring.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.23: A fuzzy subnear-ring N of P is called fuzzy invariant if NP ⊆ N 
and PN ⊆ N we call a fuzzy subnear-ring N of P to be a fuzzy right invariant if NP ⊂ 
N.  
 
The following theorem is left as an exercise to the reader.  
 
THEOREM 1.8.35: Every fuzzy subnear-ring N of P is fuzzy right invariant.  
 

 
115
DEFINITION 1.8.24: The set P = {0, 1} with two binary operations ⊕ and ☼ is called 
fuzzy right seminear-ring if {P, ⊕} and {P, ☼} are right seminear-ring.  
 
All results can be easily extended in case of fuzzy seminear-ring.  
 
Example 1.8.3: Let {P, ⊕, ☼} be the fuzzy seminear-ring. Define ⊕ as p ⊕ q if p + q 
< 1 and p ⊕ q = 0 if p + q ≥ 1. Then (P, ⊕) is a semigroup. Define ☼ as p ☼ q = p for 
all p, q ∈ P. Clearly {P, ⊕, ☼} is a special fuzzy seminear-ring.  
 
 
Now we proceed on to define binear-rings and the concept of fuzzy binear-rings.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.25: Let (N,+, y) be a non empty set. We call N a binear-ring if N = 
N1 ∪ N2 where N1 and N2 are proper subsets of N i.e. N1 ⊄ N2 or N2 ⊄ N1 satisfying 
the following conditions: 
 
Atleast one of (Ni , +, y) is a right near-ring (i = 1, 2) i.e. for preciseness we say  
 
i. 
(N1 , +, y) is a near-ring. 
ii. 
(N2 , +, y) is a ring. 
 
We say that even if both (Ni, +, y) and (N2, +, y) are right near-rings still we call    
(N, +, y) to be a binear-ring. By default of notation we mean by a binear-ring only a 
right binear-ring unless explicitly stated. 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.26: Let (N, +, y) be a binear-ring. We call (N, +, y) as abelian if (N, 
+) is abelian i.e. if N = N1 ∪ N2 then (N1, +) and (N2, +) are both abelian. If both (N1, 
y) and (N2, y) are commutative then we call N a commutative binear-ring. If N = Nd 
i.e. N1 = (N1)d and N2 = (N2)d then we say N is a distributive binear-ring. If all non-
zero elements of N are left (right) cancelable we say that N fulfills the left (right) 
cancellation law. N is a bi-integral domain if both N1 and N2 has no zero divisors. If 
N \ {0}= N1 \ {0} and N2 \ {0} are both groups then we call N a binear field. 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.27: Let (P,+) be a bigroup i.e. (P = P1 ∪ P2) with 0 and let N be a 
binear-ring µ : N × P→ P is called the N-bigroup if for all pi ∈ Pi and for all n, ni ∈ 
Ni we have (n +ni ) p = np + ni p and (nni ) p = n(ni p) for i = 1, 2.

DEFINITION 1.8.28: A sub-bigroup M of a binear-ring N with M.M ⊂ M is called a 
sub-binear-ring of N. A bi subgroup S of N P with NS ⊂ S is a N-sub-bigroup of P. 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.29: Let N = N1 ∪ N2 be a binear-ring; P a N-bigroup. A binormal

DEFINITION 1.8.30: A sub-binear-ring M of the binear-ring N is called bi-invariant if 
MN1 ⊂ M1 and M2 N2 ⊂ M2 (where M = M1 ∪ M2 and N = N1 ∪ N2 ) and N1 M1 ⊂ M1 
and N2 M2 ⊂ M2 .  
 
A minimal bi-ideal, minimal right bi-ideal and minimal left bi-ideal and dually the 
concept of maximal right bi-ideal, left bi-ideal and maximal bi-ideal are defined as in 
case of bi-rings.

Let V denote the collection of all binear-rings and X be any non-empty subset.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.33: A binear-ring FX ∈ V is called a free binear-ring in V over a

DEFINITION 1.8.34: A finite sequence N = N0 ⊃ N1 ⊃ N2 ⊃ … ⊃ Nt = {0} of sub-
binear-rings Ni of the binear-ring N is called the binormal sequence of N if and only if 
for all i ∈ {1, 2, …, t}, Ni is a bi-ideal of Ni – 1.  
 
In the special case when all the Ni's are bi-ideals of the binear-ring N, we call the 
binormal sequence an bi-invariant sequence.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.35: Let P be a bi-ideal of a binear-ring N. P is called the prime bi-
ideal if for all bi-ideals I and J of N; IJ ⊂ N implies I ⊂ P or J ⊂ P. The binear-ring N 
is called a prime binear-ring if {0} is a prime ideal.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.36: Let S be a bi-ideal of a binear-ring N. S is semiprime if and only 
if for all bi-ideals I on N, I 2 ⊂ S implies I ⊂ S. N is called a semiprime binear-ring if 
{0} is a semiprime bi-ideal.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.37: Let L be a left bi-ideal in a binear-ring N. L is called modular if 
and only if there exists e ∈ N1 ∪ N2 and for all n ∈ N1 ∪ N2 and for all n ∈ N1 ∪ N2, 
n – ne ∈ L. In this case we also say that L is modular by e and that e is a right identity 
modulo L, since for all n ∈ N, ne = n (mod L).  
 
Notation: For z ∈ N, denote the bi-ideal generated by the set {n – nz / n ∈ N} by LZ. 
LZ = N if z = 0. z ∈ N is called quasi regular if z ∈ LZ, S ⊂ N is called quasi-regular if 
and only if for all s ∈ S, s is quasi regular.  
 
Let N be a binear-ring. An idempotent e ∈ N = N1 ∪ N2 is called central if it is in the 
center of (N1, y) or (N2, y) i.e. for all n ∈ N1 (or n ∈ N2) we have ne = en.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.38: A binear-ring is said to be biregular if there exists some set E of 
central idempotents with  
 
i. 
For all e ∈ N1 (e ∈ N2), N1 e (N2 e) is an ideal of N1 (or N2). i.e. N1 e ∪ {0} 
or {0} ∪ N2 e or if e ∈ N1 ∩ N2  then N1 e ∪ N2 e is a bi-ideal of N.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.39: Let N be a binear-ring; N = N1 ∪ N2 where N1 and N2 are near-
rings. The binear-ring N is said to fulfill the insertion factors property (IFP) provided 
for all a, b, n ∈ N1 (or a, b, n ∈ N2 ) we have ab = 0 implies anb = 0. The biring has 
strong IFP property if every homomorphic image of N has the IFP. The binear-ring N 
has the strong IFP if and only if for all I in N and for all a, b, n ∈ N1 (a, b, n ∈ N2), a, 
b ∈ I1 implies anb ∈ I1 (ab ∈ I2 implies anb ∈ I2 ) where I = I1 ∪ I2.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.40: Let p be a prime. A binear-ring N is called a p-binear-ring 
provided that for all x ∈ N, x p = x and px = 0.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.41: A biright ideal I of a binear-ring N is called right quasi 
bireflexive if whenever A and B are bi-ideals of the binear-ring N with AB ⊂ I then 
b(b' + a) – bb' ∈ I for all a ∈ A and b, b' ∈ B. A binear-ring N is strongly sub-
bicommutative if it is right quasi bireflexive.  
 

 
118
DEFINITION 1.8.42: Let N be a binear-ring S a subnormal sub-bigroup of (N, +). S is 
called a quasi bi-ideal of N if SN ⊂ NS ⊂ S whereby NS we mean elements of the form 
{n(n' + s) – nn' / for all s ∈ S and for n, n' ∈ N} = NS.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.43: An infra binear-ring (INR) is a triple (N, +, y), (N = N1 ∪ N2 ) 
where 
 
i. 
(N, +) is a bigroup. 
ii. 
(N, y) is a bisemigroup. 
iii. 
(x + y)y z = x y  z –  0 y z + y y z for all x, y, z ∈ N.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.44: Let (N, +, y) be a non-empty set, where N = N1 ∪ N2 ∪ N3 ∪ N4 
where each Ni is a near-ring or a ring for each i = 1, 2, 3, 4. (N, +, y) is defined as 
the quad near-ring or bi-binear-ring.  
 
Example 1.8.4: Let (N, +, y) be a non-empty set, where N = Z2 ∪ Z12 ∪ Z7 ∪ Z where 
Z2 and Z are near-rings and Z12 is the ring of integers modulo 12 and Z7 is the prime 
field of characteristic 7. N is a quad near-ring.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.45: Let (N, +, y) be a binear-ring where N = N1 ∪ N2 and G = G1 ∪ 
G2 be a bigroup. The bigroup binear-ring NG = N1G1 ∪ N1G2 ∪ N2G1 ∪ N2G2 is a 
quad near-ring where each NiGj is a group near-ring or group ring, i = 1, 2 and j = 
1, 2. Similarly we can define bisemigroup binear-ring NS = N1S1 ∪ N2S1 ∪ N1S2 ∪ 
N2S2 where S is a bisemigroup (S = S1 ∪ S2) and N = N1 ∪ N2 is a binear-ring. It is 
easily verified that the bisemigroup binear-ring is also a quad near-ring where NiSj 
are semigroup near-rings or semigroup rings, i = 1, 2 and j = 1, 2. All results 
connected with binear-rings can be extended in case of quad near-rings.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.46: Let (N, +, y) is a non-empty set where N = N1 ∪ N2. We say N is 
a biseminear-ring if one of (N1, +, y) or (N2, +, y) are seminear-rings. Thus for N to 
be biseminear-ring we need one of (N1, +, y) or (N2, +, y) or both to be a seminear-
ring.  
 
We can have several such biseminear-rings. All notions studied in case of binear-rings 
can also be easily studied and generalized for biseminear-rings. The only means to 
generate several new classes of biseminear-rings are as follows: 
 
DEFINITION 1.8.47: Let (S, +, y) be a biseminear-ring with S = S1 ∪ S2. G any group 
the group biseminear-ring SG = S1G ∪ S2G where SiG are group seminear-rings. 
Clearly every group biseminear-ring is a biseminear-ring.  
 
By using this definition we can have infinite class of both commutative or non-
commutative and infinite or finite classes of biseminear-rings. If in the definition of 
group biseminear-rings if we replace the group by a semigroup we get yet another 
new class of biseminear-rings.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.48: Let (N, +, y) be a non-empty set. We say (N, +, y) is a quad 
seminear-ring or bi-biseminear-ring if the following conditions hold good.  
 

 
119
i. 
N = N1 ∪ N2 ∪ N3 ∪ N4 where Ni are proper subsets of N such that Ni ⊄ 
Nj for any i ≠ j, i, j = 1, 2, 3, 4. 
ii. 
(Ni , +, y) is at least a seminear-ring for i = 1, 2, 3, 4 under the operations 
of N. Then we call (N, +, y) a quad seminear-ring or bi-biseminear-ring.  
 
DEFINITION 1.8.49: Let (N, +, y) be a binear-ring i.e. N = N1 ∪ N2 where N1 ⊄ N1 or 
N2 ⊄ N1 such that (N1,+, y) is a near-ring and (N2,+, y) is a ring. The map µ : N → 
[0,1] is said to be a fuzzy bi near-ring if µ = µ1 ∪ µ2 where µ1 : N1 → [0, 1] is a fuzzy 
near-ring and µ2 : N2 → [0,1] is a fuzzy ring; the ‘∪’ is just only a notational 
convenience.  
 
Equivalently we can define µ restricted to N1 is a fuzzy near-ring and µ restricted to 
N2 is a fuzzy ring where µ =

All notions as in case of fuzzy near-rings can be extended to fuzzy binear-rings in an 
analogous way using appropriately the fuzzy near-ring concept and the fuzzy ring 
concept. However we will be defining in the later chapters the concept of 
Smarandache fuzzy binear-rings and their properties. 
 
 
1.9 Fuzzy vector spaces and fuzzy bivector spaces 
 
In this section we introduce the concept of fuzzy bivector spaces and recall the 
definition and properties of fuzzy vector spaces. The study of fuzzy vector spaces 
started as early as 1977; but till date the study of fuzzy bivector spaces is absent.  
 
For more about fuzzy vector spaces refer [75, 79, 91, 92, 96, 101]. 
 
Throughout this section V denotes a vector space over a field F. A fuzzy subset of a 
non-empty set S is a function from S into [0, 1]. Let A denote a fuzzy subspace of V 
over a fuzzy subfield K of F and let X denote a fuzzy subset of V such that X ⊆ A. 
Let 〈X〉 denote the intersection of all fuzzy subspaces of V over K that contain X and 
are contained in A. 
 
DEFINITION 1.9.1:  
 
i. 
A fuzzy subset K of F is a fuzzy subfield of F, if K (1) = 1 and for all c, d ∈ F, 
K (c – d) ≥ min {K(c), K (d)} and K(cd –1) ≥ min { K( c), K (d)} where d ≠ 0. 
 
ii. 
A fuzzy subset A of V is a fuzzy subspace over a fuzzy subfield K of F, if A(0) > 
0 and for all x, y ∈ V and for all c ∈ F, A (x – y) ≥ min {A(x), A(y)} and A(cx) 
≥ min {K(c ) , A(x)}. If K is a fuzzy subfield of F and if x ∈ F, x ≠ 0, then K(0) 
= K(1) ≥ K(x) = K(–x) = K(+ x–1). 
 
In the following we let L denote the set of all fuzzy subfields of F and let AL denote 
the set of all fuzzy subspaces of V over K ∈ L. 
 

 
120
If A and B are fuzzy subsets of V then A ⊂ B means A(x) ≤ B(x) for all x ∈ A. For    
0 ≤ t ≤ 1, let At = {x ∈ V | A(x) ≥ t}.

Now we proceed on to define the notion of fuzzy freeness. Let ζ denote a set of fuzzy 
singletons of V such that xλ , xK∈ ζ, then λ = k > 0. Define the fuzzy subset X(ζ) of V 
by the following; for all x ∈ V, X (ζ) (x) = λ if xλ ∈ ζ, and X(ζ)(x) = 0, otherwise. 
Define  〈ζ〉 = 〈X(ζ)〉. Let X be a fuzzy subset of V. Define ζ(X) = {xλ | x ∈ V, λ = 
X(x) > 0}. Then X (ζ (x)) = X and ζ X(ζ)) = ζ. If there are only a finite number of xλ 
∈ ζ, with λ > 0 we call ζ finite. If X (x) > 0 for only a finite number of x ∈ X, we call 
X finite. Clearly ζ is finite if and only if X (ζ) is finite and X is finite if and only if 
ζ(X) is finite. For x ∈ V let X \ x denote the fuzzy subset of V defined by the 
following; for all y ∈ V let (X \ x) (y) = X (y) if y ≠ x and (X \ x) (y) = 0 if y = x. 
 

 
123
DEFINITION 1.9.6: Let A∈ AK and let X be a fuzzy subset of V such that X ⊂ A. Then X 
is called a fuzzy system of generators of A over K if 〈 X〉 = A. X is said to be fuzzy free 
over K if for all xλ ∈ X where λ = X (x), xλ ⊄  〈 X \ x〉. X is said to be a fuzzy basis for 
A if X is a fuzzy system of generators of A and X is fuzzy free. Let ζ denote a set of 
fuzzy singletons of V such that if xλ, xK ∈ ζ then λ = k and xλ ⊆ A. Then ζ is called a 
fuzzy singleton system of generators of A over K. if 〈 ζ 〉 = A. ζ is said to be fuzzy free 
over K, if for all xλ ∈ ζ , xλ ⊄ 〈ζ \ {xλ}〉. ζ is said to be a fuzzy basis of singletons for A 
if ζ is a fuzzy singleton system of generators of A and ζ is fuzzy free.

Now we proceed on to define the notion of fuzzy algebraically independent. Let X be 
a fuzzy subset of F, we let φ (X) = {xt t = X(x) > 0}. If φ is a set of fuzzy singletons 
such that xt, xs ∈ φ implies t = s, then we let X (φ) denote the fuzzy subset of F 
defined by (X(φ)) (x) = t if xt ∈ φ and (X(φ)) (x) = 0 if xt ∉ φ. Clearly X(φ(X)) = X 
and φ (X(φ)) = φ. We let i = (i1, …, in). If F is a field F(F) denotes the set of all fuzzy 

 
125
subfields of F. F(A) will denote the set of all fuzzy subsets X of F such that X ⊆ A 
and F(A/B) the set of all fuzzy subfields C of F such that B ⊆ C ⊆ A. 
 
We in the forthcoming definitions and results assume A, B ∈ F(F) and B ⊂ A . 
 
Now we proceed on to recall the definitions as given by [91]. The following notation 
and definition is recalled for they are used in the following results. 
 
DEFINITION 1.9.8: Let A, B ∈ F(R), B ⊂ A, and let X be a fuzzy subset of R such that 
X ⊆ A. Define B[X] to be the intersection of all C ∈ F(R) such that B ∪ X ⊆ C ⊆ A. 
For A, B ∈ F(F), B ⊆ A and let X be a fuzzy subset of F such that X ⊂ A. Define B(X) 
to be the intersection of all C ∈ F(F) such that B ∪ X ⊆ C ⊆ A. B [X] is a fuzzy 
subring of R and B [X] is a fuzzy subfield of F.

The following theorem can also be a proved as a matter of routine. 
 
THEOREM 1.9.22: Let X ∈ F(A). Then X is fuzzy algebraically independent over B if 
and only if X* is algebraically independent over B*. 
 
THEOREM 1.9.23: Let X ∈ F(A).  
 
i. 
For all t ∈ (0, 1], Bt (Xt ) ⊆ B(X)t . 
ii. 
If B(X) has the sup property then for all t ∈ (0, 1],  
 
Bt (Xt) = B(X)t . 
 

 
126
 
Proof:  
 
i. Let z ∈ Bt (Xt), then (B(X))(z) ≥ t follows easily from properties of fuzzy sets. 
ii. Let z ∈ B(X)t. Then (B(X))(z) ≥ t and since B(X) has the sup property, z has a 
representation as an element of Bt (Xt) by results on fuzzy sets. Thus z ∈ Bt (Xt). 
 
THEOREM 1.9.24: Let X ∈ F(A). Then B(X)* = B*(X* ) and B[X* ] = B* [X* ]. 
 
Proof: x ∈ B(X)* if and only if (B(X)) (x) > 0 if and only if x ∈ B*(X*). Similarly we 
can prove B[X]* = B* [X*]. Let X ∈ F(A). We say that X is maximally fuzzy 
algebraically independent over B if and only if X is fuzzy algebraically independent 
over B and there does not exist Y ∈ F(A) such that Y is fuzzy algebraically 
independent over B and X ⊂ Y. 
 
THEOREM 1.9.25: Let X ∈ F(A). Suppose for all x ∈ X *  we have X (x) = A(x). Then X 
is maximally fuzzy algebraically independent over B if and only if X* is transcendence 
basis of A*/B*. 
 
Proof: By earlier results and routine calculations the result can be arrived.  
 
The following theorem is a direct consequence of the above result. Hence the reader is 
expected to prove the result.

F and the cardinality of each is unique.

DEFINITION 1.9.10: Let ct ⊆ A, with t > 0 Then ct is called fuzzy algebraical over B if 
and only if ct is not fuzzy transcendental over B. If every such ct is fuzzy algebraical 
over B, then A/B is called fuzzy algebraical; otherwise A/B is called fuzzy 
transcendental.  
 
DEFINITION 1.9.11: Let ct ⊆ A, with t > 0 If there exists s ∈ (0, t] such that cs ⊆ B, 
then ct is called neutral over B. If every such ct is neutral over B, then A/B is called 
neutral. 
 
Clearly A/B is neutral if and only if A* = B*. Suppose that for c ∈ F, t = A (c ) > B(c ) 
= s > 0. Then ct + (-cs ) = 0s, so that ct is fuzzy algebraical over B. In fact we can think 
of ct as being a root of a first degree polynomial in x, x + (–cs ) with cs ⊆ B.

THEOREM 1.9.30: Let K, b ∈ B*, K ≠ 0. If B(K) ≠ B(b) then B(Kb) = min {B(K),

THEOREM 1.9.31: Let ct ⊆ A with t > 0. Suppose that c is algebraic over B*. Let p(x) 
be the minimal polynomial of c over B*. If ct fuzzy algebraical (not fuzzy algebraic)

every irreducible polynomial over B* which has c as a root.  
 
Proof: Let p(x) = xn + …+ K1x + Ko. Then there exists Ki such that 0 < B (Ki) < t. Let

B(K) = B(Ki), then 0 < B (K) < t and the desired result follows in this case since K is

The following result is a direct consequence of the definitions and a matter of routine. 
 
THEOREM 1.9.32: Define the fuzzy subset B (n) of F by B (n) (x) = A(x) if x ∈ B* and 
Bn(x) = 0 if x ∉ B∗. Then Bn ∈ F (A/B). Suppose that inf {A (x) | x ∈ B*} ≥ sup {A(x) | 
x ∉ B∗}.  If A/B is fuzzy algebraical then A/B (n) is fuzzy algebraic. 
 
Now we proceed on to define fuzzy transcendence basis. 
 
DEFINITION 1.9.13: Let X ∈ F (A). Then X is called a fuzzy transcendence basis of 
A/B if and only if X is fuzzy algebraically independent over B and A/B (X) is fuzzy 
algebraical. 
 
THEOREM 1.9.33: A/B has a fuzzy transcendence basis and the cardinality of a fuzzy 
transcendence basis is unique. In fact X is a fuzzy transcendence basis of A/B if and 
only if X* is a transcendence of A*/B*. 
 
Proof: Follows easily by the very definition.

THEOREM 1.9.34: 
 
i. A/B is fuzzy pure inseparable if and only if A*/B* is purely inseparable. 
ii. A/B is fuzzy separable algebraical if and only if A*/B* is separable algebraic.

THEOREM 1.9.35: Let C ∈ F(A/B),  
 
i. 
A/B is fuzzy pure inseparable if and only if A/C and C/B are fuzzy pure 
inseparable. 
ii. 
A/B is fuzzy separable algebraical if and only if A/C and C/B are fuzzy 
separable algebraical. 
 
THEOREM 1.9.36: Let ct ⊆ A with t > 0. If ct is fuzzy algebraical (pure inseparable or 
separable algebraical) over B, then B (ct ) /B is fuzzy algebraical (pure inseparable or 
separable algebraical). 
 
Proof: Define the fuzzy subset c of F by c(x) = A(x) if x ∈ B (ct)* and c(x) = 0 other 
wise. Then c* = B (ct)* = B* ((ct)*) = B* (c) and B* (c) / B* i.e. c* / B* is either 
algebraic or purely inseparable or separable algebraic according as c has these 
properties over B*.  
 
Using earlier result the proof of the theorem is a mater or routine. 
 
Now we proceed on to define fuzzy separating transcendence basis of A / B. 
 
DEFINITION 1.9.15: Let X ∈ F (A). Then X is called a fuzzy separating transcendence 
basis of A / B if and only if X is fuzzy algebraically independent over B and A / B(X) is 
fuzzy separable algebraical. 
 
The following two theorems are also a matter of routine hence left for the reader as an 
exercise. 
 
THEOREM 1.9.37: A/B has a fuzzy separating transcendence basis if and only if A*/B* 
has a separating transcendence basis. In fact X is a fuzzy separating transcendence 
basis of A/B if and only if X* is a separating transcendence basis of A*/B*.

DEFINITION 1.9.16: A fuzzy vector space (V, η) or ηV is an ordinary vector space V 
with a map η : V → [0, 1] satisfying the following conditions. 
 
i. 
η (a + b) ≥ min {η (a), η (b)}. 
ii. 
η (– a) = η(a).

iii. 
η (0) = 1. 
iv. 
η (ra) ≥ η(a) for all a, b ∈ V and r ∈ F where F is a field.

Now similar to vector spaces are the notions of modules. We recall the definition and 
some of the properties of fuzzy modules. 
 
DEFINITION 1.9.19: A fuzzy R-module ηM is an ordinary module M with a map η : M 
→ [0, 1] satisfying the following conditions:  
 
i. 
η (a + b) ≥ min { η(a), η(b)}. 
ii.

Let a vector space V be over R, where R is a field or a division ring. For any ν ∈ V 
and r ∈ R, r ≠ 0 we choose ν = r–1ν and then rν' = ν. By definition V is a divisible 
module. Further let W be a vector subspace of V. For any ν ∉ W and r ∈ R; r ≠ 0, r ν 
∈ W implies r–1 (r ν) ∈ r W = W i.e. ν ∈ W. This is contrary to the hypothesis. Hence 
rν ∉ W and so W is prime submodule.

and then we obtain a fuzzy submodule

1.10 Fuzzy semigroup and their properties 
 
The study of fuzzy semigroups started in 1979. Several results have been obtained in 
this direction. However to make our study most recent we recall some facts about 
fuzzy semigroups from the result of [142, 143]. Researchers have studied many 
classes of semigroups using fuzzy ideals. Here we recall most notions from [143]. 

 
133
 
DEFINITION [54]: Let S be a non-empty set completely ordered by transitive, 
irreflexive relation '<'. For all x, y ∈ S, exactly one relations x < y, x = y, y < x holds.  
 
For a, b ∈ S define  
  
(a, b)  
=  {x ∈  S /  a < x < b} 
[a, b)  
=  {x ∈  S / a ≤ x < b} 
(a, b]  
=  {x ∈ S / a < x ≤ b} and  
[a, b]  
=  {x ∈ S / a ≤ x ≤ b}. 
 
This notation we have used already we may use them as an algebraic structure viz. 
semigroups whenever the situation demands. 
 
Now we define a fuzzy semigroup.  
 
DEFINITION 1.10.1: Let (S, y) be a semigroup. A map µ: S → [0, 1] is called a fuzzy 
semigroup if µ(x y y) = min {µ(x), µ(y)} for all x, y ∈ S. 
 
From this definition we evolve the following. All algebraic structures viz. fuzzy 
groups, fuzzy rings, fuzzy near-rings, fuzzy semirings are obviously fuzzy semigroups 
as at least under one operation we have µ : X → [0, 1], X an algebraic structure we 
have µ(x ∗ y) = min{µ(x), µ(y)}. ∗ can be some closed operation on X.  
 
Let S denote a semigroup. A non-empty subset I of S is called a left-ideal of S if SI ⊂ I. 
I is called a right ideal if IS ⊂ I. I is said to be an ideal of S, if I is both a left and a 
right ideal of S. 
 
We call a non-empty subset L of S which is a left ideal of S to be prime if for any two 
ideal A and B of S such that AB ⊆ L, it implies that A ⊆ L or B ⊆ L. L is called quasi 
prime if for any two left ideals L1 and L2 of S, L1L2 ⊆ L then L1 ⊂ L or L2 ⊂ L and L is 
called weakly quasi prime if any one of the two left ideals of L1 and L2 of S such that L 
⊆ L1, L2 and L1L2 ⊂ L we have L1 = L or L2 = L. 
 
Let S be a semigroup. A function f from S to the unit interval [0, 1] is a fuzzy subset of 
S. A semigroup S itself is a fuzzy subset of S such that S(x) = 1 for all x ∈ S denoted 
by S. Let µ and δ be any two fuzzy subsets of S. Then the inclusion relation µ ⊆ δ is 
defined by µ(x) ≤ δ(x) for all x ∈ S. (1 – µ) is a fuzzy subset of S defined for all x ∈ S. 
 
(1 – µ)(x) = 1 – µ(x), µ ∩ δ and µ ∪ δ are fuzzy subsets of S defined by (µ ∩ δ)(x) = 
min{µ(x), δ(x)}, (µ ∪ δ)(x) = max{µ(x), δ(x)} for all x ∈ S. The product µ y δ is 
defined as follows: 
 
(µ y δ)(x) =

A fuzzy subset f of S is called a fuzzy left ideal of S if S o f ⊆ f and f is called a fuzzy 
right ideal of S if f o S ⊆ f. If f is both a fuzzy left and fuzzy right ideal then we call f a 
fuzzy ideal of S. Equivalently if f(xy) > max{f(x), f(y)} for all x, y ∈ S. 
 
THEOREM 1.10.1: Let f and g be fuzzy subsets of S. Then the following statements are 
true:  
 
i. 
f o (g ∪ h) = (f o g) ∪ (f o h). 
ii. 
f o (g ∩ h) = (f o g) ∩ (f o h). 
iii. 
If f1, f2 are fuzzy subsets of S such that f1 ⊆ f, f2 ⊆ g. Then f1 o f2 ⊆ f o g.  
 
Proof: Straightforward.

THEOREM 1.10.4: Let A be a subset of S. Then for any λ ∈ (0, 1] the following 
statements are true  
 
i. λfA o λfB = λfAB 
ii. λfA ∩ λfB = λfA ∩ B

Proof: Easy consequence.  
 
DEFINITION 1.10.2: A fuzzy left ideal f is called prime if for any two fuzzy ideals f1 
and f2 ,  f1  o  f2  ⊆  f implies that fi  ⊆  f or f2  ⊆  f. 
 
The following theorem can be proved as a matter of routine.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.5: A fuzzy left ideal f of S is prime if and only if for any two fuzzy 
points xr , yt ∈ S (rt > 0), xr  o S o yt o S  ⊆   f implies that xr ∈ f or yt ∈ f.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.6: A left ideal L of S is prime if and only if fL is a prime fuzzy left ideal 
of S.  
 
Proof: Follows as a matter of routine. The reader who is not able to prove kindly refer 
[142, 143]. 
 
Now we proceed on to recall the definition of quasi prime ideals of semigroup S.  
 
DEFINITION 1.10.3: A fuzzy left ideal f is called quasi prime if for any two fuzzy left 
ideals f1 and f2; f1  o  f2  ⊆  f implies that f1 ⊆  f and f2 ⊆  f; f is called quasi semiprime 
if for any fuzzy left ideal g of S, g2 ⊆  f implies that g ⊆  f.  
 
Similar to the two theorems just stated we have the following two theorems.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.7: A fuzzy left ideal f of S is quasi prime if and only if for any fuzzy 
points xr, yt ∈ S (rt > 0), xr o S o yt ⊆ f implies that xr ∈ f and yt ∈ f.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.8: A left ideal L of S is quasi prime if and only if fL is a quasi prime 
fuzzy left ideal of S.  

 
136
 
Now we proceed on to recall the definition of fuzzy m-systems.  
 
DEFINITION 1.10.4: A fuzzy subset f of S is called fuzzy m-system if for any t, s ∈        
[0, 1) and a, b ∈ S, f(a) > t, f(b) > s imply that there exists an element x ∈ S such that 
f(axb) > t ∨ s.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.9: Let M be a subset of S. Then M is a m-system of S if and only if fM 
is a fuzzy m-system.  
 
Proof: For any t, s ∈ [0, 1) and a, b ∈ S if fM(a) > t, fM(b) > s, then a, b ∈ M. By 
hypothesis there exists an element x ∈ S such that axb ∈ M that is fM(axb) = 1. Thus 
fM(axb) > t ∨ s. 
 
Conversely let a, b ∈ M. Then fM(a) = fM(b) = 1. Thus for any t, s ∈ [0, 1), fM(a) > t, 
fM(b) > s which implies that there exists an element x ∈ S such that fM(axb) > s ∨ b. 
Therefore axb ∈ M.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.10: Let f be a fuzzy left ideal of S. Then f is quasi prime if and only if 
1 – f is a fuzzy m-system.  
 
Proof: It is easily obtainable by routine calculations, hence left as an exercise for the 
reader.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.11: A fuzzy left ideal f is called weakly quasi prime if for any two 
fuzzy left ideals f1 and f2 such that f ⊆ f1 and f ⊆ f2 and f1 o f2 ⊆  f then f1 ⊆  f or f2 ⊆  f.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.12: A left ideal L is weakly quasi prime if and only if f is weakly quasi 
prime.  
 
Proof: Please refer [143]. 
 
THEOREM 1.10.13: Let S be a commutative, f a fuzzy left ideal of S. Then the 
following statements are equivalent.  
 
i. 
f is prime.  
ii. 
f is quasi prime. 
iii. 
f is weakly quasi prime.  
 
Proof: (i) ⇒ (ii) ⇒ (iii) is obvious. (iii) ⇒ (i). Let f1 o f2 ⊂ f for any two fuzzy ideals 
f1 and f2 of S. Since S is commutative, so we have f is a fuzzy ideal of S and  
 
(f1 ∪ f) o (f2 ∪ f)  
⊆  
f1 o f2 ∪ f1 o f ∪ f o f2 ∪ f 2
 
⊆ 
f 
 
THEOREM 1.10.14: Let f be a fuzzy left ideal of S. Then the following statements are 
equivalent.  
 
i. f is weakly quasi prime fuzzy left ideal of S  

 
137
ii. For any fuzzy ideals f1 , f2 ⊆ S if ( f1 ∪ f ) o ( f2 ∪ f ) ⊆  f  then f1 ⊆  f or f2  ⊆  f.  
iii. For any two fuzzy left ideals f1 , f2 ⊆ S if f1  ⊆  f and f1 o f2 ⊆  f then f1 = f or f2  ⊆  f.  
iv. For any two fuzzy ideals f1 , f2 ⊆ S, if (f1  ∪  f) o f2 ⊆  f then f1  ⊆  f or f2  ⊆  f.  
v. For any two fuzzy points ar, bt ∈ S (rt > 0) if (ar ∪ f) o S o (bt ∪ f) ⊆ f, then ar ∈ f 
or bt ∈ S.  
 
Proof: Left for the reader to prove.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.15: Let f be fuzzy left ideal of S and µ be a fuzzy subset of S satisfying 
that  
 
i. f ∩ µ = 0.  
ii. For any at, br ∈ µ (at ∪ f) o S(br ∪ f) ∩ µ ≠ 0. 
 
If g is a maximal fuzzy left ideal of S with respect to containing f and g ∩ µ = 0. Then 
g is a weakly quasiprime fuzzy left ideal of S.  
 
Proof: Let f1 and f2 be a fuzzy left ideal of S such that g ⊆ f1, f2 and f1f2 ⊆ g. If g ⊂ f1 
and g2 ⊂ f2 then there exists at ∈ f \ g, br ∈ f2 \ g, (rt > 0).  
 
Thus g ⊂ g ∪ L(at) ⊆ f1, g ⊂ g ∪ L(br) ⊆ f2.  
 
By hypothesis, we have  
 
(g ⊂ L(a r )) ∩ µ ≠ 0, (g ∪ L(b r )) ∩ µ ≠ 0. Let c k  ∈ (g ∪ L(a t))  ∩  µ (k > 0), dl ∈ (g 
∪ L(b r)) ∩ µ ( l > 0).  
 
Then (ck ∪ f) o S o (dl ∪ f) ⊆ (f1 ∪ f) o S o (f2 ∪ f) ⊆ f1 o S o f2 ∪ f1 ∪ S o f ∪ f o S o 
f2 ∪ f o S o f ⊆ f1 o f2 ⊆ g which contradicts with the fact that (ck ∪ f) o S o (dl ∪ f) ∩ 
µ ≠ 0. Hence the claim. 
 
Fuzzy ideals in i(f) and I(f) 
 
Let f be a fuzzy left ideal of S, we define two fuzzy subsets of S, denoted by i(f) and 
I(f) respectively as follows (∀ x ∈ S), 
 
i(f)(x) = ∨ {tα /

THEOREM 1.10.16: Let f be a left-ideal of S. Then i(f) is the largest fuzzy ideal of S 
contained in f.  
 
Proof: Left for the reader to prove.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.17: Let S be a semigroup with an identity e and f a prime fuzzy left 
ideal of S. If i(f) ≠ 0 then i(f) is a quasi prime fuzzy ideal of S.  
 

 
138
Proof: Let at and br (tr > 0) be fuzzy points of S such that at o S o br ⊆ i(f). Then (S o 
at o S) o (S o br o S) ⊆ i(f) ⊆ f.  
 
Since f is prime we have S o at o S ⊆ f or S o br o S ⊆ f. Say S o at o S ⊆ f. Using 
results we have S o at o S ⊆ i(f). Since S has an identity e so that at = (eae)t, et o at o et 
⊆ S o at o S ⊆ i(f).  
 
The following theorem is interesting.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.18: Let f be a fuzzy ideal of S. Then I(f) is the largest fuzzy 
subsemigroup of S such that f is a fuzzy ideal I(f).  
 
Proof: Left as an exercise for the reader. Please refer [143]. 
 
THEOREM 1.10.19: Let S be a semigroup with an identity e, f a fuzzy left ideal of S but 
not a fuzzy ideal of S. Then the following statements are equivalent.  
 
i. f is a weakly quasi prime fuzzy left ideal of S. 
ii. If  f1 is a fuzzy left ideal of S such that f o f1 ⊆  f then f1 ⊆  f.  
iii. For any fuzzy point at ∈ S  if  f o (S o at ) ⊆  f, then at ∈ f.  
iv. f is the largest fuzzy left ideal of S contained in I(f).  
 
Proof: Refer [143].  
 
Now we proceed on to define the concept of strongly semisimple semigroups.  
 
DEFINITION 1.10.5: A semigroup S is called strongly semisimple if every left ideals of 
S is idempotent. A left ideal f of a semigroup S is called idempotent if f = f o f that is   
f 2 = f.  
 
The following result gives equivalent formulation of strongly semisimple semigroup. 
 
THEOREM 1.10.20: For a semigroup S the following conditions are equivalent. 
 
i. 
S is strongly semisimple. 
ii. 
For any a ∈ S, a ∈ SaSa. 
 
Proof: For any a ∈ S, since S is strongly semisimple we have L(a) = L(a)2. Thus L(a) 
= L(a)4. Since L(a)2 = (a ∪ Sa)(a ∪ Sa) ⊆ Sa ∪ SaSa then a ∈ L(a) = L(a)4 ⊆ (Sa ∪ 
SaSa), (Sa ∪ SaSa) ⊆ SaSa.  
 
Conversely let L be any left ideal of S. For any a ∈ L by (ii) we have a ∈ SaSa ⊆ 
L(a)L(a) ⊆ L2. Thus L ⊆ L2. Clearly L2 ⊂ L. Therefore L2 = L. 
 
The following theorem characterizes the strongly semisimple semigroups by means of 
fuzzy quasi prime left ideals of S. 
 
THEOREM 1.10.21: Let S be a semigroup. Then the following statements are 
equivalent.  

 
139
 
i. 
Every fuzzy left fuzzy ideal of S is idempotent. 
ii. 
For any two fuzzy left ideals f1 and f2 of S, f1 ∩ f2 ⊆ f1 o f2. 
iii. 
For any fuzzy point ar ∈ S, L(ar) ⊆ L(ar)2
. 
iv. 
For any fuzzy point ar ∈ S, ar ∈ S o ar o S o ar.  
v. 
Every fuzzy left ideal of S is a quasi semiprime fuzzy left ideal of S. 
vi. 
Every fuzzy left ideal of S is the intersection of all quasi prime fuzzy left 
ideals of S containing it.  
 
Proof: Since the proof is long, it is left for the reader to prove. The reader is advised 
to refer [143]. 
 
THEOREM 1.10.22: A semigroup S is strongly semisimple if and only if every fuzzy left 
ideal of S is idempotent.  
 
Proof: Follows easily by the definitions and the theorem.  
 
THEOREM 1.10.23: Let S be commutative. Then the fuzzy left ideals of S are quasi 
prime if and only if they form a chain and S is strongly semisimple.  
 
Proof: Let g and h be fuzzy left ideals of S. Since g o h is a fuzzy left ideal of S by       
g o h ⊂ S o h we have g ⊆ g o h ⊂ S o h ⊂ h or h ⊆ g o h ⊆ g o S ⊆ g. Thus the fuzzy 
left ideals of S form a chain. Moreover for any fuzzy left ideal f of S obviously f   2 ⊆ f. 
Since f o f ⊆ f 2 we have f ⊆ f 2 so that f 2 = f.  
 
Conversely let f, g be two fuzzy left ideals of S, and f o g ⊂ h. Since the fuzzy left 
ideals of S form a chain i.e. f ⊆ g or g ⊆ f we have f 2 ⊂ f o g ⊂ h or g2
 ⊆ f o g ⊆ h. 
By hypothesis f ⊆ h or g ⊆ h holds.

As the main aim of this book is fuzzy algebra and Smarandache fuzzy algebra we 
have restrained ourselves from this study.  
 
 
1.11 Fuzzy Sub-half groupoids and its generalizations 
 
In this section we recall the definition of non-closed fuzzy algebraic structures, 
namely fuzzy sub-halfgroupoids of a halfgroupoid, which is a generalization of a 
fuzzy group. Here we give the necessary and sufficient condition for the two fuzzy 
sub-halfgroupoids to have open fuzzy extension. Also we recall yet another new 
notion of the concept called anti fuzzy sub halfgroupoid and anti fuzzy extension and 
obtain some characterization theorems about them. We also recall the notion of 
normalized fuzzy extension and maximal fuzzy extension and obtain a relation 
between these two concepts. 
 
A new notion called fuzzy extension chains for half groupoids is studied 
characterization theorems for normalized fuzzy extension and maximal fuzzy 
extension are obtained. Also a relationship between fuzzy translation extension and 
fuzzy multiplication extension is given in this section. 
 
We prove if µ is a fuzzy sub-halfgroupoid of a halfgroupoid P and α ∈ [0, 1 – 
sup{µ(x) | x ∈ P}] then {

fuzzy extension chain for P. We prove that {

We denote by (P, *) the halfgroupoid where '∗' need not in general be closed or 
associative binary operation. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.1: Let P be a halfgroupoid. A map µ : P → [0, 1] is said to be a 
fuzzy sub-halfgroupoid of the halfgroupoid P if ab = c in P implies µ (c ) ≥ min {µ(a) , 
µ(b)} (Here ‘a b= c’ in P means that a, b, c ∈ P and a b = c). 
 
We illustrate this by an example.

THEOREM 1.11.3: Every open fuzzy extension is a fuzzy extension but not conversely. 
 
Proof: Every open fuzzy extension is a fuzzy extension, directly follows from the 
definitions of open fuzzy extension and fuzzy extension.

not an open fuzzy extension of µ1.

THEOREM 1.11.4: Let µ1 and µ2 be any two fuzzy sub half groupoids of a half 
groupoid P. If the fuzzy extension µ2 (of µ1) is an fuzzy extension of µ1 then µ1 (c) = 
min {µ1 (a), µ1 (b)} if and only if µ2 (c) = min {µ2 (a), µ2 (b)}. 
 
We prove the converse of the contra positive method. Let ab = c in P and µ1 (c) ≠ 
min{µ1 (a), µ2 (b)} to prove µ2 (c) ≠ min {µ2 (a), µ1 (b)}. 
 
If ab = c in P and µ1 (c) ≠ min {µ1 (a), µ1 (b)}, then we have µ1 (c) > min {µ1 (a), 
µ1(b)} (since µ1 is a fuzzy subhalf-groupoid of the half groupoid P). 
 
This implies that µ2 (c) > min {µ2 (a), µ2 (b)} (since µ2 is an open fuzzy extension of 
µ1). That is µ2 (c) ≠ min {µ2 (a), µ2 (b)}. Hence we have proved that if ab = c in P and 
µ1 (c) ≠ min {µ1 (a), µ1 (b)} then µ2 (c) ≠ min {µ2 (a), µ2 (b)}. This proves that if the 
fuzzy extension µ2 (of µ1) is an open fuzzy extension of µ1 then µ1 (c) = min {µ1 (a), 
µ1 (b)} if and only if µ2 (c) = min {µ2 (a), µ2 (b)} for every ab = c in P.  
 
We illustrate the above theorem by an example. 
 
Example 1.11.6: Take µ1 and µ2 given by the half groupoid P = {– n1, – (n –1), …,–1, 
0,1,…, n} then it is easy to verify that if µ2 is an open fuzzy extension of µ1 then µ1(c) 
= min {µ1 (a), µ1 (b)} if and only if µ2 (c) = min {µ2 (a), µ2 (b)} for every ab = c in P. 
 

 
148
Since there are not many books on fuzzy algebra and especially no paper even on 
fuzzy half groupoids we are forced to give all definitions and results about them. 
 
Now we proceed on to give the definition of anti fuzzy extension. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.5: A fuzzy subset µ of a half groupoid P is said to be an anti-fuzzy 
subhalfgroupoid of the half groupoid P if ab = c in P implies µ(c) ≤ min{µ(a), µ(b)}. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.6: Let µ1 and µ2 be any two anti fuzzy subhalf-groupoids of a half 
groupoid P. Then µ2 is said to be an anti fuzzy extension of µ1 if the following two 
conditions hold good.  
 
i. µ1 (x) ≥ µ2 (x) for every x ∈ P.  
ii. if ab = c in P and µ1(c) = max {µ1(a), µ1(b)} then µ2(c) = max {µ2(a), µ2(b)}. 
 
As in case of fuzzy extension we can prove that if µ2 is an anti fuzzy extension of µ1 
and ab = c in P with µ2 (c) = max {µ2 (a) , µ2 (b)} then it need not in general imply 
that µ1 (c) = max {µ1 (a) , µ1 (b)}. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.7: Let µ1 and µ2 be any two anti fuzzy subhalf-groupoids of a half 
groupoid P. Then the anti fuzzy extension µ2 (of µ1) is said to be an open anti fuzzy 
extension of µ1 if a b = c in P and µ1 (c) < max {µ1 (a), µ1 (b)} implies µ2 (c) < 
max{µ2 (a), µ2 (b)}. 
 
If µ2 is not an open anti fuzzy extension of µ1 then we say that µ2 is a closed anti 
fuzzy extension of µ1. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.8: Let P be a half groupoid. A countable collection of anti fuzzy sub 
half groupoids of the half groupoid P, denoted by {µi i = 0, 1, 2, …} or { }
∞
=0
i
iµ
 is 
called an anti fuzzy extension chain for P if µi+1 is an anti fuzzy extension of µi for i = 
0, 1, 2, ….

THEOREM 1.11.5: Let P be a half groupoid. Then µ is a fuzzy subhalf-groupoid of the 
half groupoid P if and only if µc is an anti fuzzy subhalf-groupoid of the half groupoid 
P. 
 
Proof: Let µ be a fuzzy subhalf-groupoid of a half groupoid P and ab = c in P. Since 
µc(x) = 1 – µ (x) for every x ∈ P, we have µ (c ) ≥ min {µ (a) , µ (b)}if and only if 
µc(c) ≤ max {µc(a), µc(b)}. This proves that µ is a fuzzy subhalf-groupoid of the half 
groupoid P if and only if µc is an anti fuzzy sub-half groupoid of the half groupoid P.

Now we proceed on to define the concept of maximal fuzzy extension. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.9: Let µ1 and µ2 be any two fuzzy sub-half groupoids of a half 
groupoid P. A fuzzy extension µ2 (of µ1) is said to be the maximal fuzzy extension of µ1 
if there does not exist any fuzzy sub half groupoid µ of the half groupoid P such that 
µ2 ⊂ µ . 
 
Example 1.11.9: P = {1, 2, 3, …, n} be a half groupoid with respect to usual 
multiplication. Define µ1(x) = ½ for every x ∈ P and µ2(x) = 1 for every x ∈ P. It is 
easy to verify that both µ1 and µ2 are fuzzy subhalf groupoids of the groupoid P. Now 
we cannot find any fuzzy sub half groupoid µ of the half groupoid P such that µ2 ⊂ µ. 
Hence µ2 is the maximal fuzzy extension of µ1. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.10: Let µ1 and µ2 be any two fuzzy sub-half groupoids of a half 
groupoid P. A fuzzy extension µ2 (of µ1) is said to be a normalized fuzzy extension of 
µ1 if µ2(x) = 1 for some x ∈ P. 
 
In the above example the fuzzy subhalf-groupoid µ2 is a normalized fuzzy extension 
of µ1. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.7: Let µ1 and µ2 be any two fuzzy sub-half groupoids of a half 
groupoid P. If µ2 is the maximal fuzzy extension of µ1 then µ2 is a normalized fuzzy 
extension of µ1.  
The converse is not true in general. 
 
Proof: The first part of the theorem follows from the definitions of the maximal and 
normalized fuzzy extensions. The converse is not true. That is every normalized fuzzy

If 
T
α
µ  is a normalized fuzzy extension of µ then it does not in general imply that µ has 
sup property. This can be seen from the example given below. 
 
Consider the half groupoid P = [0, 1] under usual multiplication. Define µ : P → [0, 1] 
by µ(x) = 1 – x for every x ∈ P. It is easy to see that µ is a fuzzy sub-half groupoid of 
the half groupoid P. Let α ∈ [0, 1 – sup {µ(x) | x ∈ P}] then 
T
α
µ  is a normalized fuzzy 
extension of µ for there exists 0 ∈ P such that 
T
α
µ  (0) = 1. Take A = [0, 1] then A ⊆ 
[0, 1] and sup {µ(x) | x ∈ A} = 1. 
 
Now it is easy to verify that there is no x0 ∈ A such that µ(x0) = 1. Hence µ does not 
have sup property. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.12: A fuzzy subset µ of a set X has the weak sup property if there 
exist x0 ∈ X such that µ(x0) = sup {µ(x) | x ∈ X }. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.11: Let µ be a fuzzy subset of a set X . If µ has sup property then µ 
has weak sup property but not conversely. 
 
Proof: Let µ be a fuzzy subset of a set X. If µ has sup property then by the definition 
of sup property for every subset A of the set X there exist x0 ∈ A such that µ(x0) = 
sup {µ(y) | y ∈ A}. The above condition is true for every subset A of the set X. If we 
take A as X then there exists x0 ∈ A such that µ(x0) = sup {µ(y) | y ∈ X}. Hence µ has 
weak sup property. 
 
However the converse is not true this can be seen by the following example. 
 
Consider the set X = [γ, δ] where γ and δ are any two arbitrary fixed numbers in the 
interval [0, 1] with γ < δ. Define µ: [γ, δ] →[0, 1] by µ(x) = x for every x ∈ X. Then 
µ(δ) = sup {µ(x) | x ∈ X}. but for A = [γ, δ) we have sup {µ(x) | x ∈ A} = δ and there 
is no x0 ∈ A such that µ(x0) = δ. That is µ has weak sup property but µ does not have 
sup property.  
 
Hence we give the characterization for normalized fuzzy extension.

Now we introduce the notions of fuzzy equivalence relation as given by [144] and 
proceed on to recall the definition of fuzzy relation on the groupoid given by [70]. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.14: Let X and Y be two non empty sets, we call a map f: X × X → Y 
× Y a semi balanced map if  
 
i. Given a ∈ X there exists υ ∈ Y such that f (a, a) = (υ, υ).  
ii. f (a, a) = (υ, υ), f (b, b) = (υ, υ) imply that f (a, b) = (υ, υ). 
 
DEFINITION [144]: Let λ and µ be two fuzzy relations on the set X. The sup-min 
product λ o µ is a fuzzy relation on X defined by  
 
λ o µ (a, b) =

a, b ∈ X; λ is reflexive on X if λ (x, x) = 1 for all x ∈ X; λ is symmetric on X if λ (a, b) 
= λ (b, a) for all a, b ∈ X; and λ is transitive on X if λ o λ ⊆ λ .  
 
A reflexive, symmetric and transitive fuzzy relation on a set is a fuzzy equivalence 
relation.

DEFINITION [70]: Let λ be a fuzzy relation on the groupoid D; λ is compatible on D if 
λ (ac, bd) ≥ min {λ (a, b), λ (c, d)} for all a, b, c, d ∈ D. 
 
A compatible fuzzy equivalence relation on a groupoid is a congruence.

A G-reflexive and transitive fuzzy relation X is a G-pre order on X. A symmetric G- 
pre order on X is a G-equivalence on X. A compatible G-equivalence fuzzy relation 
on a groupoid is a G-congruence. 
 
THEOREM [45]: If λ is a G-pre order on the set then λ o λ = λ. 
 
DEFINITION [44, 45]: Let λ be a fuzzy relation on the set X and let 0 < α ≤ 1; λ is α-
reflexive on X if λ (a, a) = α and λ (a, a) ≤ α for all a, b ∈ X. An α-reflexive 
symmetric and transitive fuzzy relation on X is a fuzzy α-equivalence relation on X. 
We call a compatible fuzzy α-equivalence relation on a groupoid as α-congruence.  
 
Clearly a fuzzy α-equivalence relation is a fuzzy equivalence, if α = 1, and every 
fuzzy α-equivalence relation is α G-equivalence.  
 
Now we proceed on to recall definition of fuzzy equivalences and congruences on a 
groupoid. 
 
THEOREM [44, 45]: If µ is compatible fuzzy relation on the groupoid S and f is a 
groupoid homomorphism from D × D into S × S then f –1(µ) is a compatible fuzzy 
relation on D. 
 
Proof: Let a, b, c, d, ∈ D. Then we have f –1 (µ) (ac, bd) = µ (f (ac, bd)) = µ (f (a, b) y 
f (c, d) ≥ min {µ (f (a, b), µ (f (c, d)} = min{f –1 (µ) (a, b), f –1 (µ) (c, d)}. 
 
Hence f –1(µ) is a compatible fuzzy relation on D. 
 
THEOREM [44, 45]: If λ is a compatible fuzzy relation on the groupoid D and f is a 
groupoid homomorphism from D × D in to S × S then f(λ) is a compatible fuzzy 
relation on S.

DEFINITION 1.11.15: Let X and Y be two non empty sets. A mapping f : X × X → Y × Y 
is called a semi balanced mapping, if  
 
i. 
Given a ∈ X there exists u ∈ Y such that f (a, a) = (υ, υ). 
ii. 
f(a, a) = (υ, υ) and f(b, b) = (ν, ν) where a, b ∈ X, υ, ν ∈ Y implies that 
f(a, b) = (υ, ν). 
 
One can easily verify the following result for the semi balanced mapping f. If f(a, b) = 
(υ, ν) where a, b ∈ X, υ, ν ∈ Y then  
 
i. 
f(a, a) = (υ, υ) and f(b, b) = (ν, ν).  
ii. 
f(b, a) = (υ, ν) and 
iii. 
given x ∈ X there exists a unique tx ∈ Y such that f(a, x) = (υ, tx ) , f(x, b) = 
(tx , ν) and f(x, x) = (tx , tx ). 
 
We give an example of a semibalanced map. 
 
Example 1.11.14: Let X = {a, b, c} and Y = {υ, ν, ω, r}. Consider the map f : X × X 
→ Y × Y defined by f (a, b) = f (c, b) = (υ, ν),  f (b, a) = f (b, c) = (ν, υ), f (a, c) = f (c, 
a) = f (c, c) = f (a, a) = (υ, υ) and f (b, b) = (υ, υ). Then, f is a semi-balanced map. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.16: If f is a semi-balanced map from X × X into Y × Y and µ is an α-
equivalence fuzzy relation on Y, then f –1(µ) is an α-equivalence fuzzy relation on X. 
 
Proof: Let a, b, ∈ X. Then f –1(µ)(a, a) = µ(f(a, a)) = α, because f(a, a) = (υ, υ) for 
some υ ∈ Y, and f –1(µ)(a, b) = µ(f(a, b)) ≤ α. Thus f –1(µ) is an α- reflexive fuzzy 
relation on X. 
 
Now f –1(µ)(a, b) = µ(υ, ν)(where f(a, b) = (υ, ν)) = µ (ν, υ) (by symmetry of µ) = 
µ(f(b, a)) = f –1(µ) (b, a).
