THEOREM 1.11.17: If µ is an α-congruence fuzzy relation on the groupoid S and f is a 
groupoid homomorphism from D × D into S × S which is a semi balanced map, then   
f –1(µ) is an α-congruence on D. 
 
Proof: Follows by the earlier theorems. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.16: Let f be a map from X × X into Y × Y . A fuzzy relation λ on X is 
f invariant if f (a, b) = f(a1, b1) implies that λ (a, b) = λ (a1, b1). A fuzzy relation λ on 
X is weakly f-invariant if f(a, b) = f(a1, b) implies that λ (a, b) = λ (a1, b). 
 
Clearly if λ is f-invariant then λ is weakly f-invariant but not conversely. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.18: Let f be a semi-balanced map from X × X into Y × Y. If λ is a 
weakly f-invariant symmetric fuzzy relation on X with λ o λ = λ , then λ is f-invariant. 
 
Proof: Let f (a, b) = f (a1, b1) = (υ, ν) where a, a1, b, b1 ∈ X , υ, ν ∈ Y. Given x ∈ X 
by definition there exists a unique tx ∈ Y such that f (x, x) = (tx, tx), f (a, x) = (υ, tx) = 
f (a1, x) and f (x, b) = (tx , ν) = f (x, b1). 
 
But λ is weakly f-invariant so we get λ (a, x) = λ (a1, x) and λ (x, b) = λ (x, b1). 
 
Lastly

Hence λ is f-invariant.  
 
The following theorem can be deduced from the earlier theorems.

THEOREM 1.11.19: Let f be a semi balanced map from X × X into Y × Y. If λ is an α-
equivalence (or G-equivalence) fuzzy relation on X which is weakly f-invariant then λ 
is f-invariant. 
 
DEFINITION [44, 45]: A mapping f : X × X → Y × Y is a balanced mapping if 
 
i. 
f (a, b) = (u, u) ⇒ a = b. 
ii. 
f(a, b) = (u, ν) ⇒ f(b, a) = (ν, u). 
iii. 
f(a, a) = (u, u) and f(b, b) = (ν, ν)⇒ f(a, b) = (u, ν) 
 
for all a, b ∈ X and u, ν, ∈ Y. 
 
A mapping f : X × X → Y × Y is a balanced mapping if and only if it is a one to one 
semi-balanced mapping. 
 
Now we give a condition under which the image of an α-equivalence fuzzy relation is 
an α-equivalence.

We proceed on to recall the definition of G-groupoid and P-fuzzy correspondence. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.18: Let S1, S2 and S3 be non empty sets and A : S1 × S2 → S3 a 
mapping. Then (S1, S2, S3, A) is a G-groupoid . If A1 and A2 are non empty sets and (P, 
≤ ) is a partially ordered set then a function A  : A1 × A2 → P is a binary P-fuzzy 
correspondence. By a P-fuzzy correspondence sometimes we consider a quadruple 
(A1, A2, P, A ).  
 
If A1 …, An are sets and (P, ≤) is a partially ordered set than a mapping A : A1 × …× 
An to P is an (n-ary) P-fuzzy correspondence on sets A1, …, An. Here we recall a 
special type of binary P-fuzzy correspondences such that (A1, ≤), (A2 ≤), …, (An ≤) are 
also partially ordered sets. We call such a correspondence PG- fuzzy correspondence. 
The notion of a binary PG-fuzzy correspondence is a generalization of the notion of a 
G-groupoid, since we obtain a binary PG-fuzzy correspondence when we equip all 
sets in a G-groupoid with orderings. 
 
If A  : A1 × … × An → P is a P-(or PG-) fuzzy correspondence for p ∈ P, a p-level 
correspondence is a function A  : A1 × A2 × … × An → {0, 1} defined by 
p
A (x1,…, xn ) 
= 1 if and only if A (x1, x2, ..., xn ) ≥ p.  
 
By AP we denote the corresponding p-level subset of A1 × A2 × … × An . Further more 
we denote the set of all level subsets of AP by A
p = {Ap | p ∈ P}. 
 
Since a P-fuzzy correspondence is a P-fuzzy set on a product of sets, the following 
results are recalled from [90]. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.32: If A  : A1 × A2 × … × An → P is a P fuzzy correspondence where 
(P, ≤) is a partially ordered set, then for every (x1, x2, …, xn ) ∈ A1 × A2 × … × An the 
following supremum exists in P ∨ (p ∈ P | 
p
A (x1, x2, …, xn ) = 1 and A ( x1, x2,…, xn ) 
= ∨(p ∈ P such that 
p
A (x1, x2, …, xn) = 1}. 
 
Proof: If A (x1, x2, …, xn) = q then 
q
A (x1, x2, …, xn) = 1 . If 
p
A (x1, x2, …, xn) = 1 
then A (x1,  …, xn) = q ≥ p. This means that q greater than all p for which 
p
A (x1, x2, 
…, xn) = 1, that is A  (x1, x2, …, xn) = q = ∨ ( p ∈ P | 
p
A (x1, x2, …, xn) = 1).

i.

q

Let A1, A2, A3, A4 and A5 be partially ordered sets and B : A2 × A3 → A5 and D  : A1 
× A2 → A4, PG fuzzy correspondence. ( A , C ) where A  : A1 × A5 → P and C  : A4 
× A3 → P are PG-fuzzy correspondences and P is a partially ordered set, of the 
functional equation A (x, B(y, z) = C ( D (x, y),z) for given PC-fuzzy correspondences 
Band D . 
 
We see the equation A (x, B(y, z)) = C ( D (x, y), z) always has a nontrivial solution 
for arbitrary B: A2 × A3 → A5 and D : A1 × A2 → A4. Indeed for A  and C  we take 
arbitrary PG-fuzzy correspondences  
 
A  : A1 × A5 → L and C : A4 × A3 → L where L is a distributive lattice (i.e. partially 
ordered set). 
 
THEOREM 1.11.35: Let A1, A2, A3, A4 and A5 be partially ordered set. Further more 
let B : A2 × A3 → A5 and D : A1 × A2 → A4 be PG-fuzzy correspondences which are 
surjections.

Let F be a family of ternary correspondences on A1 × A2 × A3 satisfying the following 
conditions: 
 
i. For every y, t ∈ A2 and z, u ∈ A3, x ∈ A1 and E ∈ F if B (y, z) = B (t, u) 
then E(x, y, z) = E(x, t, u). 
ii. For every x, z ∈ A1, y, t ∈ A2, u ∈ A3 and E ∈ F if D (x, y) = D (z, t) then 
E(x, y, u) = E(z, t, u). 
iii. For all (x, y, z) ∈ A1 × A2 × A3; ∩ (E ∈ F | E(x, y, z) = 1) ∈ F. 
iv. ∪(E / E ∈ F) = A1 × A2 × A3.  
v. For all E ∈ F, let the correspondence AE on A1 × A5 and CE on A4 × A3 be 
defined in the following way: 
 
   AE (x, y) = E(x, z, t) for B (z, t) = y. 
vi. CE (x, y) = E (υ, ν, y) for D (υ, ν) = x, then partially ordered sets F, FA = 
{AE / E ∈ F} and FC = {CE / E ∈ F} are isomorphic under the mappings    
f : FA → F defined f(AE) = E and g : FC → F defined by g(CE) = E.

It is important and interesting to note that PG-fuzzy correspondences such that all 
related partially ordered sets are equal (S, ≤), A  : S × S → S, B: S × S → S, C : S × 
S → S and D : S × S → S are semilattices i.e. groupoids satisfying commutative, 
idempotent and associative laws. We prove that if such A , B, C and D  are solutions 
of the functional equation then they are equal that is A  = B = C  = D .  
 
THEOREM 1.11.37: If (S, A ), (S, B ), (S, C ) and (S, D ) are semi-lattices such that 
for all x, y, z ∈ S, A  (x, B (y, z)) =C ( D (x, y), z) then A = B = C = D . 
 
Proof: Let x, y ∈ S. Then  
 
A (x, y) = A (y, x) = A (y, B(x, x)) = C ( D (y, x), x) = C ( D (x, y), x). 
A (x, B(y, x)) = A (x, B(x, y)) = C ( D (x, x), y) = C (x , y). 
A (x, y) = A (x, B(x, y))  
 
 
=  
A (x, B( B(x, y)), B(x, y)) 
 
=  
C ( D (x, B(x, y), B(x, y)) 
 
=  
C ( D ( B(x, y), x), B(x, y)) 
 
=  
A ( B(x, y), B(x, B(x, y))),  
 
 
=  
A ( B(x, y), B(x, y)). 
 
 
=  
B(x, y). 
 
Similarly we prove C  = D . 
 
This will find several applications in social groups, pattern recognitions, traffic, public 
relations and correspondences. 
 
Now using [110], we recall some results about lattice of all idempotent fuzzy subsets 
of a groupoids. 
 
We know from the definition of a fuzzy subset of a set S originally defined by [144] 
in his classical paper is a mapping from S into the real interval [0, 1]. If λ and µ are 
fuzzy subsets of S then the equality λ = µ and the inclusion λ ⊆ µ are defined 
pointwise, endowed with this partial order the set F(S) of all fuzzy subsets of S form a 
completely distributive lattice. The meet λ ∩ µ and the join λ ∪ µ the arbitrary 
supremum ∪ λi and the arbitrary infimum ∩ λi can be verified to have the following 
formulas. 
 
i. 
(λ ∩ µ) (x) = min {λ (x) , µ (x)}. 
ii. 
(λ ∪ µ) (x) = min {λ(x) , µ(x)}. 
iii. 
(∪ λi ) (x) = supi λ(x). 
iv. 
(∩ λi )(x) = infi λ(x), x ∈ S. 
 
The sup-min product of two fuzzy subsets λ and µ of a groupoid D is the fuzzy subset 
λ. µ of D defined by

Now we proceed on to recall the fuzzy subgroupoid as given by [47]. In this study he 
uses the concept of t-norm as given by [150]. 
 
DEFINITION [150] : A t-normal is a function T : [0,1] × [0,1] → [0,1] satisfying for 
each x, y and z in [0,1]  
 
i. T (x,1) = x. 
ii. T (x, y) ≤ T (z x) if x ≤ z. 
iii. T (x, y) = T (y, x). 
iv. T (x, T(y,z)) = T(T(x, y), z). 
 
A few t-norms which are frequently encountered are Tm , Prod and Min defined by  
Tm (x, y) = Max (x + y –1, 0), Prod (x, y) = xy and 
 
Min (x, y) = 



<
≤
x
y
if
y
y
x
if
x
. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11. 20: A t-norm, T1 is stronger than a t-norm T2 if and only if T1 (x, y) 
≥ T2 (x, y) for all x, y ∈ [0,1]. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.52: Min is the strongest of all t-norms. 
 
Proof: Directly proved.  
 
Let X = (X, y) be a groupoid we will denote x y y by xy. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.21: Let X = (X, y) be a groupoid. A function µ: X → [0, 1] is a fuzzy 
subgroupoid of X with respect to a t-norm T, if and only if for every x, y ∈ X we have 
µ (xy) ≥ T (µ(x), µ(y)). 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.22: Let X1 and X2 be groupoid and let µ1 and µ2 be fuzzy 
subgroupoids of X1 and X2 respectively, with respect to a t-norm T. The fuzzy 
subgroupoids µ1 and µ2 are homomorphic (isomorphic) if and only if there exists a 
groupoid homomorphism (isomorphism) φ: X1 → X2 such that µ1 = µ2 o φ.  
 
In this situation we say that µ1 is given by pull back of µ2 along φ. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.53: Let µ be a fuzzy subgroupoid of X with respect to Min then µ is a 
fuzzy subgroupoid of X with respect to any t-norm T.  
 
Proof: Left as an exercise for the reader. 

 
178
 
DEFINITION 1.11.23: Let (X, y) be a groupoid. Let G denote the family of 
subgroupoids of X. G is called a generating family if for every element x ∈ X there 
exists a S ∈ G such that x ∈ S. {X} is a trivial generating family. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.24: Let (X, y) be a groupoid. An element e ∈ X is called an identity 
of X, if ex = xe = x for all x ∈ X. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.54: Let (X, y) be a groupoid, then X has at most one identity. 
 
Proof: Obvious from the fact if e and e' are two identities e = ee' = e'. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.55: Every groupoid is a subgroupoid of some groupoid with an 
identity. 
 
Proof: Let (X, y) be a groupoid. Let e be an element such that e ∉ X. Define an 
operation 'o' on the set X' = X ∪ {e} as follows x o y = x y y if x, y ∈ X; x o e = e o x 
= x for all x ∈ X, e o e = e. Then obviously (X, o) is a groupoid with identity e and 
(X, y) is a subgroupoid of (X', o). 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.25: We call (X', o, e) the identity extension of (X, y) by e. Let (X, y) 
be a groupoid. By the virtue of the above theorem we may assume that X has an 
identity whenever necessary, without loss of generality. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.56: Let (X, o) be a groupoid then there exists a unique identity 
extension of X up to isomorphism. 
 
Proof: Let (X', o, e) be constructed as in the proof of the earlier theorem. Suppose that 
(X'', , υ) is also an identity extension of X such that X" = X ∪ {u} with identity 
element u. It is easily shown that φ: X' → X" defined by

extension of X.

Now for any x, y ∈ X' if x, y ∈ X then x o y = x y y ∈ X; hence µ'(x o y) = µ(x y y) ≥

It (Ω , A , P) is a probability space then P is infact a fuzzy subset of A . Let Ω = 
[0,1]. Let L be the set of all Lebesgue measurable subsets in [0,1] and P be the 
Lebesque measure then (Ω, L, P) is a probability space. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.58: Let (Ω,A , P) be a probability space then (A , ∩) becomes a 
groupoid and P is a fuzzy subgroupoid of A  with respect to Tm . 
 
Proof: For any A, B ∈ A , we have P(A ∩ B) = P(A) + P(B) – P(A∪B) ≥ P(A) + 
P(B) – 1. Since P(A ∩ B) ≥ 0, we obtain P (A ∩ B) ≥ Max (P(A) + P(A) – 1,0) =  
Tm(P(A), P(B)). 
 
THEOREM 1.11.59: Let (X, y) be a groupoid and let (Ω,A , P) be a probability space. 
Suppose that the mapping, ψ: X → A satisfies ψ (x y) ⊇ ψ(x) ∩ ψ(y). Set µ(x) = 
P(ψ(x)) for all x ∈ X, then µ is a fuzzy subgroupoid of X with respect to Tm. One says 
that µ is induced by the mapping ψ and the probability space (Ω ,A , P). 
 
Proof: For any x, y ∈ X we have ψ (x y) ⊇ ψ(x) ∩ ψ (y) therefore by the above 
theorem it follows that µ(xy) = P (ψ (xy)) ≥ P (ψ (x) ∩ ψ (y)) ≥ Tm (P(ψ(x), P (ψ(y))) 
= Tm(µ(x) , µ(y)). 
 
The following result is direct hence left for the reader to prove. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.60: Let (X, y) be a groupoid and let (Ω,A , P) be a probability space. 
If µ: X → A  is a homomorphism from (X, y) to (A , ∩). Set µ(x) = P (ψ(x)), then µ 
is a fuzzy subgroupoid of X with respect to Tm induced by ψ and (Ω,A , P). 
 
THEOREM 1.11.61: Let (X, y) be a groupoid and G be a generating family of X. For x 
∈ X let Sx = {S ∈ Gx ∈ S} and let W = {Sx x ∈ X}. Let A be any σ -algebra on G 
which contains the σ - algebra generated by W and let m be a probability measure on 
(G, A). Then µ: X → [0,1] defined by µ(x) = m(Sx) for each x ∈ X is a fuzzy 
subgroupoid of X with respect to Tm. A fuzzy subgroupoid obtained in this manner is 
called “subgroupoid generated”. 
 
Proof: Let x, y ∈ X. Suppose that S ∈ Sx ∩ Sy; Then S is a subgroupoid of X 
containing both x and y. Consequently x y ∈ S and hence S ∈ Sx. Therefore Sxy ⊇     
Sx ∩ Sy. Define ψ : X → A by x →Sx. Then ψ (xy) = Sxy ⊃ Sx ∩ Sy = ψ(x) ∩ ψ(y). 
Set µ(x) = m (ψ(x)) = m(Sx) then by earlier theorem µ is a fuzzy subgroupoid of X 
with respect to Tm. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.62: Let (X, y) and all other symbols be the same as described in the 
theorem 1.11.61. If there exists an element e of X such that e is contained in every 
subgroupoid of the generated family G, then µ(e) = 1. 
 

 
180
Proof: Obviously Se = G and hence µ(e) = m(Se) = m(G) = 1.  
 
The following result is straightforward.  
 
THEOREM 1.11.63: Let all symbols be the same as described in the above theorem. If 
(X, y) is a monoid with the identity e then µ(e) = 1.  
 
Further we have a still stronger result.

= µ(x) for all x ∈ X.

THEOREM 1.11.65: Let (Ω,A , P) be a probability space and τ be a non empty set. 
Let φ : Ω → τ be a mapping. Then φ induces a new probability space (τ, A, m) where 
A = {B ⊆ τ φ –1 (B) ∈ A } and for each B ∈ A, m(B) = P (φ –1 (B)). 
 
We say that (τ, A, m) is induced by φ and (Ω,A , P). 
 
THEOREM 1.11.66: Let (Ω , A  , P) be a probability space and τ be a non empty set. 
P(τ) denotes the power set of τ. Let φ : Ω → τ be a mapping. Suppose that (X, y) is a 
groupoid and there exists a mapping ψ : X → P(τ) such that

to Tm. 
 
Proof: By the above result φ and (Ω , A , P) induce a new probability space (τ, A, 
m). Result follows by the earlier theorems.

THEOREM 1.11.67: Let Ω = [0,1] and L be the set of all Lebesgue measurable sets on 
[0,1]. Let P by the Lebesgue measure. Suppose that we have a non empty set τ, a

to Tm.

Now we proceed on to recall properties of a fuzzy subgroupoid of a subgroupoid of a 
direct product. 
  
THEOREM 1.11.68: Let (X, y) be a groupoid and let Y be a fixed subgroupoid of X. 
Let (Ω , A , P) be a probability space and let (F,) be a groupoid of functions 
mappings Ω into X and  defined by pointwise multiplication in the range space. A 
further restriction is placed on F by assuming that for each f ∈ F, Xf = {w ∈ Ω | f(w) 
∈ Y} is an element of A . Then ν : F → [0,1] defined by ν (f) = P(Xf ) for each f in F 
is a fuzzy subgroupoid of F with respect to Tm. A fuzzy subgroupoid obtained in this 
manner is called “ function generated”. 
 
Proof: Let f, g ∈ F. Suppose ω ∈ Xf ∩ Xg. Then f(ω) ∈ Y. Since Y is a subgroupoid 
of X. (f o g)(ω) = f(ω) o g(ω)∈ Y and consequently ω ∈ Xfog. Therefore Xfog ⊇ Xf ∩ 
Xg. Now consider the mapping ψ : F → A . f → Xf. We have ψ (f o g) = Xfog ⊇ Xf ∩ 
Xg = ψ (f) ∩ ψ(g). Set ν(f) = P (ψ(f)) = P(Xf) for all f ∈ F, then by earlier results ν is 
a fuzzy subgroupoid of F with respect to Tm. 
 
It is pertinent to mention that for each ω ∈ Ω by defining Xω = X, we can construct a 
direct product 
ω
Ω
∈
ωΠ X  then (F, ) is in fact a subgroupoid of 
ω
Ω
∈
ωΠ X . 
 
If f ∈ F and f(ω) ∈ Y, for all ω ∈ Ω then ν (f) = 1. 
 
Now we recall the fuzzy subgroupoid representations. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.69: Every function generated fuzzy subgroupoid is subgroupoid 
generated. 
 
Proof: The proof is lengthy the reader is requested to refer [47].  
 
The proof of the following theorem is omitted and the reader is advised to refer [47]. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.70: Every subgroupoid generated fuzzy subgroupoid is isomorphic to 
a function generated fuzzy subgroupoid. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.71: Let (X, y) be a groupoid with the identity e and let ν be a fuzzy 
subgroupoid of X with respect to Min such that ν(e) = 1. For each t in [0,1] let Xt = 
{x ∈ X, ν (x) ≥ t}. Let a and b be in [0,1] such that a ≤ b; then  
 
i. Xt is a subgroupoid of X for every t in [0,1]. 
ii. Xb ⊆ Xa. 
iii. if x ∈ Xa – Xb for all b in (a, 1]; then ν (x) = a. 
 
Proof:  
 
i. For each t in [0,1] since ν(e) = 1 ≥ t we have e ∈ Xt. Hence Xt is not empty. 
For any x, y ∈ Xt it follows that ν(x) ≥ t, ν(y) ≥ t. Therefore ν(xy) ≥ 
Min{ν(x), ν(y)}≥ t; hence x y ∈ Xt. This shows that Xt is a subgroupoid of X. 

 
182
ii. For any x ∈ Xb we have ν(x) ≥ b. Since b ≥ a it follows that ν (x) ≥ a and so x 
∈ Xa. Hence Xb ⊆ Xa. 
 
iii. If x ∈ Xa – Xb for all b in (a, 1] then we have a ≤ ν (x) < b for all a < b ≤ 1. 
Hence ν(x) = a.  
 
Finally we state the following two theorems from [47] the proof is left for the reader 
to refer. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.72: Every fuzzy subgroupoid with respect to Min is subgroupoid 
generated. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.73: Let µ be a subgroupoid generated fuzzy subgroupoid with (X, y) 
and (G, A, m) and set Sx for x ∈ X, as described in the theorem 1.11.72. If there exists 
G* ∈ A which is linearly ordered by set inclusion such that m(G*) = 1 then µ is a 
fuzzy subgroupoid with respect to Min. 
 
The converse of the above theorem holds if (X, y) has an identity e such that µ (e) = 1. 
In this case we set G* = {χt | t ∈ [0,1]} then from the earlier result it is obvious that 
G* is linearly ordered with measure one. 
 
Some more algebraic properties are discussed in the following:

It is evident that fuzzy subgroupoids of X with respect to a t-norm T form a complete 
lattice. In this lattice, the inf of a set of fuzzy subgroupoids µi with respect to T is just 
∩µi, while their sup is the least µ (i.e. the ∩ of all µ's) which ⊇ ∪ µi, where ∪µi (x) = 
sup µi (x) for all x ∈ X. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.27: Let f: X → X' be a homomorphism from groupoid X into 
groupoid X'. Suppose that ν is a fuzzy subgroupoid of X' with respect to a t-norm T. 
Then the fuzzy set µ = ν o f (defined by µ (x) = ν (f(x)) for all x ∈ X) is called the pre 
image of ν under f. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.75: Let µ = ν o f be the preimage of ν under f as described in the 
above definition, then µ is a fuzzy subgroupoid of X with respect to T. 
 
Proof: For x, y ∈ X we have µ(xy) = (ν o f) (x y) = ν (f(x y)) = ν (f(x), f(y) ≥ 
T(ν(f(x)), ν(f(y))) = T (µ(x), µ(y)). So by definition µ is a fuzzy subgroupoid of X 
with respect to T. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.28: Let f: X → X' be a homomorphism from groupoid X onto 
groupoid X'. Suppose that µ is a fuzzy subgroupoid of X with respect to a t-norm T, 
then the fuzzy set ν in X' = f(X) defined by

Now we proceed on to define fuzzy bigroupoid. The concept of bigroupoid is itself 
very new so the notion is fuzzy bigroupoid is totally absent in literature. Here we 
define fuzzy bigroupoid and recall just the definition of bigroupoid. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.29: Let (G, +, y) be a non empty set we call G a bigroupoid if G = 
G1 ∪ G2 and satisfies the following: 
 
i. 
(G1 , +) is a groupoid. 
ii. 
(G2 , y) is a semigroup. 
 
Example 1.11.16: Let (G, +, y) be a groupoid where G = G1 ∪ G2 with G1 a groupoid 
given by the following table: 
 
 

 
185
 
+ 
X1 
X2 
X3 
X1 
X1 
X3 
X2 
X2 
X2 
X1 
X3 
X3 
X3 
X2 
X1 
 
And G2 = S(3) the symmetric semigroup of mappings of (123) to itself.  
 
Clearly (G, +, y) is a bigroupoid. 
 
Now we define fuzzy bigroupoid as follows. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.30: Let (G, +, y) be a bigroupoid; G = G1 ∪ G2 proper subsets of 
G; with (G1, +) a groupoid and G2 a semigroup. µ : G → [0, 1] is said to be a fuzzy 
bisubgroupoid (or by default of notation fuzzy bigroupoid) if and only if µ = µ1 ∪ µ2 
where µ1 from G1 → [0, 1] is a fuzzy subgroupoid and µ2: G2 → [0, 1] is a fuzzy 
subsemigroup. 
 
Thus almost all properties true in case of fuzzy groupoids and fuzzy semigroups can 
be easily extended to the case of fuzzy bisubgroupoids. 
 
Now we proceed on to define fuzzy loops and fuzzy biloops. 
 
DEFINITION [118]: Let G be a group. A fuzzy subset µ : G → [0, 1] is called a fuzzy 
subloop if for at least a triple x, y, z ∈ G we have µ((xy)z) ≠ µ(x(yz)). 
 
Study of this type was carried out by [118].  
 
Since all groups are loops we can define fuzzy subloop in a more generalized way as  
 
DEFINITION [118]: Let L be a loop A: L → [0, 1] is a fuzzy subloop of L if atleast for 
a triple x, y z ∈ L we have A((xy)z) ≠ A(x(yz)). 
 
DEFINITION [118]: Let L be a group or a loop. The fuzzy subloop V: L → [0, 1] is 
called a fuzzy Bruck subloop if P (x (yx) z) = P(x(y(xz))) and P (xy)–1 = P (x–1 y –1) for 
all x, y ∈ L. 
 
DEFINITION [118]: Let G be a group or a loop. A fuzzy subloop P of G (P: G → [0, 1] 
is a fuzzy Bol subloop of G if P(((xy)z)y) = P(x((yz))y) for all x, y, z ∈ G.  
 
DEFINITION [118]: Let L be a loop or a group. A fuzzy subloop P: L → [0, 1] is 
called a fuzzy Moufang subloop of L if P((xy(zx)) = P((x(yz))x) for all x, y, z ∈ L. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.77: Every fuzzy subloop of a loop L need not in general be a fuzzy 
Moufang subloop of L. 
 
Proof: Consider the loop L given by the following table:

V((1(42)1)) = 0 = V(0) . So V is not fuzzy Moufang subloop of L. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.78: Let L be a loop or a group. The fuzzy subloop V of L → [0 1] is a 
right alternative fuzzy subloop of L if V((xy)y) = V(x(yy)) for all x, y ∈ L. 
 
Proof: Straightforward by the very definition. 
 
THEOREM 1.11.79: Let L be a loop or a group. A fuzzy subloop V of L is a weak 
inverse property loop if V((xy)z) = 0 imply V(x(yz)) = 0 for all x, y, z ∈ L. 
 
Proof: Given V: L → [0, 1] is a fuzzy subloop of L, clearly if V((xy)z) = 0 imply 
V(x(yz)) = 0 then it is the weak inverse property as the result is true for all x, y, z ∈ L 
we have V to be a fuzzy subloop which satisfies weak inverse property. 
 
As we are not able to find any other means to define fuzzy subloop as to the best of 
the authors knowledge we do not have any other definition for fuzzy subloops we take 
this as the basic definition. Also we wish to mention the definition of fuzzy subloops 
given by us is distinctly different from the classical definitions of other algebraic 
structure. 
 
Also it is pertinent to mention here that all fuzzy subloops are not subloops for they 
do not satisfy the axiom of a subloop. 
 
Now we proceed on to define the notion of fuzzy biloop; for which first we give the 
definition of biloop. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.31: Let (L, +, y) be a non empty set with two binary operations. L is 
said to be a biloop if L has 2 nonempty finite proper subsets L1 and L2 of L such that  
 
i. 
L = L1 ∪ L2. 
ii. 
(L1, +) is a loop. 
iii. 
(L2, y) is a loop or a group. 
 
We define fuzzy biloop and request the reader to refer [135] for more properties about 
biloops. 
 
DEFINITION 1.11.32: Let (L, +, y) be a biloop. A map µ : L → [0, 1] is called fuzzy 
biloop if we have (L = L1 ∪ L2 ) with (L1 ,+) a loop and (L2 , y) group or a loop) µ = 
µ1∪ µ2 where µ1 denotes the fuzzy subloop of L1 and µ2 is the fuzzy subloop of L2 (i.e. 

 
187
µ restricted to L1 is denoted by µ1 and µ restricted to L2 denoted by µ2 the symbol '∪ ' 
in µ = µ1 ∪ µ2 is only by default of notation). 
 
All fuzzy properties studied for loops can be easily extended to fuzzy biloops in an 
analogous and routine ways. 
 
 
1.12 Miscellaneous Properties in Fuzzy algebra  
 
This section is devoted to the miscellaneous properties about fuzzy algebra which has 
not been covered in the earlier eleven sections like fuzzy continuous map on groups, 
fuzzy polynomial ring, fuzzy polynomial semiring, and finally the concept of fuzzy 
opposite sets and systems. Now we proceed on to recall the concept of fuzzy 
continuous map on groups. For more about these refer [139]. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.1: A fuzzy topology τ on a group G is called a g fuzzy topology. The 
pair (G, τ) is called a g-fuzzy topological space. 
 
Example 1.12.1: Let G = {1, –1} be the group with respect to usual multiplication 
and τ = {φG , 1G, λ, µ} where λ, µ : G → [0, 1] are given by

union of members of τ is a member of τ. Hence τ is a g-fuzzy topology on G. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.2: Let τ1 and τ2 be g-fuzzy topologies on the group G1 and G2 
respectively. A function f : (G1 τ1) → (G2 τ2) is said to be a g-fuzzy continuous map 
from G1 to G2 if it satisfies the following conditions:

For every µ ∈ τ2, f –1(µ) ∈ τ1 and 
ii.

G1) in τ1 .

Now we proceed on to define g-fuzzy homeomorphism. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.3: Let (G1, τ1) and (G2, τ2) be any two g-fuzzy topological spaces. A 
function f: (G1, τ1) → (G2, τ2) is said to be a g-fuzzy homeomorphism if it satisfies the 
following three conditions. 
 
i. f is one to one and onto. 
ii. f is a g-fuzzy continuous map from G1 to G2 and  
iii. f –1 is a g-fuzzy continuous map from G2 to G1. 
 
We now give an example of a g-fuzzy homeomorphism.

We know that a fuzzy subset of a set X is said to be a fuzzy point if and only if it 
takes the value 0 for all y ∈ X. expect on one element, say, x ∈ X.  
 
If its value at x is t (0 < t ≤ 1) then we denote this fuzzy point by x1. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.4: A fuzzy subset µ in a g-fuzzy topological space, (G, τ) is called a 
Qg-neighborhood of the fuzzy point xt (for x ∈ G) if and only if there exists fuzzy 
subgroup λ (of G) in τ such that λ ⊂ µ and xt q λ (where xt q λ means t+ λ (x) > 1 and 
xt is quasi coincident with λ).

DEFINITION 1.12.5: A g-fuzzy topological space (G, τ) is said to be a g-fuzzy 
Housdorff space if and only if for any two fuzzy points xt and ys (x, y ∈ G and x ≠ y) 
there exist Qg - neighborhoods λ and µ of xt and ys respectively such that λ ∩ µ = φG.

We have nice results analogous to usual polynomial rings. 
 
THEOREM 1.12.5: Let R[x[0, 1]] be the polynomial ring over the field of reals R. Then 
R[x[0, 1]] is an infinite dimensional vector space over R. 
 
Proof: Follows from the fact R[x[0, 1]] is an additive abelian group for any fuzzy 
polynomial ring and we have for all r ∈ R and p(x) ∈ R[x[0, 1]],  rp(x) ∈ R[x[0, 1]]. 
R[x[0, 1]] is a vector space over R since the interval [0, 1] is of infinite cardinality we 
say R[x[0, 1]] is a vector space with infinite basis.  
 
Can we have fuzzy polynomial rings which are finite dimensional or has a finite 
basis? The answer is yes. 
 
Example 1.12.7: Let R be the ring of integers R[x[0, 1]] = {all polynomial formed by x0 
= 1 x0.2, x0.4, x0.6, x0.8 and x with coefficients from R} = {α0 + α1x0.2 + α2x0.4 + α3x0.6 
+ α4x0.8 + α5x | α1, α2, α3, α4, α5 ∈ R}.  
 
Clearly R[x[0, 1]] is a finite dimensional vector space over R. 
 
Dimension of R[x[0, 1]] is 6. One of the basis is [1, x0.2, x0.4, x0.6,x0.8 and x] 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.8: Let R[x[0, 1]] be a fuzzy polynomial ring. A non empty subset P of 
R[x[0, 1]] is said to be a fuzzy polynomial subring of R[x[0, 1]] if P itself is a fuzzy 
polynomial ring under the operations inherited from R[x[0, 1]].  
 
One more major difference between a polynomial ring and fuzzy polynomial ring is 
that R[x[0, 1]] is not an integral domain even if R is a field. 
 
THEOREM 1.12.6: Let R be a field. R[x[0, 1]] be a fuzzy polynomial ring R[x[0, 1]] is not 
a field or an integral domain. 
 
Proof: To show R[x[0, 1]] is not a field or an integral domain we have to show R[x[0, 1]] 
contains two polynomials 0 ≠ p(x), 0 ≠ q(x) ∈ R[x[0, 1]] with p(x) o q(x) = 0. Take p(x) 
= x + x0.5 and q(x) = 1 – x0.5. 
 
p(x) o q(x)  
= 
(x + x0.5) (1 – x0.5) 
  
    
=  
x + x0.5 – x0.5 – x1 
 
  
= 
x + x0.5 – x0.5 – x 
= 
0. 
 
Hence the claim. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.9: Let R[x[0, 1]] be a fuzzy polynomial ring. A proper subset I of 
R[x[0, 1]] is said to be a fuzzy polynomial ideal of the fuzzy polynomial ring if  
 
i. I is a fuzzy polynomial subring of R[x[0, 1]] 
ii. For every p(x) ∈ R[x[0, 1]] and q(x) ∈ I;  p(x) o q(x) ∈ I.

We say p is a fuzzy semifield if in p(x y) = p(x) y  p(y) = 0 i.e. p(x) y  p(y) = 0 if and 
only if p(x) = 0 or p(y) = 0. 
 
It is important to note that if R is taken as a semiring instead of a ring then any 
semiring homomorphism p from R to [0, 1] is a fuzzy subsemiring. If p is not a 
homomorphism then p is not a semiring. 
 
We have for p : R → [0, 1] which is a fuzzy subsemiring then p is said to be a strict 
fuzzy semiring if x + y = 0 implies x = 0 and y = 0 then p(x) + p(y) = 0 implies p(x) = 
0 and p(y) = 0. 
 
Once again we mention the fuzzy semiring in the usual sense has been carried out in 
the section on fuzzy semirings. Here we devote to see fuzzy semiring in a different 
perspective. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.13: Let V = [0, 1] be a fuzzy semifield. An additive abelian 
semigroup P with 0 is said to be a fuzzy semivector space over [0, 1] if for all x, y ∈ P 
and c ∈ [0, 1]; c x and x c ∈ P i.e. c[x + y] = c x + c y ∈ P. In short [0, 1] P ⊆ P and 
P [0, 1] ⊆ P. 
 
We define for the fuzzy semivector space defined in this manner the fuzzy semivector 
transformation. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.14: Let V be a semivector space over a semifield. Let F and P be 
fuzzy semivector spaces over [0, 1]. A map p : V → P is called a fuzzy semivector 
transformation if for all v ∈ V, p(ν) ∈ V. For every c ∈ F, p(c) ∈ [0, 1] such that   
p(cv + u) = p(c)p(ν) + p(u) where p(c) ∈ [0, 1] ; p(u), p(ν) ∈ P.

DEFINITION 1.12.15: Let P be a fuzzy semivector space over [0, 1]. The fuzzy 
dimension of P over [0, 1] is the minimum number of elements in P required to 
generate P. 
 
As in case of semivector spaces [134] several results in this direction can be 
developed and defined. But as in case of classical fuzzy semivector space we do not 
view fuzzy semivector spaces as a fuzzy subset. As once again we wish to state that 
our main motivation is the study of Smarandache fuzzy algebra we leave the fuzzy 
algebra development to the reader. 
 
Now we proceed on to define a system called fuzzy opposite sets and systems. This 
system happens to be opposite of the system [0, 1] denoted [0, 1] opposite. Here 1 
happens to be the least element and 0 happens to be largest element i.e. for example 
0.99 is an element closer to largest element 1 in the classical fuzzy sets and systems 
but in fuzzy opposite sets and systems 0.99 is an element closer to the least element. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.16: Let S be a set. A fuzzy subset A of S is a function A : S → [0, 1] . 
The fuzzy opposite subset or opposite fuzzy subset for the same function. A denoted by 
Aopp is a map from S to [0, 1]opp. 
 
In most of the algebraic developments the opposite fuzzy subset which is a fuzzy 
subgroup or a fuzzy subring or a fuzzy ideal or a fuzzy module or a fuzzy vector space 
happens to give way to fuzzy opposite structures i.e. if A is a fuzzy subgroup of S with 
a modification Aopp happens to be opposite fuzzy subgroup. 
 
DEFINITION 1.12.17: Let G be a group. A fuzzy subset A of G is a fuzzy subgroup of 
G. If A : G → [0, 1] such  
 
i. A (x y) ≥ min{A(x), A(y)} for all x, y ∈ G.  
ii. A(x) = A(x –1) for all x ∈ G. 
 
Now Aopp is a fuzzy opposite subgroup if Aopp : G → [0, 1] such that  
 
i. Aopp(x y) ≤ max {A(x), A(y)}  
ii. Aopp(x) = Aopp(x –1) for all x, y ∈ G. 
 
Thus the notions of fuzzy normality and other notions can be developed using Aopp 
given A to be fuzzy algebraic structure. 

 
200
 
DEFINITION 1.12.18: Let A be a fuzzy subset of S i.e. A : S → [0, 1]. For t ∈ [0, 1] the 
set At = {x ∈ SA(x) ≥ t} is called the level subset of the fuzzy subset A. Now Aopp is a 
fuzzy subset of the set S. For t ∈ [0, 1]opp, the set At
opp = {x ∈ SA(x) ≤  t} is called 
the level subset of the fuzzy opposite subset A.  
 
Most results which hold good for fuzzy level subset can be carried out for level subset 
of fuzzy opposite subset A.

PART TWO

 
202
 
 
Here we study Smarandache Fuzzy Algebra. 

 
203
Chapter Two 
 
SMARANDACHE FUZZY SEMIGROUPS AND 
ITS PROPERTIES 
 
This chapter has five sections. In section one we give the definition of Smarandache 
fuzzy semigroup, describe several Smarandache definitions and give results. In 
section two we study the Smarandache fuzzy semigroups. Here we have given 28 
Smarandache fuzzy definitions of a S-semigroup and we have developed these 
properties in about 67 theorems. Section three analyzes about elementwise S-fuzzy 
properties in S-semigroups. About 30 new definitions in this direction are given and 
the properties enunciated in 59 theorems. The fourth section is devoted to the study of 
Smarandache fuzzy bisemigroups and bigroups. Thirty-two new definitions about S-
fuzzy semigroups are introduced and its properties are analyzed in 23 theorems. The 
chief attraction is the fifth section which proposes 54 problems for the reader to solve.  
 
 
2.1. Definition of Smarandache fuzzy semigroups with examples: 
 
In this section we for the first time introduce the concept of Smarandache fuzzy 
semigroups. Smarandache semigroups are thoroughly studied in the year [123]. Fuzzy 
semigroups were introduced in the late 1970s. To get the concept of Smarandache 
fuzzy semigroups we go for the concept of fuzzy groups; as Smarandache groups do 
not exist in literature we get almost all properties of groups to be present in 
Smarandache semigroups. Just for the sake of reader we recall the definition of 
Smarandache semigroup then proceed on to define Smarandache fuzzy semigroup and 
give examples. 
 
DEFINITION 2.1.1: Let S be a semigroup. S is said to be a Smarandache semigroup 
(S-semigroup) if S has a proper subset P such that P is a group under the operations 
of S. 
 
Example 2.1.1: Let S(3) be a permutation semigroup. S(3) is a S-semigroup as          
S3 ⊂ S(3) and S3 is the permutation group of degree 3. 
 
Example 2.1.2: Let Mn×n = {(aij)aij ∈ Q} be the set of all n × n matrices with entries 
from Q. Mn×n is a semigroup under matrix multiplication. Pn×n = {
}

is a proper subset of Mn ×n which is a group. So Mn×n is a S-semigroup. 
 
The semigroup given in example 2.1.1 is a non-commutative S-semigroup of finite 
order where as in example 2.1.2 the semigroup is an infinite non-commutative S-
semigroup. 
 

 
204
Example 2.1.3: Let Z20 = {0, 1, 2, …, 19} be a semigroup under multiplication. 
Clearly,  Z20  is  a  S-semigroup  for  P  = {1, 19} is a subgroup in Z20.   This  is  a  
commutative  S-semigroup of finite order. 
 
Example 2.1.4: Let Q[x] be the ring of polynomials; Q[x] is a semigroup under 
multiplication. P = Q \ {0} ⊂ Q[x] is a group under multiplication; so Q[x] is an 
infinite commutative S-semigroup. 
 
As our main motivation in this paper is the study of Smarandache fuzzy semigroup we 
stop at this stage and request the reader to refer [123] for complete information 
regarding S-semigroup. 
 
Fuzzy semigroups are dealt in chapter one section ten. Now we proceed on to define 
Smarandache fuzzy semigroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.1.2: Let S be a S-semigroup. A fuzzy subset A of S is said to be a 
Smarandache fuzzy semigroup (S-fuzzy semigroup) if A : S → [0, 1] is such that A 
restricted to atleast one subset P of S which is a subgroup is a fuzzy group. That is for 
all x, y ∈ P ⊂ S. A(x, y) ≥ min {A(x) , A(y)} and A(x) = A(x–1) for all x in P. 
 
This S-fuzzy semigroup is denoted by AP i.e. AP : P → [0, 1] is a fuzzy group. 
 
Thus in case of S-fuzzy semigroup we face several situations which are enlisted below. 
Here S denotes a S-semigroup. Suppose P1, P2, …, Pn are proper subsets of S which 
are groups under the operations of S. 
 
A fuzzy subset A : S → [0, 1] may be a S-fuzzy semigroup or sometimes may not be    
S-fuzzy semigroup. So we call all fuzzy subsets from S to [0, 1] which are not S-fuzzy 
semigroups where S is a S-semigroup as Smarandache non-fuzzy semigroup (S-non-
fuzzy semigroup). 
 
If A : S → [0, 1] such that there exist atleast one subset Pi ⊂ S which is a group and 
iP
A is a fuzzy subgroup then we call the fuzzy subset P a S-fuzzy semigroup. 
 
If the fuzzy subset A : S → [0, 1] in such that 
iP
A : Pi → [0, 1], for i = 1, 2, …, n are 
fuzzy subgroups then we call the fuzzy subset A : S → [0, 1] a Smarandache strong 
fuzzy semigroup (S-strong fuzzy semigroup). 
 
Thus we see in general a fuzzy subset A : S → [0, 1] where S is a S-semigroup may be 
a S-fuzzy semigroup or a S-strong fuzzy semigroup or S-non-fuzzy semigroup.

2.2. Substructures of S-fuzzy semigroups and their properties 
 
In this section we introduce the notion of S-fuzzy ideals in a S-fuzzy semigroup and 
also define and introduce several properties enjoyed by fuzzy groups as all S-fuzzy 
semigroups contain S-fuzzy subgroups. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.1: Let G be a S-semigroup. µ :G → [0, 1] is said to be a 
Smarandache fuzzy ideal (S-fuzzy ideal) of the S-fuzzy semigroup µ if µ(x) = µ(yxy–1) 
for all x, y ∈ A where A ⊂ G and A is a subgroup of G. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.2: Let G be a S-semigroup and µ :G → [0, 1] be a S-fuzzy semigroup 
of G. For t ≤ µ (e), t ∈ [0, 1] the set µt = {x ∈ Aµ(x) ≥ t} is called the Smarandache 
level semigroup (S-level semigroup) of the S-fuzzy semigroup µ where A ⊂ G and A is 
the subgroup of the S-semigroup G. 
 
The following results are important. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.1: Let G be a S-semigroup with A ⊂ G is a proper subset of G which is 
a subgroup of G µ : G → [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy semigroup if and only if µ (xy–1) ≥ min 
(µ(x), µ(y)) for all x, y ∈ A. 
 
Proof: The result is straightforward by the very definitions of fuzzy subgroup,           
S-semigroup and S-fuzzy semigroup. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.2: Let G be a S-semigroup. For t ≤ µ (e), t ∈ [0, 1] for a given t and 
given G and fixed µ we can have several S-level semigroups depending on the number 
of subgroups A in G.  
 
DEFINITION 2.2.3: Let G be a S-semigroup. If A be a proper subset of G which is a 
subsemigroup of G and A contains the largest subgroup of G; then we call the fuzzy 
subset µ : G → [0, 1] to be a Smarandache fuzzy hyper subsemigroup (S-fuzzy 

 
208
hypersub semigroup) if µ restricted to P, i.e. µ : P → [0, 1], P ⊂ A and P is the 
largest subgroup of A is a fuzzy subgroup of G.

DEFINITION 2.2.4: Let G be a S-semigroup. We say G is a Smarandache fuzzy simple 
semigroup (S-fuzzy simple semigroup) if G has no S-fuzzy hyper subsemigroup. 
 
Now we proceed on to define Smarandache fuzzy symmetric semigroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.5: Let S(n) denote the symmetric semigroup i.e. the semigroup of all 
mapping of a set of n elements {1, 2, …, n} to itself S(n) under composition of 
mappings is a semigroup. 
 
Let  SF(S(n))  denote  the  set  of  all  Smarandache fuzzy subsemigroups  of  S(n).  If 
µ ∈ SF(S(n)) then Im µ = { f(x) x ∈ A ⊂ S(n)} where A is a proper subset of S(n) 
which is a subgroup of S(n) under the operations of S(n) Let µ, σ ∈ SF (S(n)) we say 
if |Im(µ)| < |Im(σ)| then we write µ < σ. By this rule we can define max SF (S(n)). Let 
f be a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of S(n). If f = max SF(S(n)) then we say that f is a 
Smarandache fuzzy symmetric semigroup of S(n) (S-fuzzy symmetric semigroup of 
S(n)). 
 
Now we introduce a new concept called Smarandache co-fuzzy symmetric semigroup. 
To do this we just recall the definition of co-fuzzy symmetric group. 
 
DEFINITION [139]: Let G (Sn) = {g g is a fuzzy subgroup of Sn and g(C(Π)) is a 
constant for every Π ∈ Sn} where C (Π) is the conjugacy class of Sn containing Π , 
which denotes the set of all y ∈ Sn such that y = x Π x–1 for x ∈ Sn. If g = max G(Sn) 
then we call g as co-fuzzy symmetric subgroup of Sn . 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.6: Let SG (S(n)) = {g g is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of S(n) and  
g(C(Π))  is  constant  for  every Π  ∈  Sn  }  where  C (Π)  is  the  conjugacy class of  

 
209
Sn ⊂ S(n) containing Π  which  denotes  the set of all y ∈ Sn such that y = x Π x–1 (for 
x ∈ Sn). If g = max SG(S(n)) then we call g as Smarandache co-fuzzy symmetric 
subsemigroup of S(n) (S-co-fuzzy symmetric subsemigroup of S(n)). 
 
THEOREM 2.2.4: Every S-co-fuzzy symmetric subsemigroup of a symmetric 
semigroup S(n) is a S-fuzzy symmetric subsemigroup of the symmetric semigroup S(n). 
 
Proof: Follows from the definitions of S-fuzzy symmetric subsemigroup and S-co 
fuzzy symmetric subsemigroup.  
 
Now we proceed on to define yet another new concept called Smarandache fuzzy 
normal subsemigroup of a S-semigroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.7: A  S-fuzzy subsemigroup  µ  of  a  S-semigroup  G  is  said  to  be  
a  Smarandache fuzzy normal subsemigroup (S-fuzzy normal subsemigroup)  of  the  
S-semigroup G if µ(xy) = µ (yx) for all x, y ∈ A, A any proper subset of G which is a 
subgroup and µ restricted to A i.e. µ : A → [0, 1] is a fuzzy subgroup of A. 
 
Now we define Smarandache fuzzy cosets of a S-semigroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.8: Let  µ  be  a  S-fuzzy subsemigroup  of  S-semigroup  G.  For  any 
a ∈ A ⊂ G (where A is the subgroup associated with this µ), a µ defined by (aµ)(x) = 
µ(a–1x) for every x ∈ A is  called  the  Smarandache  fuzzy  coset  (S-fuzzy coset) of 
the S-semigroup (S-fuzzy coset of the S-semigroup) G determined by a and µ. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.9: Let  µ  be  a  S-fuzzy subsemigroup  of  a  S-semigroup  G  and H 
= {x ∈ A ⊂ Gµ(x) = µ(0)} (where µ is the associated S-fuzzy subsemigroup with A) 
then o(µ), order of µ is defined as o(µ) = o(H). 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.10: Let λ and µ be two S-fuzzy subsemigroup of a S-semigroup G. 
Then λ and µ are said to be Smarandache  conjugate  fuzzy  subsemigroups               
(S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups) of G (relative to the same A) if for some g ∈ A ⊂ G 
(A proper subset of G which is a subgroup) we have λ(x) = µ(g–1xg) for every x ∈ A. 
 
The main result on S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups of a S-semigroup G is as 
follows. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.5: If λ and µ are S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups relative to a 
subgroup A ⊂ G; where G is a S-semigroup, then o(λ) = o(µ). 
 
Proof: Let λ and µ be S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups of the S-semigroup G. By the 
very  definition  of  S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups of the S-semigroup there exists 
g ∈ A ⊂ G such that λ(x) = µ(

To prove that o(λ) = o(µ) it is sufficient to prove o(H) = o(K), by the definition of the 
order of the fuzzy subgroup of the subgroup A. 
 
To prove that o(H) = o(K) as the first step we prove H ⊂ gKg–1 for some g ∈ A ⊂ G. 
In the second step we prove that for the same g ∈A; K ⊂ g–1Hg. 
 
Let x be any element in H. Since λ and µ are S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups of the 
subgroup A ⊂ G (equivalently of the S-semigroup G) we have for some g ∈ G. 
 
    µ (g–1x g)    = λ(x)  
= λ(e) (since x ∈ H) 
= µ(g–1eg) (since λ and µ are S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups of G) 
= µ (e). 
 
Hence there exists g ∈ A such that µ (g–1x g) = µ(e). Now by the definition of K we 
have g–1x g ∈ K implies x ∈ gKg–1. Therefore H ⊆ gKg–1. 
 
To prove the other inclusion:   Take  x  an  arbitrary element in K. Since λ and µ are 
S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups of the S-semigroup G for the same g ∈ A ⊆ G (A a 
proper subset which is a subgroup of the S-semigroup and λ and µ are conjugate 
fuzzy subgroup relative to this A ⊂ G; i.e. S-conjugate fuzzy subsemigroups of G) 
used in the earlier result to prove H ⊆ g K g–1 we have  
 
λ (gxg–1) = µ (x) 
 
= µ (e) (since x ∈ K)  
 
= λ(geg–1) (since λ and µ are conjugate fuzzy subgroups of A ⊂ G)  
 
= λ(e). 
 
Hence gxg–1 ∈ H for the same g ∈ A ⊆ G, that is x ∈ g–1H g. Hence K ⊆ g–1 H g.  
 
From the first and the second steps we have H ⊆ gKg–1 and K ⊆ g–1Hg, i.e. Hg ⊆ gK 
and gK ⊂ Hg Thus Hg = gK so H = gKg–1. Since K is a subgroup of the group A 
contained in the S-semigroup G we have o(xKx–1) = o(K) for every x ∈ A. Now 
choose x = g. Then we have o (gKg–1) = o(K). Therefore o(H) = o(K). Hence o(λ) = 
o(µ). Hence the theorem. 
 
Recall G a S-semigroup. A and B are S-fuzzy subsemigroups of G related to the 
subgroup P in G (P ⊂ G, P a proper subset of G which is a group) such that B ⊆ A. 
Let xt ⊆ A. Then the fuzzy subset xt o B (B o xt) is called the Smarandache fuzzy left 
(right) coset (S-fuzzy left (right) coset) of B in A with representative xt where by the 
operation ‘o’ we mean the following for all x ∈ P (xt o B) (x) = sup {inf {xt (y), B(z)} 
such that x = yz}. 
 
We give the following results which can be easily extended to S-fuzzy left (right) 
cosets in an analogous way.

THEOREM 2.2.6: Let G be a S-semigroup. A be a S-fuzzy semigroup of G relative to a 
subgroup P ⊂ G. B be a S-fuzzy subsemigroup relative to P, such that B ⊂ A. Then for 
all z ∈ P, (xt o B) (x) = inf {t, B (x–1z)} and (B o xt) (z) = inf {t, B (z x–1)} for xt ∈ A. 
 
Proof: As in case of fuzzy subgroups the proof is straightforward. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.7: Let A, B, and G as in the Theorem 2.2.6 Let xt, ys ∈ A. Then  
 
i. 
(xt o B) = (ys o B) if and only if inf {t, (B(e)} = inf {s, B (y–1x)} and                 
inf {s, B(e)} = inf {t, B (x–1y)}. 
ii. 
B o xt = B o ys if and only if inf {t, B(e) } = inf {s, B(xy–1)} and inf {s, B(e)} 
= inf {t, B (yx–1)}. 
 
Proof: Straightforward; hence left for the reader as an exercise. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.8: Let A, B, G be as in Theorem 2.2.6 Let xt, yt ⊆ A. If B(y–1x) = B(e) 
then xt o B = yt o B. 
 
Proof: Since B(x–1y) = B(y–1x) = B(e); inf{t, B(e)} = inf{t, B(x–1y)} = inf{t, B(y–1x)}. 
Hence by earlier results xt o B = yt o B. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.9: Let A, B, G be as in Theorem 2.2.6 Let xt, yt ⊆ A. Then the following 
conditions are equivalent. 
 
i. xt o B = yt o B. 
ii. (y–1x)t o B = et o B. 
iii. (x–1y)t o B = et o B. 
 
Proof: By earlier results we have xt o B = yt o B iff inf{t, B(e)}= inf{t, B(y–1x)} and 
inf{t, B(e)} = inf {t, B(x–1y)}. The latter conditions are equivalent to (ii) and (iii). 
 
THEOREM 2.2.10: Let  A,  B,  G  and  P  be  as  in  Theorem  2.2.6.  Let x, y ∈ P and 
s, t ∈ [0, A(e)]. Suppose that B (e) = A(e); then  
 
i. xt o B = ys o B if and only if t = inf {s, B (y–1x)}, s = inf { t, B (x–1y)}. 
ii. xt o B = yt o B if and only if (y–1x)t ⊆ B. 
iii. xt o B = ys o B if and only if t = s ≤ B (x–1y) 
iv. xt o B = xs o B if and only if t = s. 
 
Proof:  
 
i. 
By earlier results  xt o B  =  ys o B  if and only if  t  =  inf {s, B(y–1x)} and 
s = inf{t, B(x–1y)}. 
ii. 
By (i),  xt o B  =  yt o B  if and only if and only if  t  =  inf {t, B (y–1x)},     
t  =  inf{t, B(x–1y)} if and only if B(y–1x) ≥ t, B(x–1y) ≥ t. 
iii. 
By (i) and the fact that  B((y–1x)  =  B(x–1y);  xt o B  =  ys o B if and only if 
t  = s < B(x–1y). 
iv. 
Follows immediately from (iii).

DEFINITION 2.2.11: Let G be a S-semigroup. P a proper subset of G which is a group.

THEOREM 2.2.21: Let  G  be  a  S-semigroup. H  and  K  be  subgroups  of  G  such

σµ is not a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G. (P ⊂ G is a proper subset which is a group). 
 
Proof: Let Ho = {(e, e)}, H1 = H × G and H2 = P × P. Let 1 ≥ t0 ≥ t ≥ t2 ≥ 0. Let µ be a 
fuzzy subset of P × P such that  
 
µ (Ho) = to 
µ (H1 \ H0) = t1 and 
µ (H2 \ H1) = t2. 
 
Then  µ  is  a  S-fuzzy subsemigroup  of  P  ×  P, since  µt  is  a  subgroup of  P × P for 
all t ∈ Im(µ). Now there exists h ∈ H, k ∈ K such that k ∉ H and h ∉ K then hk ∉ H 
and hk ∉ K. Hence (hk, x), (x, hk) ∉ H1 for all x ∈ G. 
 
Then σµ(hk) = t2. Now since (h, k) ∈ H1 \ H0, σµ(h) = t1 = σµ(k). Thus σµ(hk) = t2 < t1 
= min {σµ(h), σµ(k)}. Hence σµ is not a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G.

If A has the sup property and B is pure in A, then n Bt = (nB)t for all t ∈ (0, A(0)]. 
 
Proof: Exactly as in case of fuzzy group as A is a S-fuzzy semigroup related to P ⊆ G. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.39: Let B, C ∈ SF(A) such that B(0) = C(0) = A(0) Then we have the 
following results to be true. 
 
i. Suppose that A has the sup property. If B is S-pure in A, then for all n ∈ N, nB 
= B ∩  nA. 
ii. Suppose that A and B have sup property. If for all n ∈ N, nB = B ∩  nA, then 
B is S-pure in A. 
iii. Suppose  that  A∗  is S-torsion free,  then  B  is  S-pure in A if and only if for 
all n ∈ N, nB = B ∩  nA.  
iv. For C ⊆ B. If C is S-pure in B and B is S-pure in A, then C is pure in A. 
v. If B is S-divisible then B is S-pure in A. 
vi. Suppose  that  A  is  S-divisible. Then  B  is S-pure  in  A  if  and  only  if  B  is  
S-divisible. 
vii. Let B ∈ S F (A) be such that B(0) = A(0). If for all xt ⊆ A such that xt ⊄ B, 
there does not exist n ∈ N such that n (xt) ⊆ B, then B is S-pure in A. 
 
Proof: The proof is for the reader to prove using the definitions.

THEOREM 2.2.51: Let B and C ∈ SF(A). If A = B ⊕ C then B and C are S-pure in A. 
 
Proof: Suppose that  nyt = x t  where  n ∈ N,  yt  ⊆ A and x t ∈ B. Since A = B ⊕ C, 
At  =  Bt ⊕ Ct. Thus Bt ∩ n A t =  nBt. Now ny t = (ny) t. Hence  ny  =  x, y ∈ A t  and  
x ∈ Bt. Thus there exists b ∈ Bt such that nb = x. Hence nbt = xt.. Thus B is a S-fuzzy 
subsemigroup in A is S-pure in A. 
 
Thus from these results we chiefly observe that those fuzzy results true in case of just 
groups can be very easily studied in case of S-semigroups by using S-fuzzy 
subsemigroup. Thus it is important to note that except for Smarandache structure such 
study would be certainly impossible. Now we give some interesting results on S-fuzzy 
semigroups. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.52: Let G be a finite S-semigroup. Suppose that there exists a S-fuzzy 
subsemigroup µ of G (for some proper subset P of G with P a subgroup of G) 
satisfying the following conditions for x, y ∈ P ⊆ G. 
 
i. 
µ(x) = µ(y) implies 〈x〉 = 〈y〉. 
ii. 
µ(x) > µ(y) implies 〈x〉 ⊂ 〈y〉. 
 
Then G is a S-cyclic semigroup. 
 
Proof: For x ∈ P ⊂ G, P a subgroup of a S-semigroup G. 〈x〉 denotes the subgroup 
generated by x.

THEOREM 2.2.53: Let G be a S-cyclic semigroup having cyclic group P (P ⊂ G) of 
order pα where p is a prime. Then there exists a S-fuzzy subsemigroup µP of G 
satisfying  µP(x)  =  µP(y)  implies 〈 x 〉 = 〈 y〉 and µP (x) > µP (y)  ⇒  〈 x 〉  ⊂  〈 y〉 
where µP : P → [0, 1] and x, y ∈ P ⊂ G. 
 
Proof: Consider the chain of subgroups of P ⊂ G; (e) = Po ⊂ P1 ⊂ …⊂ Pn = P where 
Pi is the subgroup of P generated by an element of order pi, i = 0, 1, 2,…, n and e is 
the identity element of P. Define a fuzzy subset µP of P by  µP(e) = to and µP(x) = ti if 
x  ∈  Pi  \  Pi–1  for all i = 1, 2, …, n; where to, t1, …, tn are numbers lying in the 
interval [0, 1] such that to > t1 > … > tn.  It is a  routine  matter  to  confirm  that  µP  is 
a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G satisfying the desired conditions. 
 
The result which follows is a consequence of the above two theorems. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.54: Let G be a S-semigroup having a subgroup P of order pα. Then G 
is S-cyclic  if  and  only  if  there  exists  a  S-fuzzy subsemigroup µP  of  G  such  that  
for all x, y ∈ P, µ (x) = µ ( y) ⇒ 〈 x〉 = 〈 y〉 and µ(x) > µ(x) ⇒ 〈 x〉 ⊂ 〈 y〉. 
 
From now onwards till we explicitly specify L denotes a completely distributive 
lattice with minimum 0 and maximum 1. X a set, the elements of the direct power LX 
are called L-subsets of fuzzy subsets of X. f0 and f1 denotes the minimum and 
maximum respectively of Lx.

Smarandache normal fuzzy subgroups (S-normal fuzzy subgroups) of the S-semigroup 
G i.e. the L-subsets of P whose cuts are normal subgroups of P.

f (x–1 z x) ≥ f(z) for all x, z ∈ P. Several interesting results in this direction can be 
developed for L-subsets.

The range set of fuzzy subgroup we shall denote by Im µ. It is well known that for a

Example 2.2.3: Let Z12 = {0, 1, 2,…, 11}; Z12 is a semigroup under multiplication 
modulo 12. In fact  Z12  is  a  S-semigroup.  The  subgroups  of  Z12  are  P1  = {1, 11}, 
P2 = {3, 9} P3 = {1, 5} , P4 = {1, 7} and P5 = {4, 8}.

the subgroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.24: Let G be a S-semigroup. µ : G → [0, 1] restricted to some subset 
P of G be a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G. We see a S-fuzzy subsemigroup as in case of a 
fuzzy subgroup may or may not attain its infimum. We shall write inf µP for 
)

µ. Let µ and η be two S-fuzzy subsemigroups of the S-semigroup G related to the 
same subset P ⊂ G, P a subgroup of G. We say µ and η are Smarandache similarly 
bounded (S-similarly bounded) if they have the same S-tip and the same S-tale that is 
µP(e) = ηP(e) and inf µP = inf ηP. 
 
Now we define Smarandache equivalent of S-fuzzy subsemigroups. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.25: Let G be a S-semigroup. Two S-fuzzy subsemigroup η and µ of 
the S-semigroup G related to the same subgroup P, P ⊂ G are said to be 
Smarandache equivalent (S-equivalent) denoted by µ 
η
S≈
 if µ and η have the same 
chain  of  S-level  subgroups  in  P. Thus, we  have  contrary  of  groups  in  case  of 
S-semigroups several S-fuzzy subsemigroups and S-equivalences depending on the 
subgroups P ⊂ G. The S-relation 
S≈ can be proved to be a Smarandache equivalence 
relation (S-equivalence relation) on the set of S-fuzzy subsemigroups only related to 
the same subgroup P ⊂ G. Thus on G a S-semigroup we can have several S-relations 
which are S-equivalence relations depending on P, the proper subset of G which is a 
subgroup of G. Thus we can say the maximum number of S-relations will correspond 
to the number of proper subsets in G which are subgroups under the operations of G.  
 
All properties pertaining to fuzzy subgroups of a group can be extended to S-fuzzy 
subsemigroups of a S-semigroup G. 
 
We proceed on to define Smarandache penultimate subsemigroup of a S-semigroup 
G. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.26: Let µ be a fuzzy set in G then the penultimate subset P (µ ) of µ in 
G, defined by P(µ) = {x ∈ G µ(x) > inf µ}. In case µ is a fuzzy subgroup of a group 

 
225
G then P (µ) is a subgroup of G provided µ is a non-constant and P (µ) is called the 
penultimate subgroup of µ in G.  
 
For more about penultimate subgroups please refer [11]. Now on similar lines we 
define  Smarandache  penultimate  subsemigroups  of  a  S-fuzzy subsemigroup µ of a 
S-semigroup G. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.27: Let  G  be  a  S-semigroup. Let  µ  be  a  S-fuzzy subsemigroup  of 
the  S-semigroup  G  related  to  a  proper subset  E  of  G,  E  a subgroup  of  G.  
P(µE)  =  {x ∈ E µE(x) > Im µE}. 
 
In case µE  is  a  S-fuzzy subsemigroup of the S-semigroup G (E ⊂ G) then P(µE) is a 
S-fuzzy subsemigroup of E provided µE is non-constant and P(µE) is called the  
Smarandache  penultimate  subsemigroup  (S- penultimate subsemigroup)  of µE  in  E  
⊂  G. 
 
It is to be noted by S-penultimate subsemigroup of a S-fuzzy subsemigroup may or 
may not be a proper subgroup of the group E (E ⊂ G a subgroup of the S-semigroup 
G). 
 
THEOREM 2.2.56: Let µE be a non-constant S-fuzzy subsemigroup of the S-semigroup 
G. Then the S-penultimate subsemigroup P(µE) is a proper subgroup of E ⊂ G if and 
only if µE attains its infimum.  
 
Proof: µ : G → [0, 1] is a fuzzy set of G; E a proper subset of G which is a subgroup 
under the operation of G. µE denotes the restriction map i.e. µE : E → [0 1] such that 
µE is fuzzy subgroup of G or µ is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G related to E. Now the 
rest of the proof is as in case of fuzzy subgroups. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.57:  Let  η1 , η2  and µ  be  fuzzy sets  in  a S-semigroup G  such  that  
η1 ≤ η2. Then µ o η1 ≤ µ o η2 and µ∗η1 ≤ µ∗η2 and µ y η1 ≤ µ y η2 on some fixed 
subgroup E of G. 
 
Proof: First G is a S-semigroup. E a proper subset of G, E a subgroup under the 
operations of G. η1, η2 and µ are fuzzy sets on G satisfying the conidion η1 ≤ η2 
restricted to the subgroup E ⊂ G. 
  
Now using the proof of [7, 10] the result follows: 
 
THEOREM 2.2.58: Let η and µ be S-fuzzy subsemigroups of a S-semigroup of G. Then 
the set product η o µ contains η and µ if and only if η and µ have the same S-tip that 
is η(eP) = µ(eP) for a subgroup P in G. 
 
Proof: As in case of groups. (Hint: Using P ⊂ G and P is a subgroup µ and η 
restricted to P serves the purpose). 
 
THEOREM 2.2.59: Let  η  and  µ  be  S-fuzzy subsemigroups  with  the  same  S-tip  of 
a S-semigroup G relative P (P ⊂ G a subgroup of G). Then  the  set product η o µ is a 

 
226
S-fuzzy subsemigroup generated by the union of η and µ if ηoµ is a S-fuzzy 
subsemigroup of G. 
 
Proof: Direct hence left as an exercise for the reader. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.60: Let η and µ be S-fuzzy subsemigroups of a S-semigroup G. Then 
the S-penultimate product η o µ contains η and µ if η and µ are similarly bound with 
respect to the same subgroup P ⊂ G. 
 
Proof: Please refer [11] using the subgroup P of the S-semigroup G instead of using 
the whole of G. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.61: Let  η  and  µ  be  S-fuzzy subsemigroups in a S-semigroup G. 
Then η o µ = µ o η if either η or µ is a S-fuzzy ideal of the S-fuzzy subsemigroup. 
 
Proof: If the S-fuzzy subsemigroup µ related to P, P ⊂ G a subgroup of G then we see 
µ can also be fuzzy normal so that µ is a S-fuzzy ideal of the S-fuzzy subsemigroup. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.62: Let  η  and µ  be  S-fuzzy subsemigroups of  a  S-semigroup G. 
Then η y µ = µ  y η if either η or µ is S-fuzzy ideal of the S-fuzzy subsemigroup. 
 
Proof: Analogous to the proof in case of fuzzy subgroups as S-fuzzy subsemigroup µ 
of G is also a fuzzy subgroup of G.  
 
THEOREM 2.2.63: Let η and µ be S-fuzzy subsemigroups of a S-semigroup G. Then 
the set product µ oη is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup if and only if η o µ = µ o η. 
 
Proof: Straightforward, hence left for the reader to prove. 
 
THEOREM 2.2.64: Let η and µ be S-fuzzy subsemigroup of a S-semigroup G. Then the 
S-penultimate product η y µ is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup if and only if η y µ = µ y η. 
 
Proof: As in case of fuzzy subgroups by using the very definition.

THEOREM 2.2.67: Let η and µ be S-fuzzy ideals of the S-fuzzy subsemigroup of the        
S-semigroup G. Then the free product η ∗ µ is a S-fuzzy ideal. 
 
Proof: As η ∗ µ is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of the S-semigroup G relative to P we see      
η ∗ µ is a fuzzy subgroup of P. The rest of the proof follows as in case of groups. 
 
We just recall the notion of Smarandache normal subgroup of a S-semigroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.2.28: Let  S  be  a  S-semigroup. Let  A  be  a  proper  subset  of  S 
which  is  a  group  under  the  operations  of  S. We  say  A  is  a  Smarandache  
normal subgroup (S- normal subgroup) of  the  S-semigroup S if xA ⊂ A and Ax ⊆ A 
or xA = {0} and Ax = {0}  for  all  x ∈  S  and  if  0  is  an  element  in  S  then  we 
have  xA = {0} and Ax = {0} . 
 
Let S be a S-semigroup µ : S → [0, 1] be a S-fuzzy semigroup of S relative to P,         
P ⊂  S; P a subgroup under the operations of S. i.e. µP : P → [0, 1] is a fuzzy group 
i.e. µP is nothing but µ restricted to P. If in addition we have µ (gx) = µ (xg) for every 
g ∈ P and x ∈ S then we call µ a Smarandache fuzzy normal subgroup (S-fuzzy 
normal subgroup) of the S-semigroup S. 
 
 
2.3 Element-wise properties of S-fuzzy subsemigroups 
 
In this section we define several of the element-wise properties in S-fuzzy 
subsemigroups.  
 
DEFINITION 2.3.1: Let G be a S-semigroup. A fuzzy subset λ of G of the form  
 


For y in P ⊂ G, P a subgroup in G is called the Smarandache fuzzy point  (S-fuzzy 
point) with Smarandache support (S-support) x and value t is denoted by xt. 
 
It is very important to note that all elements in the S-semigroup G need not in general 
have S-fuzzy point. That is to be more precise only those elements in G which fall into 
one or more subgroups of G will have S-fuzzy point that too depending on the fuzzy 
subset λ of G. Thus  unlike  in  a  group  G  we  will  not  be  in  a  position  to 
associate S-fuzzy points. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.2: A fuzzy subset λ of a S-semigroup G is said to have Smarandache 
sup property (S-sup property) if for every non empty subset T of G where T is a 
subgroup and there exists a ∈ T such that λ(a) = sup {λ(t) t ∈ T}. 

 
230
 
DEFINITION 2.3.3: A fuzzy subset λ of a S-semigroup G is said to be an Smarandache 
(∈, ∈∨ q)  fuzzy  subsemigroup  (S-(∈, ∈∨ q) fuzzy subsemigroup)  of  G  if  for  any 
x, y ∈ P ⊂ G (P a subgroup of G) and t, r ∈ (0, 1]  
 
i. 
xt, yt ∈ λ ⇒ (xy)M (t ,r) ∈∨ qλ  and 
ii. 
xt ∈ λ ⇒ (x–1)t ∈∨ qλ. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.4: A S-fuzzy subsemigroup λ of G is said to be  
 
i. 
Smarandache (∈,∈) fuzzy normal (S-(∈,∈) fuzzy normal) if for all x, y ∈ P 
⊂ G and t ∈ (0,1], xt ∈ λ ⇒ (x–1y x)t ∈ λ. 
 
ii. 
Smarandache (∈,∈∨ q) fuzzy normal (S-(∈,∈∨ q) fuzzy normal) (or simply  
S-fuzzy normal )   if  for  any  x, y ∈ P ⊂ G and  t  ∈  (0, 1],  xt  ∈  λ  ⇒  
(y–1x y)t ∈∨ qλ.  
 
Several relations and results in this direction can be evolved using Smarandache fuzzy 
notions. 
 
 
THEOREM 2.3.1:  
 
i. 
A fuzzy subset λ of a S-semigroup G is an S- (∈,∈∨ q) fuzzy subsemigroup 
(respectively S- (∈,∈∨ q)fuzzy normal subgroup) related to a subgroup P 
of G if and only if the (∈∨ q)- level subset λ q  of P ⊂ G is a subgroup of P 
(normal subgroup of P) for all t ∈ (0, 1] where λq = { x ∈ Pλ (x) ≥ t or 
λ(x) + t > 1}. 
 
ii. 
Let f : G → H be  a S-semigroup homomorphism. Let λ and µ be S-fuzzy 
subsemigroups of G and H respectively. Then f (λ) and f –1 (µ) are S-fuzzy 
subsemigroups of f (P) and P respectively (P ⊂ G is a subgroup of G). 
 
Proof: Refer [25]. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.2: For  any  S-(∈, ∈∨ q)  fuzzy  subsemigroup λ of S-semigroup G the 
S-(∈∨ q)-level subgroup λq = P (P ⊂ G, P a subgroup of G) for all t ∈ I if and only if 
λ(x) ≥ 0.5 for all x ∈ P ⊂ G. 
 
Proof: Straightforward.

P ⊂ G whose S- (∈∨ q)-level subgroups are precisely the members of the chain. 
 
Proof: Let {ti ti ∈ (0, 0.5); i = 1, 2, …, r} be such that t1 > t2 >…>tr. Let λ : P → I

It is important to mention here that for a given S-semigroup G we can have several 
subgroups P, Q, R,…, X, Y, Z such that we have a chain of subgroups associated with 
each of the subgroups P, Q, R,…, X, Y, Z leading to many S-(∈,∈∨q) fuzzy subgroup 
P, Q, R,…, X, Y, Z of G. 
 
Thus only the Smarandache notion alone can give a nice spectrum of S-(∈,∈∨q) 
fuzzy subsemigroups relative to a S-semigroup G having several distinct proper 
subsets which are subgroups of G.

THEOREM 2.3.4: Let λ be a S-(∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy subgroup of G with Im µ = {t, r} where 
0 < t < r < 0.5. If λ = µ ∪ ν where µ, ν are S-(∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy subsemigroups of G then 
either µ ≤ ν, or ν ≤ µ.  
 
Proof:  Follows as in case of (∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy subgroup. We say S-(∈, ∈∨q)-fuzzy 
subgroup of a S-semigroup G is said to be proper if it is not constant in G.

THEOREM 2.3.5: Let G be a S-semigroup that has several proper subgroup. A proper 
S-(∈,∈) fuzzy subsemigroup λ of G such that card. Im λ ≥ 3 can be expressed as the 
union of two proper non-equivalence S-(∈,∈)- fuzzy subsemigroups of G. 
 
Proof: As in case of (∈,∈)-fuzzy subgroups. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.6: Let λ be a proper S-(∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy subgroup of G such that the 
cardinality of {λ(x) λ(x) < 0.5 } ≥ 2. Then λ can be expressed as the union of two 
proper non-(∈∨q)-equivalent  S-(∈,∈∨q) fuzzy  subgroups of G. 
 
Proof: Follows by the same arguments as in case of fuzzy subgroups. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.7: Let λ be an S-(∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy subsemigroup of the S-semigroup G 
such that λ(x) ≥ 0.5 for all x ∈ G. Then λ can be expressed as the union of two non-
(∈∨ q) equivalent S- (∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy subsemigroups if and only if P = H ∪ K, where 
H and K are proper S- (∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy subsemigroups of P (where P is a proper subset 
of G which is a subgroup of G). 
 
Proof: For proof please refer [23]. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.5: Let µ be a fuzzy subset of a S-semigroup G. An S- (∈,∈∨q) fuzzy 
subsemigroup ξ of G is said to be the S-(∈, ∈∨q)-fuzzy subsemigroup generated by µ 
in G if ξ ≥ µ and for any other S- (∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy subsemigroup η of G with η ≥ µ it 
must be η ≥ ξ .

DEFINITION 2.3.7: A S-subsemigroup H of a S-semigroup G is said to be 
Smarandache quasi normal (S-quasi normal) if for every S-subsemigroup K of H, we 
have X Y = Y X, X ⊂ H and Y ⊂ K are subgroups of X and Y respectively. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.12: The  S-homomorphic pre-image  of  a  S-quasi normal subgroup is 
S-quasi normal. 
 
Proof: Follows by very definitions. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.13: A maximal S-quasi normal subgroup of a S-semigroup G is 
normal. 
 
Proof: Left as an exercise for the reader. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.14: Let G be a finite S-semigroup and Q be a S-quasi normal subgroup 
T of G. If Q is core-free then every Sylow subgroup in T is S-quasi normal in T ⊂ Q. 
 
Proof: Follows by simple computations.

THEOREM 2.3.15: Let ξ be a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of the S-semigroup G with S-sup 
property. Then each level subset  µt,  for  t ∈  (0,  ξ(e))  is a S-quasi normal subgroup

THEOREM 2.3.16: Let ξ be a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G, G a S-semigroup. Then ξ  is 
S-fuzzy  quasi  normal  II  if and only if  ξ o η = η o ξ  (restricted  to  some  subgroup  
P ⊂ G relative to which ξ is defined) for any S-subsemigroup η of G. 
 
From now onwards G will denote a S-semigroup; P a subgroup of G (P ⊂ G) under 
the operations of G. Let eP be the identity element of P and λP an S-(∈,∈∨ q)- fuzzy 
subsemigroup of G. By a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G we shall mean a S-(∈,∈∨ q)- 
fuzzy subsemigroup of G. For a fuzzy subgroup λ of G there exists x and y ∈ G such 
that λ(x) ≥ 0.5 and λ(y) < 0.5. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.12: 
 

 
236
i. 
λ is said to be a S-(∈,∈)-fuzzy quasi normal if for any S-fuzzy 
subsemigroup µ of G and for all z ∈ P ⊂ G, t ∈ (0,1], zt ∈ (λ o µ) if and 
only if zt ∈ (µ o λ). 
 
ii. 
λ is said to be a S-(∈,∈∨q) fuzzy quasi normal if for any S-fuzzy 
subsemigroup µ of G and for all z ∈ P ⊂ G, t ∈ (0,1], zt ∈ (λ o µ) implies 
zt ∈∨q (µ  o λ) and zt ∈ (µ o λ) implies zt ∈∨q (λ  o µ). 
 
THEOREM 2.3.17: λ is S-(∈,∈) fuzzy quasi normal if and only if for any S-fuzzy 
subsemigroup µ of G and for all z ∈ P ⊂ G, (λ o µ) z = (µ o λ)z. 
 
Proof: By way of contradiction if (λ o µ)(z) ≠ (µ o λ)(z) for some z ∈ P ⊆ G and we 
arrive at a contradiction. 
 
Remark: Let  λ  be  an  S-(∈,∈)  fuzzy  subsemigroup  of  a  S-semigroup G. If λ is a 
S-(∈,∈)-fuzzy quasi normal then λ is a S-fuzzy quasi normal subsemigroup of type I 
or II. 
 
As an S-(∈,∈∨q) fuzzy subsemigroup is different from the S-fuzzy semigroup it 
follows that an S-(∈,∈) fuzzy quasi normal for an S-(∈,∈∨q) fuzzy subsemigroup is 
different from a S-fuzzy quasi normal subsemigroup of type I or II. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.18: λ is S-(∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy quasi normal if and only if for any S-fuzzy 
subsemigroup µ of G relative to a subgroup P ⊂ G and for all z ∈ P,  (µ  o λ)(z)  =  
(λ o µ)(z)  if  (λ o µ)(z) < 0.5 and (µ o λ)(z) ≥ 0.5 if (λ o µ)(z) ≥ 0.5. 
 
Proof: Let λ be S-(∈, ∈∨q) fuzzy quasi normal. Let z ∈ P ⊂ G. 
 
Case 1 (i): Let (λ o µ)(z) < 0.5. If  possible  let  (µ o λ) (z) ≠ (λ o µ) (z). Suppose (i) 
(µ o λ)(z) = t < (λ o µ) (z) = r. Then zr ∈ (λ o µ) but zr ∈∨q (µ o λ) a contradiction. 
 
Case 1 (ii) : Let r < t. Then there exists δ > 0 such that r + δ < t and r + δ + t ≤ 1. 
Now zr+δ ∈ (µ o λ) but zr+δ

THEOREM 2.3.19: If µ is a S-fuzzy quasi normal subsemigroup (type I or II) of P ⊂ G, 
then λ is an S- (∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy quasi normal subsemigroup of G. 
 
Proof: Straightforward as in case of subgroups. The following theorems are stated and 
the proof is left as an exercise for the reader. 
 

 
237
THEOREM 2.3.20: Let H be any non-empty subset of the S-semigroup G.H is a S-quasi 
normal  subgroup  of  G  if  and  only  if  χH  ( the  characteristic  function  of  H) is 
an  S-(∈,∈∨ q) fuzzy quasi normal subgroup of G.  
 
THEOREM 2.3.21: Let λ be a  S-(∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy quasi normal subsemigroup of G, G a 
S-semigroup with the sup property. Then  (∈∨ q)-level subset  λt = {x ∈ P | xt ∈∨ qλ} 
is a S-quasi normal subsemigroup G relative to P ⊂ G ( P  a  subgroup  of  G)  for  all 
t ∈ (0, 1]. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.22: Let λ be a fuzzy subset of the S-semigroup G with “sup-property” 
and for all t ∈ (0, 1], S-(∈∨ q)-level subset λt be a S-quasi normal subsemigroup of 
G. 
 
Proof: λ is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G relative to a proper subset P ⊂ G; P a 
subgroup of G. Let µ be any S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G and z ∈ P. Then zt ∈ (λ o µ) 
implies z ∈ (λ o µ)t implies z ∈ λt y µt implies z ∈ µt y λt. (Since λt is S-quasi normal) 
implies z ∈ (µ o λ)t implies zt ∈∨q (µ o λ). Similarly zt ∈(µ o λ) ⇒ zt ∈∨q (λ o µ). 
Therefore λ is S-(∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy quasi normal. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.23:  Let  G  be  a  S-semigroup   such  that  for  all  x,  y,  υ,  ν  ∈  G, 
xy y υν  = xυ y  yν. If λ µ are S-(∈, ∈∨q) fuzzy quasi normal subsemigroups of G 
then so also is λ o µ.. 
 
Proof: Refer [25] and obtain the proof analogous for S-semigroup G with suitable 
modifications. 
 
Remark: Let G be a S-semigroup such that for all x, y, υ,ν ∈ P (P a subgroup of G), 
xy y υν = xυ y yν and Q(P) is the set of all S-(∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy quasi-normal 
subsemigroup of P then (Q(P), y) is a commutative semigroup. 
 
Thus for a single S-semigroup G we have several commutative semigroups associated 
with them in fact depending on the number of nontrivial subsets P of G which are 
subgroups of G. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.24: Let   H   be   a  S-semigroup.  G  a  S-semigroup, suppose  f  is  a  
S-semigroup homomorphism from G onto H (i.e. f : P ⊂ G → L ⊂ H i.e. P and L are 
subgroups of G and H respectively and f is a onto group homomorphism i.e. 
equivalently f is a S-semigroup homomorphism); then  
 
i. f (λ o θ ) = f(λ) o f(θ ) where λ and θ  are S-fuzzy subsemigroups of P ⊂ G. 
ii. f (f –1(θ ) = θ  where θ  is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of f(P). 
 
Proof: Direct as in case of fuzzy subgroups. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.25: Let f : G → H (H a S-semigroup), be a S-semigroup 
homomorphism). If λ is a S-(∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy quasi normal subgroup of P ⊂ G then f (λ) 
is an S-(∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy quasi normal subgroup of f (P). (P ⊂  G  is a subgroup of the 
S-semigroup G).

Proof: As in case of (∈,∈∨q) fuzzy normal subgroups of G.

an  S-(∈,∈∨q)-fuzzy quasi normal subsemigroup of G.

i.

subsemigroup relative the subgroup P ⊂ G. 
 
ii. 
Follows directly by the very definition.

semigroup G. We for the sake of completeness recall some of the definitions. 
 
DEFINITION [14]: Let A be a fuzzy subset of a group G. Then A is called a fuzzy 
subgroup of G under a t-norm T (T-fuzzy subgroup) if and only if for all x, y ∈ G. 
 

 
242
i. 
A(xy) ≥ T (A(x), A(y)). 
ii. 
A(e) = 1 where e is the identity of G. 
iii. 
A(x) = A(x–1). 
 
A is called a Min-fuzzy subgroup if A satisfies conditions (i) and (iii) only by 
replacing T with Min Let A be a fuzzy subset of the group G. Then the subset               
{x ∈ G A(x) ≥  t }, t ∈ [0, 1]  of  G is called a t-level subset of G under A and 
denoted by At. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.22: Let A be a fuzzy subset of the S-semigroup G. Then A is called a 
Smarandache fuzzy subsemigroup of P ⊂ G under a t-norm T (S-T-fuzzy 
subsemigroup) if and only if for all x, y ∈ P ⊂ G. 
 
i. 
A (xy) ≥ T (A(x), A(y)). 
ii. 
A(e) = 1 where e is the identity element of P. 
iii. 
A(x) = A(x–1) for all x ∈ P. 
 
A is called a S-Min-fuzzy subsemigroup if A satisfies conditions (i) and (ii) only by 
replacing T by Min. 
 
Let A be a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of P ⊂ G is called a t-level subset of P ⊂ G under A 
and is denoted by S(At). 
 
THEOREM 2.3.40: Let A be a S-min fuzzy subsemigroup of the S-semigroup G. Then 
every S-t-level subset S(At) of P ⊂ G, t ∈ [0, A(e)] is a S-subsemigroup of G. 
 
Proof: As in case of subgroups. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.41: Let A be a S-fuzzy subset of S-subsemigroup G such that every        
t-level subset S(At) of P ⊂ G, t ∈ Im (A) and A(e)= 1. Then A is a S-Min-fuzzy 
subsemigroup of P ⊂ G. 
 
Proof: Similar to results in groups. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.42: Let f : G → H be a S-semigroup homomorphism and A be a S-T-
fuzzy subsemigroup of G. Then f(A) is a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup of H. 
 
Proof: As in case of groups, hence left for the reader to prove. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.23: Let A be a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup of a S-semigroup P ⊂ G, xA 
(Ax) be left (right) fuzzy coset of A in P ⊂ G such that  xA(g) =  A(x–1 g) A(xg)  = 
A(gx–1)), g ∈ P ⊂ G. Then  A  is  said  to  be  a  Smarandache  normal  fuzzy  
subsemigroup  (S-normal fuzzy subsemigroup)  of  G  if  and  only  if  xA = Ax for all 
x ∈ P ⊂ G and hence P/A = {x A x ∈ P} is a group (if A is normal) with the 
operation xAyA = xyA, x, y ∈ P ⊂ G. 
 
Notation: Let A be a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup of G, x ∈ P ⊂ G r, n, ∈ N, r > n, such 
that A(xr) = A(xn) = 1. Since r > n1 then there exists q1, q2 ∈ N such that r = q1 n1 + n2,

THEOREM 2.3.43:  Let  A  be  a  S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup  of  the  S-semigroup  G;      
x ∈ P ⊂ G, r, n1 ∈ N, r > n such that A(xr) = A (xn) = 1. Then there exists d ∈ N such 
that A(xd) = 1 where d = (r, n). 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.24: Let  A be  a  T - fuzzy subsemigroup  of  a  S-semigroup of G and 
x ∈ P ⊂ G. Then the least positive integer n satisfying the condition A(xn) = 1 is 
called the fuzzy order of x with respect to A and we use the notation A o (x) = n. If n 
does not exist we say x is of infinite fuzzy order with respect to A and write A o (x)=∞. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.44: Let A be a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup of a S-semigroup  of  P ⊂  G,    
x ∈ P ⊂ G. Then 
 
i. If A(xr) = 1 then A o (x) r 
ii. If A o (x) < ∞ then A o (x)O(x). 
 
(Here A o(x) = O(x) iff A1 = {e} such that x ∈ P ⊂ G and O(x) is the order x). 
 
Proof: As in case of T-fuzzy subgroups. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.45: Let  A  be  a  S-T-fuzzy subgroup  of  P  ⊂  G, G a S-semigroup and 
x ∈ P ⊂ G such that A o (x) < ∞. Then A o (x) = A o (x–1). 
 
Proof: Let A o (x)  =  n and A o (x –1)  =  m. Then  A((x –1)n)  =  A((xn)–1)  = A(x) = 1.  
From the above theorem we get m / n. On the other hand,   A(x m) = A((x m)–1) = 
A((x–1) m) = 1 and hence n / m. Therefore n = m. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.46: Let A be a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup an S-commutative semigroup 
G. Then the set of all elements in P⊂ G (P a proper subset of G which is an abelian 
group of G) whose fuzzy order with respect to A are finite is a subgroup of P. 
 
Proof: Direct as in case of T-fuzzy subgroup of an abelian group. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.25: Let A be a S-T fuzzy subsemigroup of an S abelian semigroup G. 
Then the subgroup of all elements of P in G whose fuzzy orders with respect to A are 
finite is called the torsion part of P with respect to  A and is denoted by AP.

x ∈ P, A o (x) < ∞, n ∈ N then A o (xn) = [A o (x)]  [(n, A o (x))]. 
 
Proof: As in case of crisp case.

THEOREM 2.3.51: If A is a fuzzy subset of a S-semigroup G and every At, t ∈ Im (A) is 
a S-primary subsemigroup of P ⊂ G then A is a S-primary Min fuzzy subsemigroup of 
P ⊂ G.

THEOREM 2.3.52: Every  S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup  of  a  S-primary semigroup is also 
S-primary. 
 
Proof: Direct from the earlier theorem and the fact if the semigroup G is S-primary 
then every proper subset P ⊂ G where P is a subgroup and is S-primary. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.53: Let A be a S-primary Min- fuzzy subsemigroup of the S-semigroup 
G. Then every t-level subset At, t ∈ Im (A), of G is S-primary if Aα = {e} where α  = 
A(e), e is the identity element of P ⊂ G relative to which A is defined . 
 
Proof: Let x ∈ P ⊂ G, A(x) = t, A o (x) = Pr, where p is prime r ∈ N. Since AA(e) = 
{e}, then O(x) = A o (x) = pr. Hence A t is S-primary. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.54: If A is a S-normal T-fuzzy subsemigroup of a S-primary semigroup 
G then P/ A is a S-primary semigroup (A is defined relative to P ⊂ G). 
 
Proof: As in case of T-fuzzy subgroup of a primary group G. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.55:   Let  f : G  →  H  be  a  S-semigroup homomorphism  and  A  be  
S-primary T-fuzzy sub semigroup of P ⊂ G  and  AA(e)  = {e}. Then f(A) is S-primary 
T-fuzzy subsemigroup of T ⊂ P. 
 
Proof: Left for the reader as an exercise. 
 
THEOREM 2.3.56: Let A be a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup of a S-cyclic primary 
semigroup G such that A1 = {e} for x, y ∈ P ⊂ G Then 
 
i. 
If Ao(x) = Ao(y) then A(x) = A(y). 
ii. 
If Ao(x) > Ao(y) then A (y) > A(x).

Proof: Since A1 = {e}. Therefore Ao(x) = O(x) and hence the proof follows by earlier 
results.
