DEFINITION 2.3.28: Let A be a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup of G. A is said to be 
Smarandache abelian (S-abelian)  if  for  all  a, b  ∈ P ⊂  G, A(a) > 0, A(b) > 0 
implies ab = ba. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.29: A a S-Min-fuzzy subsemigroup of P ⊂ G is called the 
Smarandache p-sylow fuzzy subgroup (S-p-Sylow fuzzy subgroup) of G if it has the 
following properties: 
 
i. Card (ImA) ≤ 2. 
ii. AA(e) is a sylow p-subgroup of P (P ⊂ G). 
 
THEOREM 2.3.58: If A is a S-p-sylow normal Min fuzzy subsemigroup of G then A is 
not a S-primary fuzzy subgroup of P ⊂ G with order as a power of p. 
 
Proof: A matter of routine as in case of subgroups. 
 
Now we proceed on to define a Smarandache p-component of a S-semigroup G. 
 
DEFINITION 2.3.30: If A is a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup of P ⊂ G then the set of all 
elements in P ⊂ G of a fuzzy order as a power of a prime p with respect to A is called 
Smarandache p-component (S-p-compotent) of P ⊂ G with respect to A and it is 
denoted by PP(A). 
 
THEOREM 2.3.59: Let A be a S-T-fuzzy subsemigroup of P ⊂ G, G a S-semigroup. 
Then TA is the sum of PP(A), p ∈ V where V is the set of all prime and it is a direct sum 
if A1 = {e}. 
 
Proof: Refer [17] and prove it as in case of S-semigroups.  
 
In the following section we define Smarandache fuzzy bisemigroup. 
 
 
2.4 Smarandache fuzzy bisemigroups 
 

 
247
In this section we introduce the concept of Smarandache fuzzy bisemigroups. The 
study of Smarandache bisemigroups and Smarandache bigroups is very recent [135]. 
Several interesting properties of these concepts are studied in this section. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.1: Let (G, y, ∗) be a non empty set such that  G = G1 ∪ G2 where G1 
and G2 are proper subsets of G, (G, y, ∗) is called a Smarandache bigroup                 
(S-bigroup) if the following conditions are true: 
 
i. 
(G, y) is a group. 
ii. 
(G,∗) is a S-semigroup. 
 
Now we have got several results about them which we just recall without proof. 
 
THEOREM [135]: Let G be a S-bigroup then G need not be a bigroup. 
 
Proof: Construct an example to prove. 
 
THEOREM [135]: If G = G1 ∪ G2 is a S-bigroup then G contains a bigroup. 
 
Proof: Left for the reader to refer [135]. 
 
DEFINITION [135]: Let G = G1 ∪ G2 be a S-bigroup, we say G is a Smarandache 
commutative bigroup (S-commutative bigroup) if G1 is a commutative group and 
every proper subset S of G2 which is a group is a commutative group. If both G1 and 
G2 happens to be commutative trivially G becomes a S-commutative bigroup. 
 
We say G is S-weakly commutative if the S-semigroup G2 has atleast one proper 
subset which is commutative.  
 
The following theorem is straight forward and hence left for the reader to prove. 
 
THEOREM [135]: Let G = G1 ∪ G2 be a S-commutative bigroup then G is a S-weakly 
commutative bigroup and not conversely. 
 
Proof: Left for the reader as an exercise. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.2: Let G = G1 ∪ G2 be a S-bigroup. We say G is a Smarandache 
cyclic bigroup (S-cyclic bigroup) if G1 is a cyclic group and G2 is a S-cyclic 
semigroup. We call G a S-weakly cyclic bigroup if every subgroups of G1 is cyclic and 
G2 is a S-weakly cyclic bigroup. 
 
The following theorem is straight forward hence left for the reader to prove. 
 
THEOREM 2.4.1: Let G = G1 ∪ G2 be a S-cyclic bigroup then G is a S-weakly cyclic 
bigroup.  
 
Now we proceed on to define Smarandache hyper bigroup. 
 

 
248
DEFINITION 2.4.3: Let G = G1 ∪ G2 be a S-bigroup. If A be a proper subset of G2 
which  is  the  largest  group  contained  in  G2  and  G1  has  no  subgroups,  then  we 
call P = G1 ∪ A the Smarandache hyper bigroup (S-hyper bigroup). We say G is 
Smarandache simple bigroup (S-simple bigroup) if G has no S-hyper bigroup. 
 
We define Smarandache double cosets in bigroups. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.4:  Let  G  =  G1  ∪  G2  be  a  S-bigroup.  Let  A  =  A1  ∪  A2   and  
B = B1 ∪  B2  be  any  S-sub-bigroups of G. We  define  the  Smarandache double 
coset  (S-double coset)  as AxB  =  {A1 x1 B1 ∪ A2 x2 B2   A1 x1 B1   provided  x  =  x1  
∈  G1  if  x ≠ x1 ∉ G1 then we just take A1B1 if x = x2 ∈ G2 then take A2x2B2  otherwise 
take A2B2  for every x ∈ G} : AxB = {A1 B1 ∪ A2 xB2  if x ∈ G2 }  or = {A1 x B1  ∪  A2 
B2  if x ∈ G1} or = {A1 x B1 ∪ A2 x B2  if  x ∈ G1 ∪ G2 }. 
 
We just recall the definition of Smarandache normal bigroup.  
 
DEFINITION 2.4.5: Let G = G1 ∪  G2  be a S-bigroup we call a S-sub-bigroup A  of  G 
to  be  a  Smarandache normal sub-bigroup  (S-normal sub-bigroup)  of  G  where  A  
= A1 ∪ A2 if xA1 ⊂ A1 and A1x ⊂ A1 if x ∈ G1 and xA2 ⊂ A2 and A2 x ⊂ A2 or xA2 = {0} 
or Ax2 = {0} if 0 is an element in G2 and x ∈ G2 for all x ∈ G1 ∪ G2. We say G is 
Smarandache pseudo simple bigroup (S-pseudo simple bigroup) if G has no S-normal 
sub-bigroup. 
 
Now we proceed on to define Smarandache maximal S-bigroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.6:  Let  G  = G1  ∪  G2  be  a  S-bigroup. We  say  a  proper  subset  
A = A1 ∪ A2 to be Smarandache maximal S-bigroup  (S-maximal S-bigroup)  of  G  if 
A1 is the largest normal subgroup in G1 and A2 is the largest proper subset of G2 
which is the subgroup of G2. 
 
Now we proceed on to define Smarandache fuzzy bigroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.7: Let  (G  =  G1 ∪ G2 , +, y)  be  a  S-bigroup. Then µ : G → [0, 1] 
is said to be a Smarandache fuzzy sub-bigroup (S-fuzzy sub-bigroup) of G if there 
exists two fuzzy subsets µ1 (of G1) and µ2 (of G2) such that 
 
i. (µ1 , +) is a fuzzy subgroup of (G1 ,+) 
ii. (µ2 , y ) is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of (G2 , y) and 
iii. µ = µ1 ∪ µ2. 
 
We illustrate this by an example.

Now we define the concept of Smarandache fuzzy sub-bigroup of a S-semigroup G. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.9: A fuzzy subset µ of a S-semigroup G is said to be a Smarandache 
fuzzy sub-bisemigroup (S-fuzzy sub-bisemigroup) of the S-semigroup G if there exists 
two S-fuzzy subsemigroups µ1 and µ2 of µ (µ1 ≠ µ and µ2 ≠ µ) such that µ = µ1 ∪ µ2. 
Here by the term S-fuzzy subsemigroup of µ we mean that λ is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup 
of the S-semigroup G and λ ⊆ µ (where µ is also a fuzzy subgroup of G).

THEOREM 2.4.6: Every S-fuzzy sub bisemigroup of a S-semigroup G is a S-fuzzy sub-
semigroup of the S-semigroup G and not conversely.  
 
THEOREM 2.4.7: Let µ be a fuzzy subset of a S-semigroup G with 3 ≤ o(Im µ) ≤ ∞ 
then µ is a S-fuzzy sub-bisemigroup of the S-semigroup G if and only if µ is a S-fuzzy 
sub-bisemigroup of the S-semigroup G. 
 
Proof: The µ once defined, it is relative to a particular fixed proper subset P of G 
where P is a subgroup under the operations of G. The rest of the result is identical 
with the fuzzy sub-bigroup. Almost all the definitions and properties derived in case 
of S-fuzzy semigroups can be easily defined and derived for S-fuzzy bigroups and    
S-fuzzy bisemigroups. We just give very few of the related results S-fuzzy bigroups 
and S-fuzzy bisubsemigroup notions in this section. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.10: Let (G, +,y) be a S-bigroup. G = G1 ∪ G2 where (G1, +) is a 
group and (G2, y) is a S-semigroup. A: G → [0, 1] where A = A1 ∪ A2 with A1 a 
divisible fuzzy subgroups of G1 and A2 is a divisible S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G2 (i.e. 
A2 : G2 →  [0, 1] is such that A2 restricted to the subgroup P2 of G2 is a fuzzy 
subgroup G2).  Then  we  call A = A1 ∪ A2: G → [0, 1] is a Smarandache divisible 
fuzzy subgroup (S-divisible fuzzy sub-bigroup) of G. 
 
Several interesting results in this direction can be obtained. 
 
Let G be a S-bigroup; (G = G1 ∪ G2) A = A1 ∪ A2: G → [0, 1] be a S-fuzzy sub-
bisemigroup of G; i.e., A1: G1 ⇒ [0, 1] is a fuzzy subgroup of G1 and A2 : G2 → [0, 1] 
is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G2 related to S-sub-bigroup P of G.

DEFINITION 2.4.11:  Let  (G = G1 ∪ G2,  +,  y) be a S-bigroup. Let A : G → [0, 1] be 
a S-fuzzy bisubsemigroup of G. Let B ∈ S (F(A)). Then B is said to be Smarandache 
pure (S-pure) in A if and only if for all fuzzy singletons xt ⊂ B with t > 0, for all n ∈ N 
for all yt ∈ A, n (yt) = xt implies that there exists bt ⊂ B such that n(bt) = xt. 
 
Next we define Smarandache (∈,∈∨q)- fuzzy sub-bigroup of a S-bigroup G. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.12:  Let  (G, +, y)  be  a  S-bigroup. A  fuzzy  subset  λ  =  λ1  ∪  λ2 : 
G = G1 ∪ G2  → [0, 1] is said to be a Smarandache (∈,∈∨ q) fuzzy sub-bigroup       
(S-(∈,∈∨ q) fuzzy sub-bigroup)  of  G  if 
 
i.For any x, y ∈ G1 and t, r ∈ (0,1] λ1 : G1 → [0, 1], (G1,+) a group; xt, yr 
∈ λ1 ⇒ (xy)M (t ,r) ∈∨qλ1 and  

 
253
ii.xt ∈ λ1 ⇒ (x–1)t ∈∨qλ1 
iii.λ2 : G2 → [0, 1], λ2 defined on P2 ⊂ G2 (P2 subgroup of the S-semigroup 
(G2, y)) is such that for any x, y ∈ P2 and t, r ∈ (0,1], xt, yr ∈ λ2 ⇒ 
(xy)M(t,r) ∈∨ qλ2 and 
iv.xt ∈ λ2 ⇒ (x–1)t ∈∨ qλ2. 
 
The conditions in the above definition are respectively equivalent to 
 
i. 
λ1 (xy) ≥ M (λ1 (x), λ1 (y), 0.5) for x, y ∈ G1 . 
ii. 
λ2 (xy) ≥ M (λ2 (x), λ2 (y), 0.5) for x, y ∈ P2 ⊂ G2 (P2 subgroup of G2). 
iii. 
λ1(x–1) ≥ M (λ1 (x), 0.5) for all x ∈ G1 .  
iv. 
λ2(x–1) ≥ M (λ2 (x), 0.5) for all x ∈ P2 ⊂ G2 . 
 
Further we have for any S-(∈,∈∨ q) - fuzzy sub-bisemigroup  λ  = λ1 ∪ λ2  of  G  = 
G1 ∪ G2 with λ1 (x) ≥ 0.5 for some x ∈ G1, λ1 (e1) ≥ 0.5, e is the identity element of G1 
and λ2 (x) ≥ 0.5 for some x ∈ P2 ⊂ G2, λ2 (e2) ≥ 0.5; e2 identity element of P2 relative 
to which λ2 is defined. 
 
Further if λ is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of G then λ is an S-(∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy 
subsemigroup. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.13: Let (G = G1 ∪ G2, +, y) be a S-bigroup. λ a S-fuzzy sub-
bisemigroup of G. λ = λ1 ∪ λ2 : G = G1 ∪ G2 → [0, 1] such that λ1 : G1 → [0, 1] is 
fuzzy subgroup and λ2 : G2 → [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup relative to P ⊂ G2; λ 
is said to be a

∈ λ1. For x, y ∈ P2 ⊂ G2 and t ∈ (0,1], xt ∈ λ2 ⇒ (y–1 xy)t ∈ λ2. 
 
ii. 
 S-(∈, ∈∨ q)-fuzzy normal (or simply fuzzy normal). 
 
a 
if for any x, y ∈ G1 and t ∈ (0,1],  xt ∈ λ1 ⇒ (y–1 xy)t ∈∨ qλ1.

THEOREM 2.4.9: λ is S-(∈,∈)-fuzzy quasi normal if and only if for any S-fuzzy sub-
bisemigroup µ of G and for all z ∈ G1 (or z ∈ P2 ⊂ G2), (λ o µ)(z) = (µ o λ)(z).

sub-bisemigroup µ of G and for all z ∈ G1 and for all z ∈ P2 ⊂ G2 (P2 a subgroup of

≥ 0.5.

G1 ∪ G2, then λ is an S-(∈,∈∨ q)-fuzzy quasi normal sub-bisemigroup of G. 
 
Proof: Using definitions and the fact that λ = λ1 ∪ λ2 and λ1 is fuzzy quasi normal 
subgroup of G1 and λ2 is a fuzzy quasi normal subgroup of P2 contained in G2.

DEFINITION 2.4.24: Let  A  be  a  fuzzy  subset  of  a S-bigroup G. Then A is called a 
S-fuzzy sub-bisemigroup of G  and a  t-norm T (T-fuzzy bigroup) if and only if for all 
x, y ∈ G.  
 
i. 
A1 (xy) ≥ T (A1(x), A1(x), A1(y)) x, y ∈ G1.  
ii. 
A1(e) = 1 where e is the identity of G1. 
iii. 
A1(x) = A1(x–1).  
iv. 
A2(xy) ≥ T (A2(x), A2(y)), x, y ∈ P2 ⊂ G2 (P2 a subgroup of G2 
relative to which A = A1 ∪ A2 is defined).  
v. 
A2(e) = 1 where e is the identity of P2 ⊂ G2.  
vi. 
A1(x) = A1(x–1) for x ∈ P2 ⊂ G2, A is called the Smarandache 
Min fuzzy sub-bisemigroup (S-Min fuzzy sub-bisemigroup) if A satisfies 
conditions (i) to (vi) only by replacing T with Min. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.25: Let A be a fuzzy subset of a S-bigroup G. Then the subset {x ∈ G| 
A(x) ≥ t} of G= G1 ∪ G2 is called the Smarandache t-level subset (S-t-level subset) of 

 
258
G under A denoted by At (i.e. A = A1 ∪ A2 and At = (A1)t ∪ (A2)t with A1(x1) ≥ t if                  
x1 ∈ G1  and A2(x2) ≥ t if x2 ∈ G2). 
 
THEOREM 2.4.19: Let A be a S-Min fuzzy sub-bisemigroup of the S-bigroup G. Then 
every t-level subset At of G, t ∈ [0, A(e)] = [0, A1 (e1) ∪ A2 (e2)] where e1 is the 
identity of the group G1 and e2 is the identity of the subgroup P2 in G2 relative to 
which A is defined is a S-sub-bigroup of G. 
 
Proof: Follows by the very definitions; hence left for the reader as an exercise to 
prove. 
 
THEOREM 2.4.20: Let A be a fuzzy subset of a S-bigroup G such that every t-level 
subset At of G, t ∈ Im (A) and A(e1) = 1 = A(e2) where e1 is the identity element of G1 
and e2 is  the  identity element of P2 in G2 relative to which A is defined. Then A is a 
S-Min fuzzy sub-bisemigroup of G. 
 
Proof: As in case of groups. Hence the reader is requested to obtain the proof. 
 
THEOREM 2.4.21: Let f : G → H be a S-bigroup homomorphism and A be a S-T-fuzzy 
sub-bisemigroup of G. Then f(A) is a S-T-fuzzy sub-bisemigroup of H.

DEFINITION 2.4.27:  Let  A  be  a S-T-fuzzy sub-bisemigroup  of  a  S-bigroup  G  and 
x ∈ G= G1 ∪ G2. Then the least positive integers n1, n2 which satisfies the condition

,  x1 ∈ G1 and x2 ∈ P2 ⊂ G2 (P2 subgroup relative to which  
A  =  A1  ∪  A2  is  defined) is  called  the  Smarandache fuzzy order  (S-fuzzy order) 
of x ∈ G1 ∪ G2 relative to A and use the notation A1 o (x1) = n1 and A2 o (x2) = n2.  
 
If n1  and  n2  does  not  exist  we  say  x1  and  x2  is  of  Smarandache infinite fuzzy 
order (S-infinite fuzzy order) with respect to A1  and  A2  and write A1  o (x1) = ∞ and 
A2 o (x2) =∞. From the above definition we see A1  o (x1) = O(x1) if and only if A1 = 
{e1} such that x1 ∈ G1 and O(x1) is the order of x1 and A2 o (x2) = O(x2) if and only if 
A2 = {e2} such that x2 ∈ P2 (P2 ⊂ G2 is a subgroup of G2) and O(x2) is the order of x2. 
 
Thus we see the S-fuzzy order is a complete generalization of fuzzy order which is the 
generalization of the usual order of the S-bigroup. But that is not true for all x1 ∈ G1 
and  x2  ∈  P2  ⊂  G2  as G1 (x1 ) = 1;  hence  G1   o (x1 )  =  1  which  is  clearly 
impossible. P2 (x2 )  =  1, hence P2 o (x2 ) = 1 which is once again impossible. 
 
Now we proceed on to define Smarandache torsion part of a S-bigroup G. 
 
DEFINITION 2.4.28: Let G = G1 ∪ G2 be a S-bigroup. A be a S-T-fuzzy sub-
bisemigroup  of  the  bigroup G. Then the  S-sub-bigroup  of  all  elements in A whose 
S-fuzzy orders with respect to A = A1 ∪ A2 are finite is called the Smarandache 
torsion part (S-torsion part) of G relative to A and is denoted by S(TA). 
 
Several interesting results in this direction can be developed. We propose some 
problems for the reader in section 2.5. 
 
Now we proceed on to define Smarandache order of the S-fuzzy sub-bisemigroup A.

We call a S-bigroup G = G1 ∪ G2 to be Smarandache primary (S-primary) if G1 is a 
primary group and every proper subset P2 of G2 which is a subgroup of G2 is a 
primary group. We say the S-bigroup G = G1 ∪ G2 to be Smarandache weakly 
primary (S-weakly primary) if G1 is primary and atleast a proper subset which is a 
proper subgroup in G2 is primary. 
 

 
260
THEOREM 2.4.23:  Let  G  be  a  S-bigroup.  If  G  is  a  S-primary bigroup then G is 
a S-weakly primary bigroup. 
 
Proof: Direct by the very definition.

2.5 Problems 
 
In this section we give fifty-four problems on S-fuzzy semigroups and S-fuzzy 
bisemigroups for the reader to solve.  
 
The problem section of each chapter happens to be an integral part of the book, for the 
solutions to these problems will throw a lot of light about these new Smarandache 
fuzzy structures.  
 
 
Problem 2.5.1: If in S(3) σ is a S-fuzzy symmetric semigroup what is o(Im(σ))? 
 
Problem 2.5.2: Give an example of a S-fuzzy symmetric semigroup in S(5). 
 
Problem 2.5.3: Obtain some interesting results about S-fuzzy symmetric group S(n). 
 
Problem 2.5.4: If g is a S-co-fuzzy symmetric subsemigroup of the symmetric 
semigroup S(3); find o(Im g). 
 
Problem 2.5.5: If g is a S-co-fuzzy symmetric subsemigroup of S(n) then find 
O(Im(g)). 

 
261
 
Problem 2.5.6: Give an example to show that every S-fuzzy symmetric subsemigroup 
of a symmetric subsemigroup S(n) need not in general be a S-co-fuzzy symmetric 
subsemigroup of S(n). 
 
Problem 2.5.7: Obtain some interesting results about S-fuzzy normal subsemigroup 
of the S-semigroup G. 
 
Problem 2.5.8: Find conditions so that (A/B, y) is a S-semigroup; or Is (A/B, y) 
always a S-semigroup? Justify your answer. 
 
Problem 2.5.9: Find conditions so that (A/B, y) is a group? Is this possible? If so 
illustrate by an example. 
 
Problem 2.5.10: Give an example of a S-simple semigroup of finite order. 
 
Problem 2.5.11: Obtain some interesting results about SF (A). 
 
Problem 2.5.12: Characterize those S-semigroups G which are S-torsion fuzzy 
subsemigroup. 
 
Problem 2.5.13: Give an example of a S-p-primary fuzzy subsemigroup of a              
S-semigroup G. 
 
Problem 2.5.14: Characterize those S-semigroups G in which A is S-p-primary if and 
only if A* is S-p-primary. 
 
Problem 2.5.15: Can all S-semigroups have S-maximal p-primary fuzzy 
subsemigroup? 
 
Problem 2.5.16: Give an example of a S-divisible fuzzy semigroup and characterize 
those S-semigroups G which are S-divisible fuzzy semigroups. 
 
Problem 2.5.17: Characterize those S-semigroups G which are S-pure. 
 
Problem 2.5.18: Does there exist any relation between S-pure and S-divisible             
S-semigroups G? 
 
Problem 2.5.19: Can all S-semigroups which has a S-fuzzy subsemigroup which is a 
S-fuzzy weak direct sum a fuzzy weak direct sum?  
 
Problem 2.5.20: Give an example of a S-semigroup G which has S-normal fuzzy 
subgroups. 
 
Problem 2.5.21: Illustrate by an example S-tips in a S-semigroup. 

 
262
 
Problem 2.5.22: Characterize those S-semigroups which has S-multi-tiped fuzzy 
semigroups. 
 
Problem 2.5.23: Do all S-semigroups have S-penultimate subsemigroups? 
 
Problem 2.5.24: Characterize those fuzzy sets and the S-semigroup G in which the 
concept of S-fuzzy semigroup and S-fuzzy ideal coincide.  
 
Problem 2.5.25: Characterize those fuzzy subset λ of S-semigroup G which are         
S-(∈, ∈∨q) fuzzy subsemigroup. 
 
Problem 2.5.26: Give an example of a S-semigroup G which has S-(∈, ∈∨q) fuzzy 
subsemigroup. 
 
Problem 2.5.27: Illustrate by an example a S-(∈, ∈) fuzzy normal subsemigroup of a 
S-semigroup G. 
 
Problem 2.5.28: Characterize those λ : G → [0,1] which are S-(∈, ∈∨q) fuzzy 
normal. 
 
Problem 2.5.29: Give an example of a S-fuzzy quasi normal subsemigroups I and II. 
 
Problem 2.5.30: Characterize those S-core S-fuzzy subsemigroup. 
 
Problem 2.5.31: Illustrate by examples S-q-fuzzy maximal subsemigroups of a         
S-semigroup G. 
 
Problem 2.5.32: If λ is a S-q-fuzzy maximal subsemigroup of a S-semigroup G. Then 
prove 
λ
G and λ0.5 are maximal subgroups of G. 
 
Problem 2.5.33: Prove S-(∈, ∈∨q)-fuzzy normality implies S-(∈, ∈∨q)-fuzzy quasi 
normality in a S-bigroup G = G1 ∪ G2. 
 
Problem 2.5.34: Prove

Chapter Three 
 
SMARANDACHE FUZZY GROUPOIDS AND 
THEIR GENERALIZATION 
 
This chapter mainly introduces the notions of Smarandache fuzzy groupoid and 
Smarandache fuzzy bigroups. Several interesting results in this direction are given. 
The chapter has four sections. In section one we give some results on Smarandache 
fuzzy groupoids and around 20 definitions related to Smarandache fuzzy groupoids 
are given. Groupoids are the generalizations of loops; so, as all loops are groupoids, 
the Smarandache fuzzy loop study has become inevitable. In section two we define 
Smarandache fuzzy loop and study these notions. The third section gives in 26 
definitions the concepts about Smarandache fuzzy bigroupoids and Smarandache 
fuzzy biloops. In order to make this book appealing to researchers, in the final section 
we give 111 problems, which would make a reader master the Smarandache fuzzy 
groupoid and its generalizations. 
 
 
3.1   Some results on Smarandache Fuzzy groupoids 
 
In this section we for the first time define the notion of Smarandache fuzzy groupoids 
and illustrate it with examples. Fuzzy groupoids are studied extensively by several 
researchers like [11, 45, 47, 89, 90, 110].   
 
DEFINITION 3.1.1: A Smarandache groupoid (S-groupoid) G is a groupoid which has 
a proper subset S, S ⊂ G such that S under the operations of G is a semigroup.  
 
Example 3.1.1: G = {0, 1, 2, …, 5} be a groupoid given by the following table:

For several properties about S-groupoids please refer [128]. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.2: Let G be a S-groupoid. A fuzzy subset µ from G to [0, 1] is said to 
be a Smarandache fuzzy groupoid (S-fuzzy groupoid) G if µ restricted to at least one 
of the proper subsets P ⊂ G, P a semigroup under the operations of G we have µ (xy) 
≥ min {µ(x), µ(y)} for x, y ∈ P, we denote this by µP . If for every proper subset Pi of 
the S-groupoid G. Pi  a semigroup,  the fuzzy subset µ : G → [0, 1] is a such that

is a fuzzy semigroup, then we call µ : G → [0, 1] a Smarandache strong fuzzy 
groupoid (S-strong fuzzy groupoid) of G. 
 
THEOREM  3.1.1:  Let  G  be a S-groupoid. Every S-strong fuzzy groupoid µ of G is a 
S-fuzzy groupoid µ of G. 
 
Proof: Follows by the very definitions. 
 
The definition of S-fuzzy groupoids using S-groupoids will be called as the type I or 
level I S-fuzzy groupoids, by default of notation we just denote it by S-fuzzy 
groupoids. 
 
We define level II or type II S-fuzzy groupoids in the following. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.3: Let µ : X → [0, 1] be a fuzzy subgroupoid of the groupoid X with 
respect to a t-norm T. We  say  µ  is  a  Smarandache  fuzzy  subgroupoid  of  type  II 
(S-fuzzy subgroupoid of type II) of X if and only if X is a S-groupoid. 
 
THEOREM 3.1.2: Every S-fuzzy subgroupoid of type II (S-subgroupoid of type II) of X 
is a fuzzy subgroup of X. 
 
Proof: Direct by the very definition. 
 
THEOREM 3.1.3:  Let  X  be  a  groupoid.  Every  fuzzy  subgroupoid  X  need  not  be  
a S-fuzzy subgroupoid of X. 
 
Proof: Follows from the fact if X is not a S-groupoid then certainly even if X is a 
fuzzy subgroupoid of X, X will not be a S-fuzzy subgroupoid of X. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.4: Let X1 and X2 be two S-groupoids. Let µ1 and µ2 be S-fuzzy 
subgroupoids of X1 and X2 respectively, with respect to a t-norm T. The S-fuzzy 
subgroupoids

given by a Smarandache pull back (S-pull back) of µ2 along φ . 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.5: Let (X, y) be a S-groupoid. Let G be the family of S-subgroupoids 
of X. G is called a Smarandache generating family (S-generating family) if for every 
element x ∈ S ⊂ X (S a semigroup of X) there exists P ∈ X such that x ∈ P. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.6: Let (X, y) be a S-groupoid and let Y be a fixed S-subgroupoid of X. 
Let (Ω, A, P) be a probability space and let (V, ~) be a S-groupoid of functions 
mapping Ω into X with ~ defined by point wise multiplication in range space. A 
further restriction in placed on V by assuming that for each f ∈ V; Xf : {ω ∈ Ω | f(ω) 
∈ Y} is an element of A . Then υ : V → [0, 1] defined by υ(f) = P (Xf) for each f ∈ V is 
a fuzzy subgroupoid of V with respect to TM. If P (Xf) is a S-fuzzy subgroupoid of V for 
each f ∈ V then we say the S-fuzzy subgroupoid obtained in this manner is called 
Smarandache function generated (S-function generated).

DEFINITION 3.1.12: Let X be a S-groupoid. A fuzzy subset f of X is called S-fuzzy left 
ideal of X if P o f ⊆  f and f is called the S-fuzzy right ideal of X if f o P ⊆  f, P a 
semigroup of X. If f is both a S-fuzzy left and S-fuzzy right ideal of X then f is called a 
S-fuzzy ideal i.e. f(xy) ≥ max {f(x), f(y)} for all x, y ∈ P ⊂ X. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.13: A fuzzy left ideal f is called Smarandache prime (S-prime) if for 
any two S-fuzzy ideals f1 and f2 , f1  o  f2  ⊆  f implies f1  ⊆  f  or  f2  ⊆  f. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.14: A S-fuzzy left ideal f is called Smarandache quasi prime (S-quasi 
prime) if for any two S-fuzzy left ideals f1 and f2, f1 o f2 ⊂ f implies f1 ⊂ f or f2 ⊂ f. f is

called Smarandache quasi semiprime (S-quasi semiprime) if for any S-fuzzy ideal g of 
X; g2 ⊂  f implies g ⊂  f. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.15: A fuzzy subset f of a S-groupoid X is called Smarandache fuzzy 
m-system (S-fuzzy m-system) if for any t, s ∈ [0, 1) and a, b ∈ P ⊂ X, f(a) ≥ t, f(b) > s 
imply that there exists an element x ∈ P such that f (a x b) > t ∨ s. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.16: A S-fuzzy left ideal f is called Smarandache weakly quasi-prime 
(S-weakly quasi-prime)  if  for any  two  S-fuzzy left ideals  f1  and  f2 such that f  ⊆  f1 , 
f  ⊆  f2  and  f1  o  f2  ⊆  f  then   f1  ⊆  f   or  f2  ⊆  f. 
 
Notation: Let f be a S-fuzzy left ideal of a S-groupoid X, we define two fuzzy subsets

DEFINITION 3.1.17: A  S-groupoid  X  is  called  Smarandache  strongly  semisimple  
(S-strongly semisimple)  if  left  ideal  of  X  is idempotent.  A  S-fuzzy  ideal  f  of  a  
S-groupoid X is called idempotent if f = f o f that is f = f 2. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.18: Let X be a S-groupoid. We call X a Smarandache fuzzy 
multiplicative groupoid (S-fuzzy multiplicative groupoid) if for any two S-fuzzy ideals 
µ and δ  of X satisfying µ ≤ δ there exists a S-fuzzy ideal λ of X such that µ = δ λ. 
 
(Recall for X a S-groupoid; λ a fuzzy subset of X is called a S-fuzzy ideal of X if 
λ(xy) ≥ max{λ(x), λ(y)} for all x, y ∈ P ⊂ X (P a proper subset of X which is a 
semigroup). 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.19: Let I = [0, 1], IX will denote the set of all mappings λ: X → I, 
M(x, y) will denote the minimum of x and y. Let λ µ ∈ IX. Then λ µ: X → I is defined 
by λµ(x) = sup {M (λ(y), µ(z)) | x = yz} for all x ∈ P ⊂ X. A S-fuzzy ideal λ of X is 
called a proper S-fuzzy ideal if λ = χX. Also for any two –S-fuzzy ideals λ, µ , λ < µ 
will mean λ ≤ µ,. λ ≠ µ. 
 
A S-fuzzy ideal λ of the S-groupoid X is called S-prime if for any two S-fuzzy ideals 
µ, δ of X µδ ≤ λ ⇒ µ ≤ λ or δ ≤ λ. 
 
For a S-fuzzy ideal λ of a S-groupoid X, the Smarandache fuzzy radical (S-fuzzy 
radical) of λ denoted by S(rad λ) is defined by  
 
S(rad λ(x)) = sup { λ (xn) n ∈ N} for all x ∈P ⊂ X ;  
 
λ defined relative to P in X. Clearly S(rad λ(x)) is a S-fuzzy ideal of X.  
 
DEFINITION 3.1.20: A S-fuzzy ideal µ of a S-groupoid X is said to be idempotent if µ 
= µ2. A S-fuzzy ideal of form xrχP where xr is a fuzzy point of the S-groupoid X is 

 
269
called a Smarandache principal fuzzy ideal (S-principal fuzzy ideal) of X. (P ⊂ X; P is 
a semigroup). 
 
Several other interesting results in this direction can be obtained. We define 
Smarandache compatible fuzzy relation on a S-groupoid X. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.21: Let X be a S-groupoid. Let P1, P2, ... , Pr be r (r >1) proper subset 
of X which are semigroups under the operations of X. Let λ be a fuzzy relation on the 
groupoid X. We say λ is a Smarandache compatible (S-compatible) on X, if λ(ac, bd) 
≥  min{λ(a, b), λ(c, d)} for all a, b, c, d in each Pi ⊂ X, i.e. for i = 1, 2, …, r. 
 
A Smarandache compatible fuzzy equivalence relation (S-compatible fuzzy 
equivalence relation) on a S-groupoid X is a Smarandache congruence                        
(S-congruence) on X. If λ is compatible on atleast one of the Pi’s then we say λ is 
Smarandache weakly compatible (S-weakly compatible). 
 
Any interested reader can obtain several exciting relations in this direction. Let λ be a 
fuzzy relation on a set X and let 0 ≤ α ≤ 1; λ is α-reflexive on X if λ (a, a) = α and 
λ(a, b) ≤ α for a, b ∈ X. An α-reflexive, symmetric and transitive fuzzy relation on X 
is a fuzzy α-equivalence relation on X. We call a compatible fuzzy α-equivalence 
relation on a groupoid, an α-equivalence. A fuzzy α-equivalence relation is a fuzzy 
equivalence if  α  = 1 and every fuzzy α-equivalence relation is a G-equivalence. A 
G-reflexive and transitive fuzzy relation on X is a G-preorder on X. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.22: Let X and Y be two nonempty set. A mapping f : X × X → Y × Y is 
called a semibalanced mapping if  
 
i. 
Given a ∈ X there exists a u ∈ Y such that f (a, a) = (u, u)  
ii. 
f(a, a) = (u, u) and f (b, b) = (v, v) where a, b ∈ X,  u, v ∈ Y 
implies f (a, b) =(u, v). 
 
Several important properties on semibalanced mapping can be derived. 
 
If f is a semibalanced map from X × X into Y × Y and µ is an α-equivalence fuzzy 
relation on Y then f–1(µ) is an α-equivalence fuzzy relation on X. 
 
DEFINITION 3.1.23: Let  f  be a map from X × X into Y × Y. A fuzzy relation λ on X is 
f-invariant if f(a, b) = f(a1 , b1 ) implies that λ(a, b) = λ(a1 , b1 ). A fuzzy relation λ on 
X is weakly f-invariant if f(a, b) = f (a1 , b) implies λ(a, b) = λ(a1 , b). 
 
We have several important properties about f-invariant fuzzy relation λ. A mapping is 
a balanced mapping if  
 
i. 
f (a, b) = (u, u) ⇒ a = b. 
ii. 
f (a, b) = (u, v) ⇒ f (b, a) = (v, u). 
iii. 
f (a, a) = (u, u) and f(b, b) = (v, v) ⇒ f(a, b) = (u, v)  
 
for all a, b ∈ X and u, v ∈ Y.  

 
270
 
A mapping f : X × X → Y × Y is a balanced mapping if and only if it is one to one 
semibalanced mapping. Let µ be a fuzzy relation on Y and f be a map from X × X into 
Y × Y. We say µ is f-stable if f (a, b) = (u, u) where a ≠ b ∈ X and u ∈ Y implies that 
µ(f(a, b)) ≤ µ f (x, x)) for all x ∈ X. 
 
Several innovative results in this direction are derived by [45]. 
 
The application of Smarandache fuzzy groupoids to the study of fuzzy automaton will 
find its place in the seventh chapter of this book. 
 
 
3.2 Smarandache Fuzzy loops and its Properties  
 
In this section we introduce the concept of Smarandache fuzzy loops. Smarandache 
loops was introduced in [129] and the concept of fuzzy loops was introduced and 
studied in [118]. The notion of Smarandache fuzzy loops enjoys more properties than 
that of the fuzzy loops: 
 
Now we just recall the definition of Smarandache loop for the sake of completeness. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.1: A Smarandache loop (S-loop) is defined to be a loop L such that a 
proper subset A of L is a subgroup (with respect to the same induced operation) that 
is φ ≠ A ⊂ L. 
 
Example 3.2.1: Let L = {e, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5} be a loop given by the following table.

It is easily verified L is a S-loop. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.2: Let L be a loop. If L has no subloops but only subgroups then we 
call L a Smarandache subgroup loop (S-subgroup loop). 
 
Example 3.2.2: Let L = {e, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5} be the loop given in example 3.2.1. All 
subloops in L are subgroups. So L is a S-subgroup loop. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.3: Let L be a loop. A proper subset A of L is said to be a 
Smarandache subloop (S-subloop) of L if A is a subloop of L and A itself is a S-loop. 
i.e. A contains a proper subset B such that B is a group under the operations of L. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.4: Let L be a loop. We say a non-empty subset A of L is a 
Smarandache normal subloop (S- normal subloop) of L if 

 
271
  
i. 
A is itself a normal subloop of L. 
ii. 
A contains a proper subset B where B is a subgroup under the 
operations of L.  
 
If L has no S-normal subloop we say the loop L is Smarandache simple (S-simple).  
 
Several results in this direction can be had from [129]. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.5: Let L be a S-loop. A fuzzy subset µ of L (µ : L → [0, 1]) is said to 
be a Smarandache fuzzy loop (S-fuzzy loop) if µ restricted to at least one of the proper 
subsets  of  P  of  L  where  R  is  a  group  under  the  operations  of  L  is  such  that 
µ(xy) ≥ min {µ(x), µ(y)} for every x, y ∈ P. µ(x –1) = µ(x) for every x ∈ P. i.e. we can 
denote this S-fuzzy loop by µP for µ is defined relative to P. If µ : L → [0, 1] is a fuzzy 
subset and µ is such that for every proper subset P of L which is a subgroup of L,      
µ : P → [0, 1] is a fuzzy group; then we call µ a Smarandache strong fuzzy subloop 
(S-strong fuzzy subloop) of L. 
 
THEOREM 3.2.1: Every S-strong fuzzy subloop of L is a S-fuzzy subloop of L. 
 
Proof: Left as an exercise for the reader as the proof is straightforward. 
 
This definition of S-fuzzy subloop and S-strong fuzzy subloop will be known as the 
level I or type I S-fuzzy subloops or S-strong fuzzy subloops. But by default of 
notation we do not mention it as type I or level I we just say S-fuzzy subloop.  
 
We give the results related with S-fuzzy subloops. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.6: Let L be a S-loop. A fuzzy subset µ of L is said to be a 
Smarandache fuzzy normal subloop (S-fuzzy normal subloop) if  
 
i. 
µ : L → [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy subloop relative to P. (P a subgroup in L 
relative to which µ is defined). 
ii. 
µ(xy) = µ(yx) for all x, y ∈ P.  
 
Let µ be a S-fuzzy normal subloop of the loop L.  
 
For t ∈ [0, 1] the set µ t = {(x, y) ∈  P × P | µ (xy–1) ≥ t} is called a Smarandache       
t-level relation (S-t-level relation) of µ. 
 
Now we define Smarandache fuzzy coset of a S-loop L. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.7: Let L be a S-loop µ be a S-fuzzy subloop of L. For any a ∈ P ⊂ L, 
P a subgroup of L, a µ defined by (aµ) (x) = µ (a–1x) for every x ∈ P ⊂ L is called the 
Smarandache fuzzy coset (S-fuzzy coset) of the S-loop L defined by a and µ. Thus if µ 
happens to be a S-strong fuzzy subloop of L then we see related to this µ we will have 
as many S-fuzzy cosets as the number of proper subsets of G which are subgroups of 
G. 
 

 
272
DEFINITION 3.2.8: Let λ and µ be two S-fuzzy subloops of a S-loop L. Then λ and µ 
are said to be Smarandache conjugate fuzzy subloops (S-conjugate fuzzy subloops) of 
L if for some p ∈ P.  (P subgroup in L relative to which λ and µ are defined) we have 
λ(x) = µ(p–1xp) for every x ∈ P ⊂ L. 
 
If λ and µ are S-conjugate fuzzy subloops of the S-loop L then o(λ) = o(µ ). 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.9: Let µ be a S-fuzzy subloop of a S-loop L then for any a, b ∈ P ⊆ L 
the Smarandache fuzzy middle coset (S-fuzzy middle coset) a µ b of the S-loop L is 
defined by (aµb) (x) = µ(a–1xb–1) for every x ∈ P ⊂ L. Further we see almost all 
results true in case of fuzzy groups can be extended in the case of S-fuzzy subloops of 
level I. 
 
Now we define Smarandache fuzzy relations on the S-loop L. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.10: Let Rλ and Rµ be any two Smarandache fuzzy relations (S-fuzzy 
relations) on a S-loop L. Then Rλ and Rµ are said to be Smarandache conjugate fuzzy 
relations (S-conjugate fuzzy relations) on the S-loop L if there exists (g1 , g2) ∈ P × P 
⊂ L × L (P a subgroup in L relative to which fuzzy relations Rµ and Rλ are defined)

DEFINITION 3.2.11: Let µ be a S-fuzzy subloop of a loop L. For a ∈ P (P ⊂ L a 
subgroup of L relative to which µ is defined). The Smarandache pseudo fuzzy coset 
(S-pseudo fuzzy coset) (aµ)p is defined by ((aµ)P)(x) = p(a)µ(x) for every x ∈ P ⊂ L 
and for some prime p. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.12: A S-fuzzy subloop µ of a S-loop L is said to be a Smarandache 
positive fuzzy subloop (S-positive fuzzy subloop) of the loop L if µ is a positive fuzzy 
subset of the S-loop L.  
 
Several interesting and innovative Smarandache analogue results can be established in 
this direction. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.13: A S-fuzzy subloop µ of a S-loop L is Smarandache normalized        
(S-normalized) if and only if µ(e) =1 where e is the identity element of the subgroup 
P,   P ⊂ L, P a subgroup of L relative to which µ is defined. 
 
DEFINITION [138]: A fuzzy topology τ on a group G is called a g-fuzzy topology. The 
pair (G, τ) is a g-fuzzy topological spaces.  
 
As loops are non-associative loops the concept of analogous l-topological spaces 
cannot be directly built using loops instead of groups. To overcome this problem the 
concept of S-loops becomes handy. To this end we next define Smarandache fuzzy 
topology on a S-loop L. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.14: A fuzzy topology τ on a S-loop L is called a Smarandache l-fuzzy 
topology (S-l-fuzzy topology) if τ is a g-fuzzy topology relative to atleast one proper 
subset P of L where P is a subgroup. We call τ a Smarandache strong l-fuzzy topology   

 
273
(S-strong l-fuzzy topology) if τ is a S-g fuzzy topology with respect to every proper 
subset P of L. 
 
THEOREM 3.2.2: Every S-strong l-fuzzy topology is a S-l fuzzy topology. 
 
Proof: Direct by the very definition hence left as an exercise for the reader. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.15: Let τ1 and τ2 be S-l-fuzzy topologies on the S-loops L1 and L2 
respectively. A function f: (L1, τ1) → (L2 τ2) is said to be a Smarandache l-fuzzy 
continuous map (S-l-fuzzy continuous map) from L1 to L2 if 
 
i. 
f from two proper subsets P1  and P2  of  L1  and L2  respectively  is  such  
that     f : (P1 , τ1 ) → (P2 , τ2 ) is a g-fuzzy continuous map from P1  to P2. 
ii. 
for every µ ∈ τ2 , f –1(µ) ∈ τ1  and  
iii. 
for every S-fuzzy subloop µ of L2 in τ2 , f –1(µ) is a S-fuzzy subloop of G1 in 
τ1 . 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.16: Let (L1 , τ1 ) and (L2 , τ2 ) be any two S-l-fuzzy topological spaces. 
A function f : (L1 , τ1 ) → (L2 , τ2 ) is said to be a Smarandache l-fuzzy homomorphism 
(S-l-fuzzy homomorphism) if it satisfies the following 3 conditions. 
 
i. 
f : (P1 , τ1 ) → (P2 , τ2 ) is one to one and onto. (Pi is a subgroup of Li 
relative to which τ1  is defined for i = 1, 2).  
ii. 
f is a S-l-fuzzy continuous map from P1 to P2 and 
iii. 
f –1 is S-l-fuzzy continuous map from P2 to P1 . 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.17: A fuzzy subset µ of a S-l-fuzzy topological space (L, τ) is called a 
Smarandache Ql -neighborhood (S-Ql -neighborhood) of the fuzzy point xt (for x ∈ P 
⊂ L, P a subgroup relative to which τ is defined) if and only if there exists S-fuzzy 
subloop λ (of L) in τ such that λ ⊆ µ, xt qλ (where xt qλ means t + λ (x) > 1 and xt is 
quasi coincident with λ). 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.18: A S-l-fuzzy topological space (L, τ) is said to be a S-l-fuzzy 
Hausdorff space if  and  only  if  for  any  two  fuzzy  points  xt  and  ys (x , y  ∈ P,  P a 
subset  of  L  which  is  a subgroup relative to which τ is defined x ≠ y)  there  exists  
S-Ql-neighborhoods λ and µ of xt and ys respectively such that λ ∩ µ = φ G. 
 
We have earlier defined the notion of fuzzy singletons; now we proceed on to define 
Smarandache p-primary fuzzy subloop of a S-loop L. 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.19: Let L be a S-loop. µ is called a Smarandache p-primary subloop 
(S-p-primary subloop) of a S-loop L ⇔ there exits a prime p such that for all fuzzy 
singleton xt ⊂ µ with t > 0 there exists n ∈ N (N-integers) such that pn(xt) = 0t . 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.20: Let L be a S-loop µ is called a Smarandache divisible fuzzy 
subloop (S-divisible fuzzy subloop) of L if and only if for all singletons xt ⊂ µ with        
t > 0, there exists n ∈ N there exists a fuzzy singleton yt ⊂ µ such that n(yt ) = xt .

DEFINITION 3.2.24: Two S-fuzzy subloops of a S-loop L are said to be Smarandache 
equivalent (S-equivalent) if they have the same family of level subloops otherwise they 
are said to be Smarandache non equivalent (S-non equivalent). 
 
DEFINITION 3.2.25: Let µ be a fuzzy subset of a S-loop L. an S-(∈ ,∈ ∨ q) fuzzy

and for any other S-(∈, ∈∨ q)-fuzzy subloop η of L with η ≥ µ it must be η ≥ ξ .

DEFINITION 3.2.28: A S-subloop H of a loop L is said to be Smarandache quasi

3.3  Smarandache fuzzy bigroups and Smarandache fuzzy biloops 
 
This section is fully devoted to the introduction of Smarandache fuzzy biloops and 
Smarandache fuzzy bigroupoid. In chapter I we have introduced the notion of both 
fuzzy biloops and fuzzy bigroupoids. 

 
276
 
Study of just biloops and bigroupoids is elaborately done is [135]. So here we define 
several of the properties of Smarandache biloops and Smarandache bigroupoids and 
proceed on to sketch their applications. 
 
DEFINITION [128]: Let (G, +, y) be a non empty set. We call G a Smarandache 
bigroupoid (S-bigroupoid) if G1 ∪ G2 where G1 and G2 are proper subsets of G 
satisfying the following conditions  
 
i. 
(G1 , +) is a S-groupoid. 
ii. 
(G2 , y) is a S-semigroup. 
 
Several interesting and important properties used about them can be had from [135]. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.1: Let  (G  = G1 ∪ G2 , +, y)  be  a  S-bigroupoid.  A  fuzzy  subset    
µ : G → [0, 1] is called the Smarandache fuzzy sub-bigroupoid (S-fuzzy sub-
bigroupoid) of G if µ = µ1 ∪ µ2 where µ1  : G1 → [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy subgroupoid of 
G1 (µ restricted to G1 denoted by µ1 ) µ2 : G2 →  [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup of 
G2 (µ restricted to G2 is denoted by µ2). Thus we say if µ : G → [0, 1] that is               
µ = µ1  ∪  µ2 : G1 ∪ G2 → [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy bigroupoid then µ1  : G1 → [0, 1] which 
is  a  S-fuzzy subgroupoid  is  defined  related  to  a  semigroup  P1  of  G1  and          
µ2  :  G2 → [0, 1] which is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup is defined related to a subgroup P2 
of G2 .  
 
Thus we can give yet another definition of the Smarandache fuzzy sub-bigroupoids 
which we choose to call it as Smarandache fuzzy sub-bigroupoids of type II. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.2: Let G be a bigroupoid. A fuzzy subset µ of G is said to be a 
Smarandache  fuzzy  sub-bigroupoid  II  (S-fuzzy sub-bigroupoid II)  of  G  if there 
existsη < µ such that η is a fuzzy sub-bisemigroup of G. 
 
THEOREM 3.3.1:  Every  S-fuzzy sub-bigroupoid µ of a bigroupoid G = G1 ∪ G2 is a 
S-fuzzy sub-bigroupoid of type II.  
 
Proof: Straightforward by the very definitions. 
 
It is left for the reader to verify whether a S-fuzzy sub-bigroupoid II, µ of G is a          
S-fuzzy sub-bigroupoid of G or will µ be a S-fuzzy sub-bigroupoid II of G will imply 
G is a S-bigroupoid. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.3: Let (T, +, y) be a S-biquasi group. i.e. (T, +) is a S-semigroup and 
(T, y) is a semigroup. A fuzzy subset µ: T → [0, 1] is said to be a Smarandache fuzzy 
sub-biquasi group (S-fuzzy sub-biquasi group) if µ : (T, +) → [0, 1] is a fuzzy 
subsemigroup and µ : (T, y) →[0, 1] is a fuzzy subsemigroup. (Recall the set (T, +, y) 
is a Smarandache biquasi group (S-biquasi group) if (T, +) is a S-semigroup and      
(T, y) is just a semigroup). 
 
We proceed on to define type II Smarandache sub-biquasi group. 
 

 
277
DEFINITION 3.3.4: Let (X, +, y) be a biquasi group. A fuzzy subset µ of X is said to be 
a Smarandache fuzzy sub-biquasi group of type II (S-fuzzy sub-biquasi group of type 
II) of X if µ : (X, +) → [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy subsemigroup and µ : (X, y) → [0, 1] is a 
fuzzy subsemigroup of X. 
 
Thus we have the following result, which is direct by the very definitions. 
 
THEOREM 3.3.2: Let (T, +, y) be a S-biquasi group. Let µ: T → [0, 1] be a S-fuzzy 
sub-biquasi group then µ is a S-fuzzy sub-biquasi group of type II.  
 
But if µ : T → [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy sub-biquasi group of type II, will µ be a S-fuzzy 
sub-biquasi group?  
 
This problem is left for the reader as an exercise. 
 
The applications of S-fuzzy bigroupoids will be dealt in the chapter VII. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.5: Let X1 and X2 be two bigroupoids. Let µ1 and µ2 be S-fuzzy sub-
bigroupoids of X1 and X2 respectively with respect to a t-norm T. The S-fuzzy sub-
bigroupoids µ1 and µ2 are homomorphic if and only if there exists a S-groupoid 
homomorphism (isomorphism) φ : X1 → X2  such that µ1 = µ2 o φ. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.6:  Let  (X  =  X1  ∪  X2, +, y)  be  a  S-bigroupoid. A fuzzy subset λ = 
λ1 ∪ λ2 of X is called a Smarandache fuzzy bi-ideal (S-fuzzy bi-ideal) of X if 
 
λ1 (x, y) ≥ max {λ1 (x), λ1 (y)} for all x, y ∈ P1 ⊂ X1 ; P1 relative to which λ1 is defined 
and λ2 (x, y) ≥ max {λ2 (x), λ2 (y)} for all x, y ∈ P2 ⊂ X2; P2 relative to which λ2 is 
defined. 
 
A S-fuzzy bi-ideal µ = µ1  ∪  µ2  of  X  = X1  ∪  X2  is  called  prime  if  for  any  two 
S-fuzzy bi-ideals λ and δ of X, λδ ≤ µ ⇒ λ ≤ µ or δ ≤ µ. Let µ be a S-fuzzy bi-ideal of 
S. The Smarandache fuzzy biradical (S-fuzzy biradical) of µ denoted by S(birad µ) = 
{sup

A S-fuzzy bi-ideal  µ  =  µ1  ∪  µ2  of  X  =  X1 ∪ X2 is called Smarandache primary 
(S-primary)  if   for  any  two  S-fuzzy  bi-ideals  δ  , λ of X.  δ λ  ≤  µ  ⇒ δ  ≤  µ  or     
λ ≤ S(birad µ). A S-fuzzy bi-ideal µ = µ1 ∪ µ2 of X = X1 ∪ X2 is called Smarandache 
maximal (S-maximal) if there does not exist any proper S-fuzzy bi-ideal λ of X such 
that µ < λ . 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.7: Let X be a S-bigroupoid, X is called a Smarandache multiplication 
bigroupoid (S-multiplication bigroupoid) if for any two S-fuzzy bi-ideals µ and δ  of X 
satisfying µ ≤ δ there exists a S-fuzzy bi-ideal λ of X such that µ = δ λ . 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.8: A S-fuzzy bi-ideal of the form xr χX  where xr is a fuzzy point of X is 
called the Smarandache fuzzy bi-ideal (S-fuzzy bi-ideal) of X. A S-bigroupoid X is said

to  be  a  S-fuzzy principal bi-ideal bigroupoid  if  every  S-fuzzy  bi-ideal  of  X  is  a 
S-principal fuzzy bi-ideal .  
 
We proceed on to define Smarandache function generated in case of S-bigroupoids. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.9: Let (X, +, y) be a S-bigroupoid and let Y be a fixed S-bigroupoid 
of X. Let (Ω , A , P) be a probability space and let (F, ~) be a S-bigroupoid of 
functions mapping Ω into X with ~ defined by pointwise multiplication in the range 
space. A further restriction is placed on F by assuming that for each f ∈ F, Xf  = {ω ∈ 
Ω f(ω) ∈ Y} is an element of A . 
 
Then ν : F→ [0, 1] defined by ν (f) = P(Xf ) for each f in F is a S-fuzzy sub-
bigroupoid of F with respect to Tm. A S-fuzzy sub-bigroupoid obtained in this manner 
is called the Smarandache function generated (S-function generated). 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.10: Given a S-bigroupoid (X = X1 ∪ X2, +, y) a t-norm T and a set I, 
for each i ∈ I and let µI  be a S-fuzzy sub-bigroupoid of X with respect to T define.

For notations about fuzzy regular and inverse subsemigroups please refer [34] we will 
be adopting these notions and notations in S-bigroupoids as S-bigroupoids contain 
proper subset which is a bisemigroup. Using the concept of fuzzy relation ρ on a 
semigroup S given by [117]. We develop the concept of Smarandache bifuzzy 
relation.  
 
DEFINITION 3.3.15: Let X = X1 ∪ X2 be a S-bigroupoid. A Smarandache bifuzzy 
relation (S-bifuzzy relation) ρ = ρ1 ∪ ρ2 on a S-bigroupoid X is one for which ρ1  is a 
fuzzy relation on P1 ⊂ X1; where P1 is a semigroup and ρ2 is a fuzzy relation on P2 ⊂ 
X2 where P2 is a semigroup. A S-bifuzzy relation ρ = ρ1 ∪ ρ2 on a S-bigroupoid X = 
X1 ∪ X2 is said to be a Smarandache fuzzy C-bicongruence relation (S-fuzzy C-
bicongruence relation) on X if M(ρ1 (x1 , y1 ), ρ (z1 , ω1 )) ≤ ρ1 (x1 z1 , y1 ω1 ) for all x1 , 
y1 , z1 , ω1  ∈ P1  ⊂  X1 (P1 semigroup of X1 ) and M(ρ2 (x2 , y2 ), ρ2 (z2 , ω2 )) ≤ ρ2 (x2 , 
z2, y2 , ω2 ) for all x2 ,  z2 , y2  , ω2 ∈  P2  ⊂  X2 . 
 
A S-bifuzzy relation ρ = ρ1  ∪ ρ2  on a S-bigroupoid  X  =  X1  ∪  X2  is  said  to  be  a 
S-fuzzy left (resp. right) bicompatible if for all x1, y1, s1 ∈ X1 and x2, y2, s2 ∈ X2 we 
have ρ1 (s1 x1 , s1 y1 ) (resp. ρ1 (x1 s1 , y1 s1 )) ≥ ρ1 (x1 , y1 ) and ρ2 (s2 x2 , s2 y2 ) (resp. 
ρ2(x2 s2, y2 s2)) ≥ ρ2 (x2 , y2 ). 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.16: Let X = X1 ∪ X2 be a S-bigroupoid such that X is a S-regular 
bigroupoid (i., e. X1  has a proper subset R1 which is a regular semigroup and X2  has 
a proper subset R2 which is a regular semigroup). 
 
A  S-fuzzy  sub-bigroupoid  λ  of  X  is  called  a  S-fuzzy  regular sub-bigroupoid if 
for all x1 ∈ R1 ⊂ X1 and x2 ∈ R2 ⊂ X2 there exists x'1, x'2 ∈ Tx where Tx = {x'1  ∈ R1 ⊂ 
X1 | x1 x'1 x1 = x1} ∪ {x'2∈ R2 ⊂ X2 | x1 x'2x1 = x1} (for all x1 ∈ R1 and for all x2 ∈ R2) 
such that (x1, t) ∈a λ1 ⇒ (x'1, t) ∈a∨ q(a , b) λ1 and (x2, t) ∈a λ2 ⇒ (x'2, t) ∈a ∨ q(a , b) λ2 
for all t ∈ (a, c]. 
 
Several results in this direction can be had in an analogous way. We suggest some 
problems in section 3.4. We just define Smarandache fuzzy prime bi-ideal of a S-

 
280
bigroupoid X; Smarandache fuzzy quasi prime bi-ideal of a S-bigroupoid and 
Smarandache fuzzy weakly quasi prime bi-ideal of a S-bigroupoid X. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.17: Let X = X1 ∪ X2 be a S-bigroupoid, a S-fuzzy left bi-ideal f is 
called  Smarandache  prime  (S-prime)  if  for  any  two S-fuzzy  bi-ideals  f1  and  f2 , 
f1  o  f2 ⊂  f implies f1  ⊂  f  or  f2 ⊂  f. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.18: Let X = X1 ∪ X2 be a S-bigroupoid. A S-fuzzy left bi-ideal f of X is 
called Smarandache quasi prime (S-quasi prime) if for any two S-fuzzy left bi-ideals f1 
and f2 , f1 o f2  ⊆  f  implies f1  ⊆  f  or  f2  ⊂  f ;  f is called Smarandache quasi-semi 
prime (S-quasi semiprime) if for any S-fuzzy left bi-ideal g of X; g2 ⊆  f implies g ⊆  f. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.19: Let X be a S-bigroupoid. A S-fuzzy left bi-ideal f is called 
Smarandache weakly quasi prime (S-weakly quasi prime) if for any two S-fuzzy left 
bi-ideals f1  and  f2   such that f ⊆  f1, f ⊆  f2 and f1  o f2  ⊆  f then f1  ⊆  f or f2  ⊆  f. 
 
Several important results in this direction can be obtained by using [133, 128]. We 
have given some problems for any researcher to tackle; as the book is for researchers 
to work more on Smarandache notions we restrain ourselves by giving elaborate 
proofs. 
 
Now we proceed on to define Smarandache biloops. Several properties related to 
bigroups can be easily formulated and obtained we define certain concepts about these 
new structures like S-bitale, S-bitip and S-penultimate biloops and give some results 
about them. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.20:  Let  (L, +, y)  be  a  biloop. We  call  L  a  Smarandache  biloop 
(S-biloop) if L has a proper subset P which is a bigroup. 
 
Now we define Smarandache fuzzy sub-biloop of a biloop L. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.21: Let (L, +, y) be a S-biloop. We call µ : L → [0, 1] a 
Smarandache fuzzy sub-biloop (S-fuzzy sub-biloop) of L if µ related to the sub-
bigroup P in L, is a fuzzy bigroup i.e. for µP  : P → [0, 1]  is  a fuzzy sub-bigroup 
where µP denotes the restriction of µ to P. 
 
This S-fuzzy sub-biloop of L we call as type I but by default of notation we just call 
the biloop L as S-fuzzy sub-biloop of L. We now proceed on to give the Smarandache 
fuzzy sub-biloop of type II. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.22: Let L be a biloop. We call µ a Smarandache fuzzy sub-biloop of 
type II (S-fuzzy sub-biloop of type II) if the fuzzy subset µ of L is such that exists a 
fuzzy subset η of L with η < µ and η is a Smarandache fuzzy sub-bigroup of L. That is 
η on a fuzzy subset of L where η is a fuzzy sub-bigroup of L.  
 
We are requesting the reader to find relations between S-fuzzy sub-biloops of type I 
and type II. 
 

 
281
DEFINITION 3.3.23: Let (L = L1 ∪ L2 , +, y) be a S-biloop. Two S-fuzzy sub-biloops η 
and µ of the S-biloop L are said to be Smarandache equivalent (S-equivalent) denoted 
by µ ≈ η if both µ and η have the same S-chain of level subgroups. (If µ is a S-fuzzy 
sub-biloop of the S-biloop X; µt  for  each  t ∈ [0, µ (e)]  (e1  –  identity element of      
P1 ⊂ X1, P1 a subgroup of X1 relative to µ1 ; P2 is a subgroup of x2 where e2 is the 
identity  element  of  P2  ⊂  X2  relative  to  µ2  where µ = µ1  ∪  µ2 ), here  µ(e)  =     
µ1 (e1 ) ∪ µ1 (e2 ) is a S-sub-biloop of the given S- biloop L called the Smarandache 
level sub-biloop (S-level sub-biloop) of µ. The set of all S-level sub-biloop of a S-fuzzy 
sub-biloop forms a chain. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.24: Two S-fuzzy subloops η  and  µ of a S-biloop  L  are  said  to  be 
S-equivalent denoted by µ 
B≈η if µ and η have the same chain of S-level subloops. Let 
µ  be  a  fuzzy  subset  of  a  S-biloop  L. Then  the  Smarandache  penultimate            
(S-penultimate) subset SP (µ) of µ in L defined by 
 
SP (µ) = {x ∈ B ⊂ L µ (x) > inf µ} (B ⊂ L is a sub-bigroup in the biloop L). In case 
µ is a S-fuzzy sub-biloop of a S-biloop L then SP (µ) is a S-sub-biloop of L provided µ 
is non -constant and SP (µ) is called the S-penultimate sub-biloop of µ in L. 
 
Now we define yet another interesting property about S-biloop L. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.25: Let η and µ be S-fuzzy sub-biloops of a loop L. Then η is said to 
be obtained by a Smarandache shift (S- shift) of the range set Im µ if there exists a 
one to one order preserving map φ from Im µ onto Imη such that η = φ o µ where ‘o’ 
is the composition of mappings. 
 
DEFINITION 3.3.26:  Let  L  be  a  S-biloop, µ  be  a S-fuzzy sub-biloop of L. i.e. µ = 
µ1  ∪  µ2 : L1 ∪ L2 → [0, 1] is a map, that µI restricted to a subgroup Pi in Li is such

3.4 Problems 
 
This section is purely devoted to the problems. We have proposed 111 problems for 
the reader to solve. The problems are of different levels: some require examples/ 
counter examples, others are routine like theorems some of them are 
characterizations. Thus tackling of these problems will make the reader not only 
strong in Smarandache notions but will induce him to discover more in Smarandache 
notions.   
 
Problem 3.4.1: Let µ  be a  S-fuzzy subgroupoid of X with respect to Min. Is µ a S-
fuzzy subgroupoids of X with respect to any t norm T? Justify / illustrate your claim.  
 
Problem 3.4.2: Let (X, y) be a S-groupoid. Let µ : X → [0, 1] be a S-fuzzy 
subgroupoid of X with respect to a t norm T. Can µ be extended to a S-fuzzy 
subgroupoid µ' of X' with respect to the same t-norm T, where X' is the identity 
extension of X? Substantiate your answer. 
 
Problem 3.4.3:  Let  (Ω, A, P)  be  a  probability space with P a fuzzy subset of A, 
(A, ∩) is a groupoid and P is a fuzzy subgroupoid of A with respect to TM. Will P be a 
S-fuzzy subgroupoid of A? 
 
Problem 3.4.4: Let (Ω, A, P) be a probability space and τ be a non-empty set ℘(τ) 
denotes the power set of τ. Let φ: Ω → τ be a mapping. Suppose (X, y) is a S-
groupoid µ: X →[0, 1], µ(x) = P(φ–1 (ψ(x))) for all x ∈ X where ψ: X → ℘(τ), such 
that φ–1 (ψ(x)) ∈ A for all x ∈ X, ψ (xy) ⊇ ψ(x) ∩ ψ(y) for all x, y ∈ X. Prove µ is a 
S-fuzzy subgroupoid of X. 
 
Problem 3.4.5: Is every S-function generated S-fuzzy subgroupoid a S-subgroupoid 
generated? 
 
Problem 3.4.6: Can we say every S-subgroupoid, S-generated fuzzy subgroupoid is 
isomorphic to a S-function generated S-fuzzy groupoid? 
 
Problem 3.4.7: Will every S-fuzzy subgroupoid with respect to Min a S-subgroupoid 
generated? Justify.  

 
283
 
Problem 3.4.8: Let µ = ν o f be the S-preimage of ν under f, can µ be a S-fuzzy 
subgroupoid of X with respect to T? 
 
Problem 3.4.9:  Prove  a  S-fuzzy  C-equivalence relation  µ on a S-groupoid X is a 
S-fuzzy C-congruence relation if and only if it is both fuzzy left and right compatible. 
 
Problem 3.4.10: Prove a fuzzy subset λ of X is a S-fuzzy regular subgroupoid of X if 
and only if λt is a regular S subgroupoid of X for all t ∈ (a, k]. 
 
Problem 3.4.11: Prove a fuzzy subset λ of X, X a S-groupoid is a fuzzy normal 
subgroupoid of X if and only if λt is normal subgroupoid of S for all t ∈ (a, k]. 
 
Problem 3.4.12: Prove a S-fuzzy left ideal f of a S-groupoid X is prime if and only if 
for any two fuzzy points xr, yt ∈ P ⊂ X (r t > 0) xr o P o yt o S ⊂ f implies that xr ∈ f 
or yt ∈ f. 
 
Problem 3.4.13: Prove a left ideal L of X, X a S-groupoid is prime if and only if fL is 
a S-prime fuzzy left ideal of X. 
 
Problem 3.4.14: Prove a S-fuzzy left ideal f of a S-groupoid X is S-quasi prime if and 
only if for any two fuzzy points xr, yt ∈ P ⊂ X, (r t > 0), xr o P o yt ⊆ f implies xr ∈ f 
or yt ∈ f. (P is a semigroup contained in X relative to which f is defined). 
 
Problem 3.4.15: Prove a left ideal L of a S-groupoid X is S-quasi prime if and only fL 
is a S-quasi prime fuzzy left ideal of X. 
 
Problem 3.4.16: Let M be a subset of X. Prove M is a S-m-system of X if and only if 
fm is a S-fuzzy m-system. 
 
Problem 3.4.17: Let f be a S-fuzzy left ideal of a S-groupoid X, prove f is S-quasi 
prime if and only if 1 – f is a S-fuzzy m-system. 
 
Problem 3.4.18:  Prove  a  left ideal  L  is  S-weakly  quasi  prime  if and only if fL is 
S-weakly quasi prime. 
 
Problem 3.4.19: Prove if X is a S-commutative groupoid, f a S-fuzzy left ideal of X, 
then  
 
i. 
f is S-prime if and only if f is S-quasi prime.  
ii. 
f is S-prime if and only if f is S-weakly quasi prime. 
 
Problem 3.4.20:  Let  f  be  a  S-left  ideal  of  a  S-groupoid X. Then prove i(f) is the 
S-largest fuzzy ideal of X contained in f. 
 
Problem 3.4.21: Let X be a S-groupoid with identity e and f a S-prime fuzzy left 
ideal of S. If i(f) ≠ 0, is i(f) a S-quasi prime fuzzy ideal of S? 
 

 
284
Problem 3.4.22:  Prove  a  S-groupoid X is strongly semisimple if and only if every 
S-fuzzy left ideal of X is idempotent. 
 
Problem 3.4.23: Let X be a S-commutative groupoid. Is the S-fuzzy left ideals of X 
S-quasi prime if and only if they form a chain and X is strongly semisimple? 
 
Problem 3.4.24: Let X be a S-groupoid which is a S-fuzzy multiplication groupoid. If 
f  : X → T is an epimorphism then will T be a S-fuzzy multiplication S-groupoid ? 
 
Problem 3.4.25: Can we prove a S-fuzzy ideal λ of a S-groupoid X is S-prime if and 
only if for any two S-fuzzy points xr, ys , xrys ∈ λ ⇒ xr ∈ λ or ys ∈ λ? 
 
Problem 3.4.26: A S-fuzzy ideal λ of a S-groupoid X is S-primary if and only for any 
two fuzzy points xr, ys, xrys ∈ λ

Problem 3.4.27: Let λ be a S-fuzzy ideal of a S-groupoid X. Is λn also a S-fuzzy ideal 
of X for all n > 0 and λn ≥ λn+1? 
 
Problem 3.4.28: Can we say λ is S-prime then λ = S(rad λ)? 
 
Problem 3.4.29: For any S-fuzzy ideal λ of a S-groupoid X. Will S(rad λ ) = ∩{µµ 
is a S-prime fuzzy ideal of X such that µ ≥ λ}? 
 
Problem 3.4.30: If λ is a S-primary fuzzy ideal of a S-groupoid X then prove, S (rad 
λ) is a S-prime fuzzy ideal of X. 
 
Problem 3.4.31: Let f: X → T be an epimorphism of S-groupoids and λ, µ be S-fuzzy 
ideals  of  X  and  T  respectively.  Prove f –1 (µ) is a S-fuzzy ideal of X, and f(λ) is a 
S-fuzzy ideal of T. 
 
Problem 3.4.32: If S(rad λ) is S-prime then λ is S-primary – prove ! 
 
Problem 3.4.33:  Let  λ  be  S-prime.  Then  prove  for  all  positive  integer  m ,  λm 
is S-primary and its S-fuzzy radical is λ. 
 
Problem 3.4.34: Let λ be S-prime and λm ≠ λm+1 for all m > 0. Is λm S-prime? Justify. 
 
Problem 3.4.35: If λ is S-primary, prove that λ = µn for some positive integer n 
where µ = S (rad λ). 
  
Problem 3.4.36: If λ is a proper S-prime and µ is a S-fuzzy ideal of X, X-S-groupoid 
such that µ ≤ λn, µ ≤ λn+1 for some n > 0 then λn = µ; ytχX where yi ∉ λ - Prove. 
 
Problem 3.4.37: If γ is a non –idempotent S-prime fuzzy ideal of X then prove that 
there is at most one S-prime fuzzy ideal µ < γ such that there is no S-prime ideal 
between. γ and µ. 
 

 
285
Problem 3.4.38: If S-prime fuzzy ideal of a S-groupoid X are linearly ordered with 
respect to the inclusion relation ‘≤’ then every non-idempotent S-prime fuzzy ideal is 
S-principal – prove. 
 
Problem 3.4.39: Let τ be the unique S-maximal fuzzy ideal of X, X a S-groupoid 
having the sup property. Then for every S-fuzzy ideal µ of X either µ = τn for some n 
> 0 or µ ≤ τn, prove or disprove? 
 
Problem 3.4.40: Let X be a S-commutative groupoid with every proper subset, which 
is a semigroup, is a monoid. Prove every S-fuzzy principal ideal is S-fuzzy 
multiplication groupoid. 
 
Problem 3.4.41: If  µ  is a S-compatible fuzzy relation on a S-groupoid X and f is a 
S-groupoid homomorphism form X × X into Y × Y will f1 (µ) be a S-compatible 
relation on X? 
 
Problem 3.4.42: Let λ be a S-compatible fuzzy relation on the S-groupoid X and f is 
a S-groupoid X and f is a S-groupoid homomorphism from X × X into Y × Y will f(λ) 
be a S-compatible fuzzy relation on Y? 
 
Problem 3.4.43: If λ is a G-pre order on the set X prove λ o λ = λ. 
 
Problem 3.4.44: If µ is an α -congruence fuzzy relation on the S-groupoid X and f is 
a S-groupoid homomorphism from D × D into T × T which is a semibalanced map  
then prove f –1(µ) is an α-congruence on D. 
 
Problem 3.4.45: Let f be a semibalanced map and a S-groupoid homomorphism from 
D × D onto S × S. Prove if λ is an α-congruence fuzzy relation on D which is weakly 
f-invariant then f(λ) is an α-congruence on S. 
 
Problem 3.4.46: If f is a S-groupoid homomorphism and a semibalanced map from   
D × D onto T × T and λ is a G-congruence fuzzy relation on D which is weakly         
f-invariant then prove or disprove f(λ) is a G-congruence on S with δ(f (λ)) = δ(λ). 
 
Problem 3.4.47: If f is a S-groupoid homomorphism and a balanced map from D × D 
into  T × T  and  λ  is  a  G-congruence  fuzzy  relation  on  D  then  prove  f (λ) is a 
G-congruence on S with δ (f (λ)) = δ(λ). 
 
Problem 3.4.48: Obtain some applications of S-fuzzy groupoids to S-fuzzy 
automatons.  
 
Problem 3.4.49: Let µ be a S-fuzzy normal subloop of a loop L and t ∈ [0, 1]. Prove 
µt is a congruence relation on the subgroup P in L relative to which µ is defined. 
 
Problem 3.4.50: Let µ be a S-fuzzy normal subloop of a S-loop L and x ∈ P ⊂ L. 
Then prove µ(xy) = µ(y) for every y ∈ P if and only if µ(x) = µ(e), e the identity 
element of the subgroup P relative to which µ is defined.  
 

 
286
Problem 3.4.51: Let L be a S-loop µ and λ be S-conjugate subloop of L relative to 
the subgroup P of L then prove o(λ) = o(µ). 
 
Problem 3.4.52: Let µ be a S-fuzzy subloop of a S-loop L then for any a ∈P ⊂ L 
prove the S-fuzzy middle coset a µ a–1 of the S-loop L is also a S-fuzzy subloop of L. 
 
Problem 3.4.53: Let µ be a S-fuzzy subloop a of S-loop L and a µ a–1 be a S-fuzzy 
middle coset of the S-loop L relative to the subloop P in L then o (a µ a–1) = o(µ) for 
any a ∈ P. 
 
Problem 3.4.54: Show if µ is a S-fuzzy subloop of a finite S-loop then prove in 
general o(µ) /  o(L). 
 
Problem 3.4.55: Let λ be a S-fuzzy subloop of a S-loop L relative to a proper subset 
P ⊂ L (P a subgroup in L). If λ and µ are conjugate fuzzy subsets of the S-loop L then 
prove µ is a S-fuzzy subloop of the S-loop L. 
 
Problem 3.4.56: Let Rλ and Rµ be any two fuzzy relations on a S-loop L. If Rλ and Rµ 
are generalized conjugate fuzzy relations on the subgroup P of L; then prove Rλ and 
Rµ are conjugate fuzzy relations on the S-loop L. 
 
Problem 3.4.57: Let µ be a S-fuzzy normal subloop of the S-loop L. Then prove for 
any g ∈ P we have µ (gxg–1) = µ (g–1xg) for every x ∈P ⊂ L (P a subgroup L relative 
to µ). 
 
Problem 3.4.58: Let λ and µ be S-conjugate fuzzy subloops of a S-loop L relative to 
the subgroup P in L. Prove λ × µ and µ × λ are S-conjugate fuzzy relations on L. 
 
Problem 3.4.59: Prove if µ is a S-positive fuzzy subloop of a S-loop L then any two 
S-pseudo fuzzy cosets of µ are either identical or disjoint. 
 
Problem 3.4.60: Let µ be a S-fuzzy subloop of a S-loop L then prove the S-pseudo 
fuzzy coset (aµ)p is a S-fuzzy subloop of the S-loop L for every a ∈ P, P ⊂ L is the 
subgroup relative to which µ is defined. 
 
Problem 3.4.61: Let µ be a S-fuzzy subloop of a S-loop L and Rµ: L × L → [0, 1] be 
given by Rµ (x, y) = µ (xy–1) for every x, y ∈P ⊂ L (P a subgroup of L relative to 
which µ is defined). If λ is a fuzzy subset of the S-loop L such that λ ⊆ µ then prove 
(a λ)p is a pre class of Rα for any a ∈ P ⊂ L. 
 
Problem 3.4.62: Let  µ  and  λ  be  any  two  S-fuzzy  subloops  of  a  S-loop L and 
Rµ ∩ λ  : L × L → [0, 1] given by Rλ ∩ µ (x, y) = (λ ∩ µ) (xy –1) for every x, y ∈ P  ⊂  L 
(P a subgroup of L relative to which µ and λ are defined). Prove a Rλ∩µ is a similarity 
relation on P only when both µ and λ are normalized. 
 
Problem 3.4.63: Let L1 and L2 be any two S-loops. If τ1 is a S-l-fuzzy topology on 
the S-loop L1 and τ2 is an indiscrete S-l-fuzzy topology on the S-loop G2, then prove 
every function is a S-l-fuzzy continuous map. 
 

 
287
Problem 3.4.64: Let τ1 and τ2 be two discrete S-l-fuzzy topology on the S-loops,      
(L1 , y) and (L2, ∗) respectively. Then prove every S-loop homomorphism from        
(L1, τ1)→ (L2, τ2) is a S-l-fuzzy continuous map but not conversely. 
 
Problem 3.4.65: Let τ1 and τ2 be any two S-l-fuzzy topologies on the S-loops (L1, y) 
and (L2, ∗) respectively. Then prove every S-loop homomorphism need not in general 
be a S-l-fuzzy continuous map. 
 
Problem 3.4.66: Let f  : (L1, τ1) → (L2, τ2) is a S-l-fuzzy homomorphism. Then prove 
(L1, τ1) is a S-l-fuzzy Hausdorff space if and only if (L2, τ2) is a S-l-fuzzy Hausdorff 
space. 
 
Problem 3.4.67:  Formulate  the  Existence  theorem  for  S-loops. That is  “Let µ be 
a S-fuzzy subloop of a S-loop L. The congruence class [x]µ of µt containing the 
element x of the subgroup P in the S-loop L (relative to which µ is defined) exists 
only when µ is a S-fuzzy normal subloop of the S-loop L”. Is this the existence 
theorem for S-loops? Justify. 
 
Problem 3.4.68: Prove µ is S-fuzzy divisible if and only if µt is S-fuzzy divisible. 
 
Problems 3.4.69: Prove for all x, y ∈ P⊂ L (P a subgroup of the S-loop L) and n ∈ N 
ny = x implies µ(x) = µ(y) for all S-divisible fuzzy subloops µ of L if and only if P is 
torsion free.

CHAPTER FOUR 
 
SMARANDACHE FUZZY RINGS AND  
NON-ASSOCIATIVE RINGS 
 
In this chapter we introduce the notion of Smarandache fuzzy rings and Smarandache-
fuzzy non-associative rings. This chapter has five sections. In the first section we 
introduce the concept of Smarandache fuzzy rings exhibited in 38 definitions and 
thirty theorems. In section two the notion of Smarandache fuzzy vector spaces are 
defined. Section three deals with Smarandache fuzzy non-associative rings. Section 
four deals with Smarandache fuzzy birings and several of its properties are defined 
and studied. In section five we have given 125 problems for the researcher. As this 
book is a research book we propose more problems. 
 
 
4.1  Smarandache fuzzy rings definitions and properties 
 
In this section we introduce the notion of Smarandache fuzzy rings. The study fuzzy 
rings [36, 37, 40, 109] is itself recent and still recent is the study of Smarandache 
rings [132]. So in this section for the first time we introduce the notion of 
Smarandache fuzzy rings. Here we give several examples and give some important 
properties about them. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.1: Let R be a S-ring. A fuzzy subset µ of R is said to be a 
Smarandache fuzzy ring (S-fuzzy ring) relative to a subset P of R where P is a field if 
µ: P → [0, 1] is such that  
 
µ (x – y) ≥ min {µ (x), µ(y)} 
µ (xy–1) ≥ min {µ (x), µ(y)} 
y ≠ 0 for all x, y ∈ P. 
 
This S-fuzzy ring will be called as type I; S-fuzzy ring. The condition of S-fuzzy ring I 
can be still tightened. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.2: Let R be a S-ring. Let µ: R → [0, 1] be a fuzzy subset of R. If µ is 
such that every proper subset Pi ⊂ R say i = 1, 2, …, n where Pi is a subfield of R; we 
have µ: Pi → [0, 1] is a fuzzy subfield of R for every Pi (i = 1, 2, …, n) then we call µ 
a Smarandache fuzzy strong ring of type I (S- fuzzy strong ring of type I). 
 
THEOREM 4.1.1: Every S-fuzzy strong ring of type I is a S-fuzzy ring of type I. 
 
Proof: Direct by definitions, the converse is not true. 
 
THEOREM 4.1.2: A S-fuzzy ring of type I need in general be a S-fuzzy strong ring of 
type I. 
 
Proof: We prove this by an example. Let Z6 = {0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5} be a S-ring. P = {0, 2, 
4} is a subset of Z6 which is a field.

We define Smarandache fuzzy ring of type II. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.3: Let R be any ring µ: R → [0, 1] be a fuzzy subset of R. We say µ is 
a Smarandache fuzzy subring of type II (S-fuzzy subring of type II) if there exist a 
fuzzy subset σ on R such that σ ⊂ µ and σ: R → [0, 1] is a fuzzy subfield of R. If for a 
given µ on a ring R no such σ exists such thatσ ⊂ µ we say µ is a Smarandache non-
fuzzy subring (S-non-fuzzy subring). 
 
Even if σ ⊂ µ is a fuzzy subring of R still we call µ only a S-non-fuzzy ring. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.4: Let R be a S-ring. A fuzzy subset µ of the ring R is called a 
Smarandache fuzzy ideal (S-fuzzy ideal) of R if the following conditions are true  
 
i. 
R has a proper subset X such that  
µ (x – y) ≥ min {µ (x), µ(y)} 
µ (xy) ≥ max {µ (x), µ(y)} for all x, y ∈ X. i.e. µ on x is a fuzzy ideal. 
 
ii. 
X contains a proper subset P such that P is a field under the operations of 
R and µ: P → [0, 1] is a fuzzy subfield. 
 
Example 4.1.1: Let Z12 = {0, 1, 2, …, 11} be a S-ring under multiplication and 
addition modulo 12.

DEFINITION 4.1.5: A S-fuzzy ideal µ of a ring R a Smarandache fuzzy prime (S-fuzzy 
prime) if σ and θ are S-fuzzy ideals of R the condition σ θ ⊆ µ implies either σ ⊂ µ or 
θ ⊂ µ. 
 
Now we proceed on to define Smarandache level subring. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.6: Let µ be a S-fuzzy subring of a S-ring R; t ∈ [0, 1] and t ≤ µ(0). 
The S-fuzzy subring (ideal) µt is called a Smarandache level subring (S-level subring) 
of µ. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.7: Let µ by any fuzzy subset of a ring R. The smallest S-fuzzy ideal of 
R containing µ is called the S-fuzzy ideal generated by µ and is denoted by 〈µ〉. 
 
THEOREM 4.1.3: Let µ be any fuzzy subset of a S-ring R. Then 〈χµ 〉 = χ〈 µ 〉.. 
 
Proof: Left as an exercise for the reader to prove.

defined by µ* (x) = sup{k | x ∈ 〈µk 〉 } is a S-fuzzy ideal generated by µ in R according 
as 〈µk 〉  is a S-fuzzy ideal generated by µk  in R. i.e. µ*(x) = t whenever x ∈ 〈 µt 〉  and 
x ∉ 〈 µs 〉  for all s > t. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.9: Let µ be any S-fuzzy ideal of a S-ring R. The fuzzy subset

DEFINITION 4.1.10:  A  fuzzy  subset  λ  of  a  S-ring R  is  said  to be an Smarandache 
(∈, ∈∨ q) fuzzy subring (S-(∈, ∈∨ q) fuzzy subring) of R if for all x, y ∈ P ⊂  R (P is a

THEOREM 4.1.4: A fuzzy subset λ of a S-ring R is an S-(∈, ∈∨ q) fuzzy subring of R if 
and only if λ(x – y), λ (xy) ≥ M (λ(x), λ(y), 0.5) for all x, y ∈ X ⊂ R, X a proper subset 
of R. which is a field. 
 
Proof: Direct by the definitions. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.11:  A  fuzzy  subset  λ  of  a  ring  R  is  said  to  be  a  Smarandache

ii. xt ∈ λ and y ∈ R ⇒ (xy)t , (yx)t ∈∨ qλ .

In view of these definition the reader is expected to prove. 
 
THEOREM 4.1.5: A fuzzy subset λ of a S-ring R is an (∈, ∈∨ q)-fuzzy ideal of a S-ring

(X a proper S-subring of R i.e. X contains a subfield as a proper subset).

THEOREM 4.1.6: A fuzzy subset λ of a S-ring R is a S-(∈, ∈∨ q) fuzzy subring (ideal) 
of R if and only if λt is a S-subring (S-ideal) of R for all t ∈ (0, 0.5]. 
 
THEOREM 4.1.7: A S-(∈, ∈∨ q) fuzzy ideal of R is S-(∈, ∈∨ q) fuzzy prime if and only 
if Max {λ (x), λ (y)} ≥   M (λ(xy), 0.5)  for  all  x , y ∈ X  ⊂  R. 
 
THEOREM 4.1.8: A S-fuzzy ideal λ of a S-ring R is a S-(∈,∈∨ q) fuzzy semiprime (or 
S-prime or S-semiprimary or S-primary) if and only if λt is S-semiprime (or S-prime 
or S-semiprimary or S-primary) for all 0 <  t  ≤  0.5.

DEFINITION 4.1.14:  The  subset  λ t =  {x ∈ X  |  λ(x)  ≥  t or λ (x) + t > 1} = {x ∈ X, 
xt ∈∨ qλ} is called (∈∨ q)-level subset of X determined by λ and t. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.15: A Smarandache L-fuzzy ring (S-L fuzzy ring) is a function              
µ : R → L, L a distribution lattice where (R, +, y) is a S-ring that satisfies, 
 
i. 
µ ≡/  0. 
ii. 
µ(x – y) ≥  µ(x)∧ µ(y) for every x, y ∈ P ⊂ R   
(P a subfield relative to which µ is defined). 
iii. 
µ (xy) ≥ µ (x) ∧ µ (y) for every x, y ∈ P ⊂ R. 
iv. 
If R is unitary then µ(1) = µ(0). 
 
S (µ) is a S-subring of R where µS denotes the restriction of µ to S(µ). Let µ : R → L 
and µ' : R' → L are S-L-fuzzy ring (R and R' S-ring). 
 
A  Smarandache  homomorphism  between  µ  and  µ'  is  a  S-ring  homomorphism    
f : R → R' that satisfies µ' (f(x)) = µ(x) for all x in P ⊂ R (P is a subfield relative to 
which µ is defined )i.e. f –1(µ') = µ .  
 
DEFINITION 4.1.16:  A  Smarandache  L-fuzzy  subring  ( S-L-fuzzy  subring )  of  a   
S-L-fuzzy ring; µ is a S-fuzzy ring µ' : R → L (R a S-ring) satisfying µ'(x) ≤ µ(x) for 
all x ∈ P ⊂ R (where µ is defined relative to the subfield P in R). 
 
For µ' a S-fuzzy subring of the S-L-fuzzy ring µ with L totally ordered. We shall call 
µ' the restriction of µ' to S(µ). It can be easily proved µ' |S  is a S-fuzzy subring of µS. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.17: Let µ: R → L be a S-L-fuzzy ring. A Smarandache L-fuzzy ideal 
(S-L-fuzzy ideal) of µ is a map δ : R → L such that the following properties hold. 
 
i. 
δ ≡/  0. 
ii. 
δ (xy) ≥ δ (x) ∧ δ (y) for every x, y ∈ P ⊂ R . 
iii. 
δ(xy) ≥ µ (x) ∧ δ (y) for every x, y ∈ P ⊂ R . 
iv. 
δ (x) ≤ µ (x)for every x∈ P ⊂ R . 
 
(P is the subfield relative to which the S-L fuzzy ring µ is defined). 

 
296
 
As in case of S-L-fuzzy subring, for δ a S-L-fuzzy ideal of the S-L-fuzzy ring µ with L 
totally ordered, δS the restriction of δ to S (µ). It holds that δS is a S-L-fuzzy ideal 
of µS where S(µ) is a S-subring of R, µS denotes the restriction of µ to S(µ).  
 
DEFINITION 4.1.18: Let µ be a fuzzy subset and t ∈ L. The t-level cut µt will be {x ∈ R 
| µ(x) ≥ t} and the Smarandache t-level cut (S-t-level cut) Sµt will be {x ∈ P ⊂ R | µ(x) 
≥  t; P  is  the  subfield of the S-ring R relative to which µ is defined} Analogously the 
t-level  strong  cut  will  be  {x ∈ R | µ (x) > t}  and Smarandache t-level strong cut 
(S-t-level strong cut) Sµt will be {x ∈ P ⊂ Rµ (x) > t – P defined above} and finally 
µ0 will stand for {x ∈ Rµ(x) = µ (0)} and Sµ0 = {x ∈ P ⊂ Rµ (x) = µ(0), ‘0’ the 
additive identity of P}. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.19: Let S be a δ-L-fuzzy ideal of µ. We define Smarandache fuzzy 
quotient ring  ( S-fuzzy  quotient  ring )   µ / s  δ  / s  as   µ / sδ / s : S (µ)α → L 
with µ / s δ /s (x + α) = µ (x) where α is the S-ideal of S(µ), S-maximal among those 
contained in Sδ 0 ∩ Sµ0. 
 
Now the notion of Smarandache prime L-fuzzy ideals is defined. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.20: A S-L-fuzzy ideal δ of a S-L-fuzzy ring µ is said to Smarandache 
prime (or Smarandache L-prime) if δ  ≡/   1 and it satisfies δ(xy) ∧ µ(x) ∧ µ(y) < δ(x) 
∨ δ(y) for every x, y ∈ P ⊂ R (P a subfield of R relative to which µ is defined). 
 
Clearly if µ ≡/  1 then we have δ (xy) ≤ δ (x) ∨ δ (y) for every x, y ∈ P ⊂ R. Further a 
S-L-fuzzy ideal δ of a S-L-fuzzy ring R is S-L-prime if for every x, y ∈ P ⊂ R, δ(xy) = 
δ(0) implies δ(x) = δ(0) or δ(y) = δ(0). We see if L is totally ordered then it is evident 
that δ(xy) ≤ δ(x) ∨ δ(y) implies δ (xy) = δ (o) implies δ (x) = δ (0) or δ (y) = δ (0).  
 
A Smarandache strongly prime L-fuzzy ideal (S-strongly prime L-fuzzy ideal) is 
defined as a non-constant S-fuzzy ideal δ of a S-ring µ that satisfies δ(xy) = δ(x) or 
δ(xy) = δ(y) for all x, y ∈ P ⊂ R. 
 
A Smarandache weakly completely fuzzy prime ideal (S-weakly completely fuzzy 
prime ideal) is defined when L = [0, 1] as a non constant S-fuzzy ideal δ of R that 
satisfies δ (xy) = max {δ (x) , δ (y)} for every x, y ∈ P ⊂ R. (P is a subfield relative to 
which δ  is defined). 
 
Note: This notion can be easily generalized to the case in which we have as arbitrary 
lattice L, changing maximum by supremum. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.21: A S-fuzzy ring µ is said to an Smarandache fuzzy integral domain 
(S-fuzzy integral domain) if x, y = 0 and µ(x) ∧ µ(y) > 0 implies x = 0 or y = 0, x, y ∈ 
P ⊂ R (P is the subfield of the S-ring R relative to which µ is defined). 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.22: Let δ be a S-L-fuzzy ideal of a S-L-fuzzy ring µ. The

DEFINITION 4.1.24: A S-fuzzy ideal λ of a S-ring R is called  
 
i. 
Smarandache semiprime (S-semiprime) if λ(x2) = λ(x) for all x ∈ P ⊂ R    
(P is a subfield of R relative to which λ is defined). 
 
ii. 
Smarandache prime (S-prime) if λ(xy) = λ(x) or λ(xy) = λ(y) for all x, y ∈ 
P ⊂ R . 
 
iii.

λ(xy) ≤ λ(xn ) for some positive integer n or λ(xy) ≤ λ(yn) for some positive 
integer n. 
 
iv. 
Smarandache primary (S-primary) if for all x, y ∈ P ⊂ R, λ(xy) = λ(x) or 
λ(yn) for some positive integer n.

v. 
Maximal if the level S-ideal { x ∈ P ⊂ Rλ (x) = 1} is maximal.  
 
DEFINITION 4.1.25: Let λ be a S-fuzzy ideal of a S-ring R. The fuzzy subset S rad λ of 
R defined by S(Rad λ) (x) = sup {λ (xn) | n > 0} is called Smarandache fuzzy radical 
(S-fuzzy radical) of λ . 
 
Several inter-relations between S-fuzzy prime, S-fuzzy primary, S-fuzzy semiprimary 
etc can be obtained as a matter of routine as in of fuzzy rings. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.26: A S-fuzzy ideal µ of a S-ring R is called Smarandache fuzzy 
irreducible (S-fuzzy irreducible) if it is not a finite intersection of two S-fuzzy ideals of 
R properly containing µ. If S-fuzzy ideal µ of a S-ring R is called Smarandache fuzzy 
weakly irreducible (S-fuzzy weakly irreducible) if  it  not  a  finite  intersection  of  a 
S-fuzzy ideal and a fuzzy ideal of R properly containing µ otherwise µ is termed as 
Smarandache fuzzy reducible (S-fuzzy reducible). 
 
THEOREM 4.1.15: If µ is any S-fuzzy prime ideal of a S-ring R, then µ is S-fuzzy 
irreducible. 
 
Proof: Straightforward as in case of fuzzy prime ideals.  
 
It is left for the reader as an exercise to prove the following theorem. 
 
THEOREM 4.1.16: Let µ be a S-fuzzy ideal of a S-ring R. If for some x, y ∈ P ⊂ R (P is 
the subfield relative to which µ is defined) µ(x) < µ (y) then µ (x– y) = µ(x) = µ(y – x). 
 
Just for the sake of completeness we recall the definition of coset of a fuzzy ideal and 
its Smarandache analogue. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.27: Let J: R → [0, 1] be a S-fuzzy ideal where R is a S-ring . The 
fuzzy subset x + J : R → L = [0, 1] defined by (x + J)y = J(y – x) is called a 
Smarandache coset of the S-fuzzy ideal (S-coset of the S-fuzzy ideal) J. 
 
THEOREM 4.1.17: If J : R → [0, 1] is a S-fuzzy ideal ,then x + J = y + J if and only if 
J(x – y) = J(0) . In that case J(x) = J(y). 
 
Proof: As in case of fuzzy ideal. Hence left for the reader to prove. 
 
THEOREM 4.1.18: Every  S-coset  of  a  fuzzy  ideal  J : R → [0, 1] is constant on 
every S-coset of a (non-fuzzy) ideal RJ of R. More specifically x + J (z) = J(y – x) for 
all z ∈ y + RJ. In particular x + J (z) = J (x) for all x ∈ RJ . 
 
Proof: As in case of fuzzy ideals the proof is straightforward. 
 
DEFINITION 4.1.28: The ring R/J of the S-cosets of the S-fuzzy ideal J is called the 
Smarandache factor ring (S-factor ring) or the Smarandache quotient ring (S-quotient 
ring) of R by J.

DEFINITION 4.1.34: Let A be a S-fuzzy ideal of S-ring R. A is called Smarandache
